'To find \\(\\tan(x+y)\\), we can use the identity for the tangent of a sum:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(x+y) = \\frac{\\tan x + \\tan y}{1 - \\tan x \\tan y}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that \\(\\tan x + \\tan y = 4\\) and \\(\\cot x + \\cot y = 5\\). We can express \\(\\cot x\\) and \\(\\cot y\\) in terms of \\(\\tan x\\) and \\(\\tan y\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cot x = \\frac{1}{\\tan x}, \\quad \\cot y = \\frac{1}{\\tan y}\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation \\(\\cot x + \\cot y = 5\\) becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\tan x} + \\frac{1}{\\tan y} = 5\n\\]\n\nCombining the two equations, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\tan x + \\tan y}{\\tan x \\tan y} = 5\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(\\tan x + \\tan y = 4\\) into the equation, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{\\tan x \\tan y} = 5\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\tan x \\tan y\\), we find:\n\n\\[\n\\tan x \\tan y = \\frac{4}{5}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute \\(\\tan x + \\tan y = 4\\) and \\(\\tan x \\tan y = \\frac{4}{5}\\) into the formula for \\(\\tan(x+y)\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan(x+y) = \\frac{4}{1 - \\frac{4}{5}}\n\\]\n\nSimplify the denominator:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\frac{4}{5} = \\frac{1}{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression for \\(\\tan(x+y)\\) becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(x+y) = \\frac{4}{\\frac{1}{5}} = 4 \\times 5 = 20\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\tan(x+y) = \\boxed{20}\\).'
'To find the smallest possible value of \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\|\\), we can use the triangle inequality for vectors, which states:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\| \\geq \\left| \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| - \\|\\mathbf{b}\\| \\right|\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = 3\\) and \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = 14\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\| \\geq \\left| 3 - 14 \\right| = 11\n\\]\n\nThe equality holds when \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) are in opposite directions. Therefore, the smallest possible value of \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\|\\) is \\(\\boxed{11}\\).'
'To find the area of the region inside the curve defined by the set of complex numbers \\( z \\) such that the real part of \\( \\frac{1}{z} \\) is equal to \\( \\frac{1}{6} \\), we start by expressing \\( z \\) in terms of its real and imaginary components. Let \\( z = x + yi \\), where \\( x \\) and \\( y \\) are real numbers and \\( i \\) is the imaginary unit.\n\nThe reciprocal of \\( z \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{z} = \\frac{1}{x + yi} = \\frac{x}{x^2 + y^2} - \\frac{y}{x^2 + y^2}i\n\\]\n\nThe real part of \\( \\frac{1}{z} \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{Re}\\left(\\frac{1}{z}\\right) = \\frac{x}{x^2 + y^2}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that this real part is equal to \\( \\frac{1}{6} \\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{x}{x^2 + y^2} = \\frac{1}{6}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n6x = x^2 + y^2\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we have:\n\n\\[\nx^2 - 6x + y^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nTo complete the square for the \\( x \\)-terms, we rewrite:\n\n\\[\nx^2 - 6x = (x - 3)^2 - 9\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back, we get:\n\n\\[\n(x - 3)^2 - 9 + y^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n(x - 3)^2 + y^2 = 9\n\\]\n\nThis is the equation of a circle centered at \\( (3, 0) \\) with radius 3. The area of this circle is given by the formula for the area of a circle, \\( \\pi r^2 \\), where \\( r \\) is the radius:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\pi \\times 3^2 = 9\\pi\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of the region inside the curve is \\(\\boxed{9\\pi}\\).'
"To find the radius of the sphere inscribed in the tetrahedron with vertices \\(A = (6,0,0)\\), \\(B = (0,4,0)\\), \\(C = (0,0,2)\\), and \\(D = (0,0,0)\\), we can use the formula for the radius of the inscribed sphere (inradius) of a tetrahedron. The formula for the inradius \\(r\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{3V}{A}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(V\\) is the volume of the tetrahedron and \\(A\\) is the total surface area of the tetrahedron.\n\n**Step 1: Calculate the volume \\(V\\) of the tetrahedron.**\n\nThe volume \\(V\\) of a tetrahedron with vertices \\((x_1, y_1, z_1)\\), \\((x_2, y_2, z_2)\\), \\((x_3, y_3, z_3)\\), and \\((x_4, y_4, z_4)\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\nx_1 & y_1 & z_1 & 1 \\\\\nx_2 & y_2 & z_2 & 1 \\\\\nx_3 & y_3 & z_3 & 1 \\\\\nx_4 & y_4 & z_4 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates of \\(A\\), \\(B\\), \\(C\\), and \\(D\\):\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n6 & 0 & 0 & 1 \\\\\n0 & 4 & 0 & 1 \\\\\n0 & 0 & 2 & 1 \\\\\n0 & 0 & 0 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nThe determinant simplifies to:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| 6 \\cdot (4 \\cdot 2 - 0 \\cdot 1) - 0 + 0 - 0 \\right| = \\frac{1}{6} \\times 48 = 8\n\\]\n\n**Step 2: Calculate the total surface area \\(A\\) of the tetrahedron.**\n\nThe surface area \\(A\\) is the sum of the areas of the four triangular faces. We calculate each face's area using the formula for the area of a triangle with vertices \\((x_1, y_1, z_1)\\), \\((x_2, y_2, z_2)\\), \\((x_3, y_3, z_3)\\):\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\nx_2-x_1 & y_2-y_1 & z_2-z_1 \\\\\nx_3-x_1 & y_3-y_1 & z_3-z_1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\n- **Face \\(ABC\\):**\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area}_{ABC} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n-6 & 4 & 0 \\\\\n-6 & 0 & 2\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 6 \\cdot 2 - 0 \\cdot 0 \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 12 = 6\n\\]\n\n- **Face \\(ABD\\):**\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area}_{ABD} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n-6 & 4 & 0 \\\\\n-6 & 0 & 0\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0 \\right| = 0\n\\]\n\n- **Face \\(ACD\\):**\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area}_{ACD} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n-6 & 0 & 2 \\\\\n0 & 0 & 2\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0 \\right| = 0\n\\]\n\n- **Face \\(BCD\\):**\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area}_{BCD} = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n0 & 4 & 0 \\\\\n0 & 0 & 2\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right| = \\frac{1}{2} \\left| 0 \\right| = 0\n\\]\n\nThe total surface area \\(A\\) is:\n\n\\[\nA = 6 + 0 + 0 + 0 = 6\n\\]\n\n**Step 3: Calculate the inradius \\(r\\).**\n\n\\[\nr = \\frac{3V}{A} = \\frac{3 \\times 8}{6} = 4\n\\]\n\nThus, the radius of the inscribed sphere is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
"To evaluate the determinant of the given \\(3 \\times 3\\) matrix, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} \nx & \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\\\ \n-\\sin \\theta & -x & 1 \\\\ \n\\cos \\theta & 1 & x \n\\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe can use the cofactor expansion along the first row:\n\n\\[\n= x \\begin{vmatrix} -x & 1 \\\\ 1 & x \\end{vmatrix} - \\sin \\theta \\begin{vmatrix} -\\sin \\theta & 1 \\\\ \\cos \\theta & x \\end{vmatrix} + \\cos \\theta \\begin{vmatrix} -\\sin \\theta & -x \\\\ \\cos \\theta & 1 \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nLet's evaluate each of these \\(2 \\times 2\\) determinants:\n\n1. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} -x & 1 \\\\ 1 & x \\end{vmatrix} = (-x)(x) - (1)(1) = -x^2 - 1\\)\n\n2. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} -\\sin \\theta & 1 \\\\ \\cos \\theta & x \\end{vmatrix} = (-\\sin \\theta)(x) - (1)(\\cos \\theta) = -x \\sin \\theta - \\cos \\theta\\)\n\n3. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} -\\sin \\theta & -x \\\\ \\cos \\theta & 1 \\end{vmatrix} = (-\\sin \\theta)(1) - (-x)(\\cos \\theta) = -\\sin \\theta + x \\cos \\theta\\)\n\nSubstituting these back into the cofactor expansion, we have:\n\n\\[\n= x(-x^2 - 1) - \\sin \\theta (-x \\sin \\theta - \\cos \\theta) + \\cos \\theta (-\\sin \\theta + x \\cos \\theta)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term:\n\n- The first term: \\(x(-x^2 - 1) = -x^3 - x\\)\n\n- The second term: \\(-\\sin \\theta (-x \\sin \\theta - \\cos \\theta) = x \\sin^2 \\theta + \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\)\n\n- The third term: \\(\\cos \\theta (-\\sin \\theta + x \\cos \\theta) = -\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta\\)\n\nCombining all terms:\n\n\\[\n-x^3 - x + x \\sin^2 \\theta + \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta - \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\(\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta - \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = 0\\), so they cancel out. We are left with:\n\n\\[\n-x^3 - x + x \\sin^2 \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we have:\n\n\\[\nx \\sin^2 \\theta + x \\cos^2 \\theta = x(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta) = x\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n-x^3 - x + x = -x^3\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the determinant is \\(\\boxed{-x^3}\\)."
'Let \\( x = \\arccos \\frac{1}{3} \\). This means that \\(\\cos x = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\nTo find \\(\\tan x\\), we need to find \\(\\sin x\\). Using the Pythagorean identity:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 x + \\cos^2 x = 1\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(\\cos x = \\frac{1}{3}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 x + \\left(\\frac{1}{3}\\right)^2 = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 x + \\frac{1}{9} = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 x = 1 - \\frac{1}{9} = \\frac{9}{9} - \\frac{1}{9} = \\frac{8}{9}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin x = \\sqrt{\\frac{8}{9}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{8}}{3} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}\n\\]\n\nNow, compute \\(\\tan x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{\\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{3}}{\\frac{1}{3}} = 2\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan \\left( \\arccos \\frac{1}{3} \\right) = \\boxed{2\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
"To find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\), we start by understanding the given information. We have \\(\\tan \\angle CAB = \\frac{22}{7}\\), and the altitude from \\(A\\) divides \\(\\overline{BC}\\) into segments of length \\(3\\) and \\(17\\). Let's denote the foot of the altitude from \\(A\\) to \\(\\overline{BC}\\) as \\(D\\). Thus, \\(BD = 3\\) and \\(DC = 17\\), making \\(BC = BD + DC = 20\\).\n\nThe tangent of angle \\(CAB\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\angle CAB = \\frac{\\text{opposite}}{\\text{adjacent}} = \\frac{h}{3}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(h\\) is the length of the altitude \\(AD\\). We are given \\(\\tan \\angle CAB = \\frac{22}{7}\\), so:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{h}{3} = \\frac{22}{7}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(h\\), we have:\n\n\\[\nh = 3 \\times \\frac{22}{7} = \\frac{66}{7}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) using the formula for the area of a triangle:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\text{base} \\times \\text{height}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 20 \\times \\frac{66}{7} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\frac{1320}{7} = \\frac{660}{7}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of triangle \\(ABC\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{660}{7}}\\)."
'To find \\(\\sin A\\), we need to use the relationship between the sides of the parallelogram and its altitudes. Let the sides of the parallelogram be \\(AB = CD = a\\) and \\(BC = DA = b\\). The perimeter of the parallelogram is given by:\n\n\\[\n2(a + b) = 40 \\implies a + b = 20.\n\\]\n\nThe area of the parallelogram can be expressed in two ways using the altitudes. If \\(h_a = 4\\) is the altitude corresponding to side \\(a\\), then the area is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = a \\cdot h_a = 4a.\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, if \\(h_b = 7\\) is the altitude corresponding to side \\(b\\), then the area is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = b \\cdot h_b = 7b.\n\\]\n\nSince both expressions represent the area of the same parallelogram, we equate them:\n\n\\[\n4a = 7b.\n\\]\n\nNow, we have the system of equations:\n\n1. \\(a + b = 20\\),\n2. \\(4a = 7b\\).\n\nFrom the second equation, solve for \\(a\\) in terms of \\(b\\):\n\n\\[\na = \\frac{7}{4}b.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute this expression for \\(a\\) into the first equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{7}{4}b + b = 20.\n\\]\n\nCombine the terms:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{7}{4}b + \\frac{4}{4}b = 20 \\implies \\frac{11}{4}b = 20.\n\\]\n\nSolve for \\(b\\):\n\n\\[\nb = \\frac{20 \\times 4}{11} = \\frac{80}{11}.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back to find \\(a\\):\n\n\\[\na = \\frac{7}{4} \\times \\frac{80}{11} = \\frac{560}{44} = \\frac{140}{11}.\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find \\(\\sin A\\) using the relationship between the area, the sides, and the sine of the angle:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = ab \\sin A.\n\\]\n\nWe already know the area from either altitude:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = 4a = 4 \\times \\frac{140}{11} = \\frac{560}{11}.\n\\]\n\nEquating the two expressions for the area:\n\n\\[\nab \\sin A = \\frac{560}{11}.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(a = \\frac{140}{11}\\) and \\(b = \\frac{80}{11}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{140}{11}\\right) \\left(\\frac{80}{11}\\right) \\sin A = \\frac{560}{11}.\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{11200}{121} \\sin A = \\frac{560}{11}.\n\\]\n\nSolve for \\(\\sin A\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin A = \\frac{560}{11} \\times \\frac{121}{11200} = \\frac{560 \\times 121}{11 \\times 11200}.\n\\]\n\nSimplify the fraction:\n\n\\[\n\\sin A = \\frac{560 \\times 11}{11 \\times 11200} = \\frac{560}{11200} = \\frac{1}{20}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin A = \\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\).'
"To solve the equation \\(\\tan (x + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan (x + 50^{\\circ}) \\tan x \\tan (x - 50^{\\circ})\\), we start by using the tangent addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a + b) = \\frac{\\tan a + \\tan b}{1 - \\tan a \\tan b}\n\\]\n\nApplying this to \\(\\tan(x + 100^{\\circ})\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(x + 100^{\\circ}) = \\frac{\\tan x + \\tan 100^{\\circ}}{1 - \\tan x \\tan 100^{\\circ}}\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, for \\(\\tan(x + 50^{\\circ})\\) and \\(\\tan(x - 50^{\\circ})\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(x + 50^{\\circ}) = \\frac{\\tan x + \\tan 50^{\\circ}}{1 - \\tan x \\tan 50^{\\circ}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(x - 50^{\\circ}) = \\frac{\\tan x - \\tan 50^{\\circ}}{1 + \\tan x \\tan 50^{\\circ}}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the original equation, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\tan x + \\tan 100^{\\circ}}{1 - \\tan x \\tan 100^{\\circ}} = \\left(\\frac{\\tan x + \\tan 50^{\\circ}}{1 - \\tan x \\tan 50^{\\circ}}\\right) \\tan x \\left(\\frac{\\tan x - \\tan 50^{\\circ}}{1 + \\tan x \\tan 50^{\\circ}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nThis equation is quite complex, so let's consider specific values of \\(x\\) that might simplify the equation. A natural choice is to test \\(x = 45^{\\circ}\\), since tangent values at multiples of 45 degrees are often simpler.\n\nSubstituting \\(x = 45^{\\circ}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan(45^{\\circ} + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan 145^{\\circ} = -\\tan 35^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(45^{\\circ} + 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan 95^{\\circ} = \\tan 5^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(45^{\\circ} - 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan(-5^{\\circ}) = -\\tan 5^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n-\\tan 35^{\\circ} = \\tan 5^{\\circ} \\cdot 1 \\cdot (-\\tan 5^{\\circ})\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-\\tan 35^{\\circ} = -\\tan^2 5^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nThis equation does not hold, so \\(x = 45^{\\circ}\\) is not a solution. Let's try another approach by considering the periodicity of the tangent function. The tangent function has a period of \\(180^{\\circ}\\), so we can consider the periodic solutions.\n\nThe equation \\(\\tan (x + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan (x + 50^{\\circ}) \\tan x \\tan (x - 50^{\\circ})\\) suggests symmetry around \\(x = 0\\) or \\(x = 90^{\\circ}\\). Testing \\(x = 90^{\\circ}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan(90^{\\circ} + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan 190^{\\circ} = \\tan 10^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(90^{\\circ} + 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan 140^{\\circ} = -\\tan 40^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(90^{\\circ} - 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan 40^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 10^{\\circ} = (-\\tan 40^{\\circ}) \\cdot \\infty \\cdot \\tan 40^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nThis equation is undefined due to the \\(\\infty\\) term, indicating \\(x = 90^{\\circ}\\) is not a valid solution. \n\nFinally, let's test \\(x = 0^{\\circ}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan(0^{\\circ} + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan 100^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(0^{\\circ} + 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan 50^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(0^{\\circ} - 50^{\\circ}) = -\\tan 50^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 100^{\\circ} = \\tan 50^{\\circ} \\cdot 0 \\cdot (-\\tan 50^{\\circ}) = 0\n\\]\n\nThis equation does not hold, so \\(x = 0^{\\circ}\\) is not a solution either.\n\nGiven the complexity and symmetry of the problem, the smallest positive solution is likely to be a simple angle that satisfies the symmetry of the tangent function. Testing \\(x = 30^{\\circ}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan(30^{\\circ} + 100^{\\circ}) = \\tan 130^{\\circ} = -\\tan 50^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(30^{\\circ} + 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan 80^{\\circ} = \\tan 10^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\tan(30^{\\circ} - 50^{\\circ}) = \\tan(-20^{\\circ}) = -\\tan 20^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n-\\tan 50^{\\circ} = \\tan 10^{\\circ} \\cdot \\tan 30^{\\circ} \\cdot (-\\tan 20^{\\circ})\n\\]\n\nThis equation simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 50^{\\circ} = \\tan 10^{\\circ} \\cdot \\tan 30^{\\circ} \\cdot \\tan 20^{\\circ}\n\\]\n\nThis equation holds true, indicating that \\(x = 30^{\\circ}\\) is indeed a solution. Therefore, the smallest positive value of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{30^{\\circ}}\\)."
'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) such that for any vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\), \\(\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v}\\) is the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(y\\)-axis, we need to determine how the projection operation works in terms of a matrix.\n\nThe projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(y\\)-axis is given by:\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{y\\text{-axis}} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe want to find a matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) such that:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) can be represented as:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nApplying \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} ax + by \\\\ cx + dy \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe want this to equal \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) for any vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\). This gives us the equations:\n1. \\(ax + by = 0\\)\n2. \\(cx + dy = y\\)\n\nFor the first equation to hold for any \\(x\\) and \\(y\\), we need \\(a = 0\\) and \\(b = 0\\).\n\nFor the second equation to hold for any \\(x\\) and \\(y\\), we need \\(c = 0\\) and \\(d = 1\\).\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that projects any vector onto the \\(y\\)-axis is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\arctan 1 + \\arctan \\frac{1}{2} + \\arctan \\frac{1}{3}\\), we can use the tangent addition formula. Using the tangent addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a + b) = \\frac{\\tan a + \\tan b}{1 - \\tan a \\tan b}\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = \\arctan \\frac{1}{2}\\) and \\(b = \\arctan \\frac{1}{3}\\). Then \\(\\tan a = \\frac{1}{2}\\) and \\(\\tan b = \\frac{1}{3}\\). "To find the area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\), we first determine the coordinates of the midpoints \\(E\\), \\(F\\), \\(G\\), and \\(H\\).\n\n1. **Midpoint \\(E\\) of \\(\\overline{BD}\\):**\n   \\[\n   B = (1, 0, 0), \\quad D = (0, 0, 3)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   E = \\left( \\frac{1+0}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+3}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, 0, \\frac{3}{2} \\right)\n   \\]\n\n2. **Midpoint \\(F\\) of \\(\\overline{AB}\\):**\n   \\[\n   A = (0, 0, 0), \\quad B = (1, 0, 0)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   F = \\left( \\frac{0+1}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, 0, 0 \\right)\n   \\]\n\n3. **Midpoint \\(G\\) of \\(\\overline{AC}\\):**\n   \\[\n   A = (0, 0, 0), \\quad C = (0, 2, 0)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   G = \\left( \\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+2}{2}, \\frac{0+0}{2} \\right) = \\left( 0, 1, 0 \\right)\n   \\]\n\n4. **Midpoint \\(H\\) of \\(\\overline{DC}\\):**\n   \\[\n   D = (0, 0, 3), \\quad C = (0, 2, 0)\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   H = \\left( \\frac{0+0}{2}, \\frac{0+2}{2}, \\frac{3+0}{2} \\right) = \\left( 0, 1, \\frac{3}{2} \\right)\n   \\]\n\nNow, we have the coordinates of the vertices of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\):\n- \\(E = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, 0, \\frac{3}{2} \\right)\\)\n- \\(F = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, 0, 0 \\right)\\)\n- \\(G = (0, 1, 0)\\)\n- \\(H = \\left( 0, 1, \\frac{3}{2} \\right)\\)\n\nTo find the area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\), we can project it onto the \\(xy\\)-plane and calculate the area of the resulting 2D quadrilateral. The projection of the points onto the \\(xy\\)-plane are:\n- \\(E' = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, 0 \\right)\\)\n- \\(F' = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, 0 \\right)\\)\n- \\(G' = (0, 1)\\)\n- \\(H' = (0, 1)\\)\n\nNotice that the projection of \\(EFGH\\) onto the \\(xy\\)-plane results in a degenerate quadrilateral (a line segment), so we need to consider the actual 3D structure.\n\nThe vectors forming the sides of the quadrilateral in 3D are:\n- \\(\\overrightarrow{EF} = \\left( 0, 0, -\\frac{3}{2} \\right)\\)\n- \\(\\overrightarrow{FG} = \\left( -\\frac{1}{2}, 1, 0 \\right)\\)\n- \\(\\overrightarrow{GH} = \\left( 0, 0, \\frac{3}{2} \\right)\\)\n- \\(\\overrightarrow{HE} = \\left( \\frac{1}{2}, -1, 0 \\right)\\)\n\nThe area of the quadrilateral can be found using the cross product of vectors \\(\\overrightarrow{EF}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{FG}\\) to find the area of one triangle, and similarly for \\(\\overrightarrow{GH}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{HE}\\).\n\n1. **Area of triangle \\(EFG\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\overrightarrow{EF} \\times \\overrightarrow{FG} = \\left| \\begin{array}{ccc}\n   \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\\n   0 & 0 & -\\frac{3}{2} \\\\\n   -\\frac{1}{2} & 1 & 0 \\\\\n   \\end{array} \\right|\n   = \\mathbf{i} \\left( 0 - 0 \\right) - \\mathbf{j} \\left( 0 - \\frac{3}{4} \\right) + \\mathbf{k} \\left( 0 - 0 \\right)\n   = \\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{j}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\text{Magnitude} = \\left| \\frac{3}{4} \\right| = \\frac{3}{4}\n   \\]\n\n2. **Area of triangle \\(GHF\\):**\n   \\[\n   \\overrightarrow{GH} \\times \\overrightarrow{HE} = \\left| \\begin{array}{ccc}\n   \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\\n   0 & 0 & \\frac{3}{2} \\\\\n   \\frac{1}{2} & -1 & 0 \\\\\n   \\end{array} \\right|\n   = \\mathbf{i} \\left( 0 - 0 \\right) - \\mathbf{j} \\left( 0 - \\frac{3}{4} \\right) + \\mathbf{k} \\left( 0 - 0 \\right)\n   = \\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{j}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\text{Magnitude} = \\left| \\frac{3}{4} \\right| = \\frac{3}{4}\n   \\]\n\nThe total area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\) is the sum of the areas of the two triangles:\n\\[\n\\text{Total Area} = \\frac{3}{4} + \\frac{3}{4} = \\frac{3}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of quadrilateral \\(EFGH\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{2}}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to find all possible values of the expression \\( z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12} \\) where \\( z \\) is a 13th root of unity, and then compute the sum of the squares of these values.\n\nFirst, note that the 13th roots of unity are given by \\( z = e^{2\\pi i n / 13} \\) for \\( n = 0, 1, 2, \\ldots, 12 \\). These roots satisfy the equation \\( z^{13} = 1 \\).\n\nThe expression we are interested in is:\n\\[ w = z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}. \\]\n\nSince \\( z^{13} = 1 \\), we have \\( z^0 = 1 \\), \\( z^{13} = 1 \\), \\( z^{14} = z \\), and so on. Therefore, the powers of \\( z \\) are periodic with period 13.\n\nThe sum of all 13th roots of unity is zero:\n\\[ 1 + z + z^2 + \\cdots + z^{12} = 0. \\]\n\nWe can use this property to simplify the expression for \\( w \\). Notice that:\n\\[ w = z + z^3 + z^4 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{12}. \\]\n\nWe can express the sum of all roots as:\n\\[ 1 + z + z^2 + z^3 + z^4 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^9 + z^{10} + z^{11} + z^{12} = 0. \\]\n\nSubtracting the terms not in \\( w \\) from this sum, we have:\n\\[ w = -(1 + z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}). \\]\n\nThus, \\( w = -\\left(1 + z^2 + z^5 + z^6 + z^7 + z^8 + z^{11}\\right) \\).\n\nNow, we need to find the possible values of \\( w \\). Since \\( z \\) is a 13th root of unity, the possible values of \\( w \\) are determined by the specific powers of \\( z \\) that appear in the expression. The roots of unity are symmetrically distributed on the unit circle in the complex plane, and the expression for \\( w \\) is a linear combination of these roots.\n\nThe possible values of \\( w \\) are the sums of certain subsets of the 13th roots of unity. By symmetry and the properties of roots of unity, the possible values of \\( w \\) are also roots of unity or sums of roots of unity that are symmetric.\n\nTo find \\( w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \\cdots + w_k^2 \\), we note that the sum of the squares of the roots of unity is related to the sum of the roots themselves. Since the sum of all 13th roots of unity is zero, the sum of the squares of the roots of unity is also zero.\n\nTherefore, the sum of the squares of all possible values of \\( w \\) is:\n\\[ w_1^2 + w_2^2 + \\cdots + w_k^2 = 0. \\]\n\nThus, the final answer is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{0}\n\\]'
"To find the length \\( OP \\), we can use the concept of the power of a point and the properties of the circumcircle.\n\nGiven that \\( \\angle A = 45^\\circ \\), we know that the circumcircle of triangle \\( ABC \\) has a special property because \\( \\angle A \\) is a right angle. The circumcenter \\( O \\) of triangle \\( ABC \\) is the midpoint of the hypotenuse \\( BC \\).\n\nLet's denote the length of \\( BC \\) as \\( a \\). Since \\( P \\) is a point on \\( BC \\) with \\( PB = 3 \\) and \\( PC = 5 \\), we have:\n\\[\na = PB + PC = 3 + 5 = 8.\n\\]\n\nThe circumcenter \\( O \\) is the midpoint of \\( BC \\), so the coordinates of \\( O \\) are the average of the coordinates of \\( B \\) and \\( C \\). If we place \\( B \\) at the origin \\((0, 0)\\) and \\( C \\) at \\((8, 0)\\), then the coordinates of \\( O \\) are:\n\\[\nO = \\left( \\frac{0 + 8}{2}, \\frac{0 + 0}{2} \\right) = (4, 0).\n\\]\n\nThe point \\( P \\) divides \\( BC \\) in the ratio \\( 3:5 \\). Therefore, the coordinates of \\( P \\) are:\n\\[\nP = \\left( \\frac{5 \\cdot 0 + 3 \\cdot 8}{3 + 5}, \\frac{5 \\cdot 0 + 3 \\cdot 0}{3 + 5} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{24}{8}, 0 \\right) = (3, 0).\n\\]\n\nNow, we calculate the distance \\( OP \\):\n\\[\nOP = \\sqrt{(4 - 3)^2 + (0 - 0)^2} = \\sqrt{1^2} = 1.\n\\]\n\nThus, the length \\( OP \\) is \\(\\boxed{1}\\)."
'To find the range of the function \\( y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) \\) for \\( 0^\\circ < x < 180^\\circ \\), we first need to determine the range of the expression inside the logarithm, \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\).\n\n1. **Range of \\(\\sin x\\):**\n\n   For \\( 0^\\circ < x < 180^\\circ \\), the sine function \\(\\sin x\\) takes values from 0 to 1. Specifically, \\(\\sin x\\) increases from 0 to 1 as \\(x\\) goes from \\(0^\\circ\\) to \\(90^\\circ\\), and then decreases from 1 to 0 as \\(x\\) goes from \\(90^\\circ\\) to \\(180^\\circ\\). Therefore, the range of \\(\\sin x\\) is \\( (0, 1] \\).\n\n2. **Range of \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\):**\n\n   Since \\(\\sin x\\) is in the range \\((0, 1]\\), \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\) will take values from \\(\\sqrt{0}\\) to \\(\\sqrt{1}\\), which is \\((0, 1]\\).\n\n3. **Range of \\(\\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x})\\):**\n\n   We now consider the function \\( y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) \\). Since \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x}\\) is in the range \\((0, 1]\\), we need to find the range of the logarithm function over this interval.\n\n   - As \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x} \\to 0^+\\), \\(\\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) \\to -\\infty\\).\n   - When \\(\\sqrt{\\sin x} = 1\\), \\(\\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) = \\log_2 (1) = 0\\).\n\n   Therefore, the range of \\( y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) \\) is \\((- \\infty, 0]\\).\n\nThus, the range of the function \\( y = \\log_2 (\\sqrt{\\sin x}) \\) for \\( 0^\\circ < x < 180^\\circ \\) is \\(\\boxed{(-\\infty, 0]}\\).'
'To find the ordered pair \\((a, c)\\) such that the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\), \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\) are pairwise orthogonal, we need to ensure that the dot product of each pair of vectors is zero.\n\n1. **Orthogonality between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 1 \\cdot 2 + (-1) \\cdot 4 + 2 \\cdot 1 = 2 - 4 + 2 = 0\n   \\]\n\n   This condition is already satisfied.\n\n2. **Orthogonality between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = 1 \\cdot a + (-1) \\cdot 1 + 2 \\cdot c = a - 1 + 2c = 0\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   a + 2c = 1 \\quad \\text{(Equation 1)}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Orthogonality between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix}\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 1 \\\\ c \\end{pmatrix} = 2 \\cdot a + 4 \\cdot 1 + 1 \\cdot c = 2a + 4 + c = 0\n   \\]\n\n   Simplifying, we get:\n   \\[\n   2a + c = -4 \\quad \\text{(Equation 2)}\n   \\]\n\nNow, we solve the system of equations:\n\n- Equation 1: \\(a + 2c = 1\\)\n- Equation 2: \\(2a + c = -4\\)\n\nFrom Equation 2, solve for \\(c\\):\n\\[\nc = -4 - 2a\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(c = -4 - 2a\\) into Equation 1:\n\\[\na + 2(-4 - 2a) = 1\n\\]\n\\[\na - 8 - 4a = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n-3a - 8 = 1\n\\]\n\\[\n-3a = 9\n\\]\n\\[\na = -3\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(a = -3\\) back into the expression for \\(c\\):\n\\[\nc = -4 - 2(-3)\n\\]\n\\[\nc = -4 + 6\n\\]\n\\[\nc = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, the ordered pair \\((a, c)\\) is \\(\\boxed{(-3, 2)}\\).'
'To find the curve defined by the equation \\( r^2 \\cos 2\\theta = 4 \\), we start by using the polar to rectangular coordinate transformations. Recall that:\n\n- \\( r^2 = x^2 + y^2 \\)\n- \\( \\cos 2\\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{x^2 - y^2}{x^2 + y^2} \\)\n\nSubstituting these into the given equation:\n\n\\[ r^2 \\cos 2\\theta = 4 \\]\n\nbecomes\n\n\\[ (x^2 + y^2) \\left(\\frac{x^2 - y^2}{x^2 + y^2}\\right) = 4 \\]\n\nwhich simplifies to:\n\n\\[ x^2 - y^2 = 4 \\]\n\nThis is the equation of a hyperbola in rectangular coordinates. Therefore, the correct option is:\n\n(E) Hyperbola'
'To find the direction vector of the line \\(\\ell\\) over which the reflection is performed, we need to identify the eigenvector of the given reflection matrix corresponding to the eigenvalue 1. This is because the line of reflection is invariant under the reflection transformation, meaning any vector along this line remains unchanged.\n\nThe given reflection matrix is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix}\n-\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{1}{3} \\\\\n-\\frac{2}{3} & \\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} \\\\\n-\\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3}\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe need to solve the equation:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix}\n-\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{1}{3} \\\\\n-\\\\frac{2}{3} & \\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} \\\\\n-\\frac{1}{3} & \\frac{2}{3} & -\\frac{2}{3}\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\begin{pmatrix}\nx \\\\\ny \\\\\nz\n\\end{pmatrix}\n=\n\\begin{pmatrix}\nx \\\\\ny \\\\\nz\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix}\n-\\frac{2}{3}x - \\frac{2}{3}y - \\frac{1}{3}z \\\\\n-\\frac{2}{3}x + \\frac{1}{3}y + \\frac{2}{3}z \\\\\n-\\frac{1}{3}x + \\frac{2}{3}y - \\frac{2}{3}z\n\\end{pmatrix}\n=\n\\begin{pmatrix}\nx \\\\\ny \\\\\nz\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us the system of equations:\n1. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}x - \\frac{2}{3}y - \\frac{1}{3}z = x\\)\n2. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}x + \\frac{1}{3}y + \\frac{2}{3}z = y\\)\n3. \\(-\\frac{1}{3}x + \\frac{2}{3}y - \\frac{2}{3}z = z\\)\n\nSimplifying each equation, we have:\n1. \\(-\\frac{5}{3}x - \\frac{2}{3}y - \\frac{1}{3}z = 0\\)\n2. \\(-\\frac{2}{3}x - \\frac{2}{3}y + \\frac{2}{3}z = 0\\)\n3. \\(-\\frac{1}{3}x + \\frac{2}{3}y - \\frac{5}{3}z = 0\\)\n\nMultiply each equation by 3 to clear the fractions:\n1. \\(-5x - 2y - z = 0\\)\n2. \\(-2x - 2y + 2z = 0\\)\n3. \\(-x + 2y - 5z = 0\\)\n\nFrom equation 2, we can simplify to:\n\\[ -x - y + z = 0 \\]\nor\n\\[ x + y = z \\]\n\nSubstitute \\(z = x + y\\) into the other equations:\n1. \\(-5x - 2y - (x + y) = 0\\) simplifies to \\(-6x - 3y = 0\\) or \\(2x + y = 0\\)\n3. \\(-x + 2y - 5(x + y) = 0\\) simplifies to \\(-6x - 3y = 0\\) or \\(2x + y = 0\\)\n\nFrom \\(2x + y = 0\\), we have \\(y = -2x\\).\n\nSubstitute \\(y = -2x\\) into \\(z = x + y\\):\n\\[ z = x - 2x = -x \\]\n\nThus, the direction vector is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix}\nx \\\\\n-2x \\\\\n-x\n\\end{pmatrix}\n= x \\begin{pmatrix}\n1 \\\\\n-2 \\\\\n-1\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nChoosing \\(x = 1\\), the direction vector is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix}\n1 \\\\\n-2 \\\\\n-1\n\\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo satisfy the condition \\(a > 0\\) and \\(\\gcd(|a|, |b|, |c|) = 1\\), the direction vector is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
'To simplify the expression \n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos x}{1 - \\sin x} - \\frac{\\cos x}{1 + \\sin x},\n\\]\n\nwe start by finding a common denominator. The common denominator for the two fractions is \\((1 - \\sin x)(1 + \\sin x)\\).\n\nUsing the identity \\((1 - \\sin x)(1 + \\sin x) = 1 - \\sin^2 x = \\cos^2 x\\), we can rewrite the expression as:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos x (1 + \\sin x) - \\cos x (1 - \\sin x)}{(1 - \\sin x)(1 + \\sin x)}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the numerator:\n\n\\[\n\\cos x (1 + \\sin x) - \\cos x (1 - \\sin x) = \\cos x + \\cos x \\sin x - \\cos x + \\cos x \\sin x = 2 \\cos x \\sin x.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2 \\cos x \\sin x}{\\cos^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nWe can simplify this further by canceling \\(\\cos x\\) from the numerator and the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2 \\cos x \\sin x}{\\cos^2 x} = \\frac{2 \\sin x}{\\cos x} = 2 \\tan x.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{2 \\tan x}\\).'
'To evaluate \\(\\sin(1998^\\circ + 237^\\circ)\\sin(1998^\\circ - 1653^\\circ)\\), we start by simplifying the angles.\n\nFirst, calculate the angles:\n\n1. \\(1998^\\circ + 237^\\circ = 2235^\\circ\\).\n2. \\(1998^\\circ - 1653^\\circ = 345^\\circ\\).\n\nNext, reduce these angles modulo \\(360^\\circ\\) to find their equivalent angles within the standard range \\([0^\\circ, 360^\\circ)\\):\n\n- \\(2235^\\circ \\mod 360^\\circ = 75^\\circ\\) (since \\(2235^\\circ = 6 \\times 360^\\circ + 75^\\circ\\)).\n- \\(345^\\circ \\mod 360^\\circ = 345^\\circ\\) (since \\(345^\\circ\\) is already within the range).\n\nNow, the expression becomes \\(\\sin(75^\\circ)\\sin(345^\\circ)\\).\n\nRecall that \\(\\sin(345^\\circ) = \\sin(360^\\circ - 15^\\circ) = -\\sin(15^\\circ)\\).\n\nThus, the expression simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\sin(75^\\circ)\\sin(345^\\circ) = \\sin(75^\\circ)(-\\sin(15^\\circ)) = -\\sin(75^\\circ)\\sin(15^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity for the product of sines:\n\\[\n\\sin A \\sin B = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(A - B) - \\cos(A + B)],\n\\]\nwe substitute \\(A = 75^\\circ\\) and \\(B = 15^\\circ\\):\n\\[\n\\sin(75^\\circ)\\sin(15^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(75^\\circ - 15^\\circ) - \\cos(75^\\circ + 15^\\circ)].\n\\]\n\nCalculate the cosine terms:\n- \\(\\cos(75^\\circ - 15^\\circ) = \\cos(60^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{2}\\),\n- \\(\\cos(75^\\circ + 15^\\circ) = \\cos(90^\\circ) = 0\\).\n\nThus:\n\\[\n\\sin(75^\\circ)\\sin(15^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{2} \\left[\\frac{1}{2} - 0\\right] = \\frac{1}{4}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore:\n\\[\n-\\sin(75^\\circ)\\sin(15^\\circ) = -\\frac{1}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThe final answer is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{1}{4}}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\sec \\theta + \\csc \\theta = \\sqrt{15}\\), we start by expressing \\(\\sec \\theta\\) and \\(\\csc \\theta\\) in terms of \\(\\sin \\theta\\) and \\(\\cos \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sec \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta}, \\quad \\csc \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\sin \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta} + \\frac{1}{\\sin \\theta} = \\sqrt{15}.\n\\]\n\nCombining the fractions, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta}{\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta} = \\sqrt{15}.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\(\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\), we get:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\sqrt{15} \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nTo simplify, we use the identity \\((\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta)^2 = \\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta + 2\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = 1 + 2\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\). Let \\(x = \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\). Then:\n\n\\[\nx^2 = 1 + 2\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back, we have:\n\n\\[\nx = \\sqrt{15} \\cdot \\frac{x^2 - 1}{2}.\n\\]\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n2x = \\sqrt{15}(x^2 - 1).\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{15}x^2 - 2x - \\sqrt{15} = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(x\\). Solving for \\(x\\) using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = \\sqrt{15}\\), \\(b = -2\\), and \\(c = -\\sqrt{15}\\). Thus:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{(-2)^2 - 4(\\sqrt{15})(-\\sqrt{15})}}{2\\sqrt{15}}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the discriminant:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{4 + 60}}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{2 \\pm \\sqrt{64}}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{2 \\pm 8}{2\\sqrt{15}}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives two solutions:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{10}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{5}{\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{3},\n\\]\n\nand\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-6}{2\\sqrt{15}} = \\frac{-3}{\\sqrt{15}} = -\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{5}.\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{3}\\), we check if this is possible. The maximum value of \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\) is \\(\\sqrt{2}\\), and \\(\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{3} \\approx 1.29\\), which is greater than \\(\\sqrt{2}\\). Therefore, this is not possible.\n\nFor \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = -\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{5}\\), we check if this is possible. The minimum value of \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\) is \\(-\\sqrt{2}\\), and \\(-\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{5} \\approx -0.7746\\), which is within the range. Therefore, this is possible.\n\nThus, there are two angles \\(\\theta\\) in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) that satisfy \\(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = -\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{5}\\).\n\nTherefore, the number of solutions is \\(\\boxed{2}\\).'
'To find the minimum value of \\( |z - w| \\), we can use the triangle inequality for complex numbers. The triangle inequality states that for any complex numbers \\( z \\) and \\( w \\),\n\n\\[\n|z - w| \\geq ||z| - |w||\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\( |z| = 5 \\) and \\( |w| = 2 \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n||z| - |w|| = |5 - 2| = 3\n\\]\n\nThe minimum value of \\( |z - w| \\) is therefore 3. This minimum is achieved when \\( z \\) and \\( w \\) are collinear with the origin, with \\( w \\) being in the direction of \\( z \\) but closer to the origin. Thus, the minimum value of \\( |z - w| \\) is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'
"To find the integer \\( n \\) such that \\(-90 < n < 90\\) and \\(\\tan n^\\circ = \\tan 252^\\circ\\), we need to use the periodicity of the tangent function. The tangent function has a period of \\(180^\\circ\\), meaning \\(\\tan \\theta = \\tan (\\theta + 180^\\circ k)\\) for any integer \\( k \\).\n\nFirst, let's find an equivalent angle to \\(252^\\circ\\) within the range \\(-90^\\circ < n < 90^\\circ\\). We can do this by subtracting \\(180^\\circ\\) from \\(252^\\circ\\) until we get an angle within the desired range:\n\n\\[\n252^\\circ - 180^\\circ = 72^\\circ\n\\]\n\nSince \\(72^\\circ\\) is already within the range \\(-90^\\circ < n < 90^\\circ\\), we have found our equivalent angle. Therefore, the integer \\( n \\) such that \\(\\tan n^\\circ = \\tan 252^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{72}\\)."
"To find the value of \\(8\\cos^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}\\), we start by expressing \\(\\cos^2 10^\\circ\\) in terms of \\(\\sin 10^\\circ\\).\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\cos^2 10^\\circ = 1 - \\sin^2 10^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n8\\cos^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ} = 8(1 - \\sin^2 10^\\circ) - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n8 - 8\\sin^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nTo simplify further, we need to find a common denominator for the terms involving \\(\\sin 10^\\circ\\). The common denominator is \\(\\sin 10^\\circ\\), so we rewrite the expression as:\n\\[\n\\frac{8\\sin 10^\\circ - 8\\sin^3 10^\\circ - 1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nNow, let's simplify the numerator:\n\\[\n8\\sin 10^\\circ - 8\\sin^3 10^\\circ - 1.\n\\]\n\nWe can factor out \\(\\sin 10^\\circ\\) from the first two terms:\n\\[\n\\sin 10^\\circ (8 - 8\\sin^2 10^\\circ) - 1.\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = 1 - \\cos^2 \\theta\\), we have:\n\\[\n8 - 8\\sin^2 10^\\circ = 8(1 - \\sin^2 10^\\circ) = 8\\cos^2 10^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\sin 10^\\circ (8\\cos^2 10^\\circ) - 1.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos^2 10^\\circ = 1 - \\sin^2 10^\\circ\\), we substitute back:\n\\[\n8\\cos^2 10^\\circ = 8(1 - \\sin^2 10^\\circ) = 8 - 8\\sin^2 10^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the expression simplifies to:\n\\[\n8\\sin 10^\\circ - 8\\sin^3 10^\\circ - 1 = 8\\sin 10^\\circ - 8\\sin^3 10^\\circ - 1.\n\\]\n\nThis expression is already simplified, and we cannot simplify it further without additional trigonometric identities or approximations. Therefore, the value of the expression is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{8\\cos^2 10^\\circ - \\dfrac{1}{\\sin 10^\\circ}}.\n\\]"
'To solve for \\(\\sec x + \\tan x\\), given that \\(\\sec x - \\tan x = 2\\), we can use the identity:\n\n\\[\n(\\sec x + \\tan x)(\\sec x - \\tan x) = \\sec^2 x - \\tan^2 x = 1\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\sec x - \\tan x = 2\\), we substitute into the identity:\n\n\\[\n(\\sec x + \\tan x) \\cdot 2 = 1\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\sec x + \\tan x\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sec x + \\tan x = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\sec x + \\tan x\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\\).'
"To find the equation of the plane containing the points \\((2,0,0)\\), \\((0,-5,0)\\), and \\((0,0,-4)\\), we first need to find a normal vector to the plane. We can do this by finding two vectors in the plane and taking their cross product.\n\nLet's find the vectors \\(\\mathbf{v}_1\\) and \\(\\mathbf{v}_2\\) using the given points:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v}_1 = (0, -5, 0) - (2, 0, 0) = (-2, -5, 0)\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v}_2 = (0, 0, -4) - (2, 0, 0) = (-2, 0, -4)\n\\]\n\nNow, we find the cross product \\(\\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{v}_1 \\times \\mathbf{v}_2\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ -2 & -5 & 0 \\\\ -2 & 0 & -4 \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i}((-5)(-4) - (0)(0)) - \\mathbf{j}((-2)(-4) - (0)(-2)) + \\mathbf{k}((-2)(0) - (-5)(-2))\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i}(20) - \\mathbf{j}(8) + \\mathbf{k}(10)\n\\]\n\\[\n= (20, -8, 10)\n\\]\n\nThe normal vector to the plane is \\((20, -8, 10)\\). The equation of the plane is given by:\n\\[\n20x - 8y + 10z + D = 0\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(D\\), we substitute one of the points, say \\((2, 0, 0)\\), into the equation:\n\\[\n20(2) - 8(0) + 10(0) + D = 0\n\\]\n\\[\n40 + D = 0\n\\]\n\\[\nD = -40\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n20x - 8y + 10z - 40 = 0\n\\]\n\nTo ensure the coefficients are in their simplest form, we divide the entire equation by 2:\n\\[\n10x - 4y + 5z - 20 = 0\n\\]\n\nThe greatest common divisor of the coefficients \\(10\\), \\(-4\\), \\(5\\), and \\(-20\\) is 1, and \\(A = 10 > 0\\). Therefore, the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{10x - 4y + 5z - 20 = 0}\n\\]"
'To solve this problem, we start by expressing the complex number \\( z \\) in terms of its polar form: \\( z = re^{i\\theta} \\). Given that \\( \\left| 2z + \\frac{1}{z} \\right| = 1 \\), we need to find the minimum value of \\( \\sin^2 \\theta \\).\n\nFirst, express \\( 2z + \\frac{1}{z} \\) in terms of \\( r \\) and \\( \\theta \\):\n\n\\[\n2z = 2re^{i\\theta} = 2r(\\cos \\theta + i \\sin \\theta)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{z} = \\frac{1}{re^{i\\theta}} = \\frac{1}{r}e^{-i\\theta} = \\frac{1}{r}(\\cos \\theta - i \\sin \\theta)\n\\]\n\nAdding these, we have:\n\n\\[\n2z + \\frac{1}{z} = 2r(\\cos \\theta + i \\sin \\theta) + \\frac{1}{r}(\\cos \\theta - i \\sin \\theta)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\left(2r \\cos \\theta + \\frac{1}{r} \\cos \\theta\\right) + i\\left(2r \\sin \\theta - \\frac{1}{r} \\sin \\theta\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\left(2r + \\frac{1}{r}\\right) \\cos \\theta + i\\left(2r - \\frac{1}{r}\\right) \\sin \\theta\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this expression is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\left| 2z + \\frac{1}{z} \\right| = \\sqrt{\\left(\\left(2r + \\frac{1}{r}\\right) \\cos \\theta\\right)^2 + \\left(\\left(2r - \\frac{1}{r}\\right) \\sin \\theta\\right)^2}\n\\]\n\nSetting this equal to 1, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{\\left(2r + \\frac{1}{r}\\right)^2 \\cos^2 \\theta + \\left(2r - \\frac{1}{r}\\right)^2 \\sin^2 \\theta} = 1\n\\]\n\nSquaring both sides, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\left(2r + \\frac{1}{r}\\right)^2 \\cos^2 \\theta + \\left(2r - \\frac{1}{r}\\right)^2 \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\nExpanding both terms:\n\n\\[\n(4r^2 + 4 + \\frac{1}{r^2}) \\cos^2 \\theta + (4r^2 - 4 + \\frac{1}{r^2}) \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we have:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 \\cos^2 \\theta + 4 \\cos^2 \\theta + \\frac{1}{r^2} \\cos^2 \\theta + 4r^2 \\sin^2 \\theta - 4 \\sin^2 \\theta + \\frac{1}{r^2} \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n4r^2 (\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta) + \\frac{1}{r^2} (\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta) + 4 \\cos^2 \\theta - 4 \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), this simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2} + 4 \\cos^2 \\theta - 4 \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2} + 4 (\\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta) = 1\n\\]\n\nLet \\( x = \\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta \\). Then:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2} + 4x = 1\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( x \\):\n\n\\[\n4x = 1 - 4r^2 - \\frac{1}{r^2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{1 - 4r^2 - \\frac{1}{r^2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nSince \\( x = \\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta \\), and we want to minimize \\( \\sin^2 \\theta \\), we use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta - \\sin^2 \\theta = x\n\\]\n\nAdding these two equations:\n\n\\[\n2\\cos^2 \\theta = 1 + x\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 + x}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubtracting the equations:\n\n\\[\n2\\sin^2 \\theta = 1 - x\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - x}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( x = \\frac{1 - 4r^2 - \\frac{1}{r^2}}{4} \\) into the expression for \\( \\sin^2 \\theta \\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1 - \\frac{1 - 4r^2 - \\frac{1}{r^2}}{4}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{4 - (1 - 4r^2 - \\frac{1}{r^2})}{8}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{4 - 1 + 4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2}}{8}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{3 + 4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2}}{8}\n\\]\n\nTo minimize \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\), we need to minimize \\(3 + 4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2}\\). By AM-GM inequality:\n\n\\[\n4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2} \\geq 2\\sqrt{4r^2 \\cdot \\frac{1}{r^2}} = 4\n\\]\n\nThus, the minimum value of \\(4r^2 + \\frac{1}{r^2}\\) is 4, achieved when \\(4r^2 = \\frac{1}{r^2}\\), or \\(r^3 = \\frac{1}{2}\\), giving \\(r = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt[3]{2}}\\).\n\nSubstituting back, the minimum value of \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{3 + 4}{8} = \\frac{7}{8}\n\\]\n\nThus, the minimum value of \\(\\sin^2 \\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{8}}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\tan 15^\\circ\\), we can use the tangent subtraction formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a - b) = \\frac{\\tan a - \\tan b}{1 + \\tan a \\tan b}\n\\]\n\nFor \\(a = 45^\\circ\\) and \\(b = 30^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 45^\\circ = 1 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these values into the formula gives:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\tan(45^\\circ - 30^\\circ) = \\frac{\\tan 45^\\circ - \\tan 30^\\circ}{1 + \\tan 45^\\circ \\tan 30^\\circ} = \\frac{1 - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}{1 + 1 \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{1 - \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}}{1 + \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}} = \\frac{\\frac{\\sqrt{3} - 1}{\\sqrt{3}}}{\\frac{\\sqrt{3} + 1}{\\sqrt{3}}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3} - 1}{\\sqrt{3} + 1}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify further, multiply the numerator and the denominator by the conjugate of the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{(\\sqrt{3} - 1)(\\sqrt{3} - 1)}{(\\sqrt{3} + 1)(\\sqrt{3} - 1)} = \\frac{(\\sqrt{3} - 1)^2}{(\\sqrt{3})^2 - 1^2}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the expressions:\n\n\\[\n(\\sqrt{3} - 1)^2 = (\\sqrt{3})^2 - 2 \\cdot \\sqrt{3} \\cdot 1 + 1^2 = 3 - 2\\sqrt{3} + 1 = 4 - 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n(\\sqrt{3})^2 - 1^2 = 3 - 1 = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 15^\\circ = \\frac{4 - 2\\sqrt{3}}{2} = 2 - \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\tan 15^\\circ = \\boxed{2 - \\sqrt{3}}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\cos \\frac{2 \\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4 \\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8 \\pi}{7}\\), we can use a known trigonometric identity for the product of cosines of angles that are fractions of \\(\\pi\\).\n\nThe identity states that for angles \\(\\theta_1, \\theta_2, \\theta_3\\) such that \\(\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 + \\theta_3 = \\pi\\), the product of their cosines is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta_1 \\cos \\theta_2 \\cos \\theta_3 = \\frac{1}{4} \\left( \\cos(\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 + \\theta_3) + \\cos(\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 - \\theta_3) + \\cos(\\theta_1 - \\theta_2 + \\theta_3) + \\cos(-\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 + \\theta_3) \\right)\n\\]\n\nIn our case, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\theta_1 = \\frac{2\\pi}{7}, \\quad \\theta_2 = \\frac{4\\pi}{7}, \\quad \\theta_3 = \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\(\\theta_3 = \\pi - \\frac{6\\pi}{7}\\), so we can rewrite \\(\\theta_3\\) as \\(\\pi - \\theta_1 - \\theta_2\\). Therefore, the sum \\(\\theta_1 + \\theta_2 + \\theta_3 = \\pi\\).\n\nUsing the identity, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{4} \\left( \\cos(\\pi) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\frac{4\\pi}{7} - \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7} - \\frac{4\\pi}{7} + \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\right) + \\cos\\left(-\\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\frac{4\\pi}{7} + \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\right) \\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term:\n\n- \\(\\cos(\\pi) = -1\\)\n- \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\frac{4\\pi}{7} - \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos(-\\frac{2\\pi}{7}) = \\cos\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\)\n- \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{2\\pi}{7} - \\frac{4\\pi}{7} + \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\frac{6\\pi}{7}\\)\n- \\(\\cos\\left(-\\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\frac{4\\pi}{7} + \\frac{8\\pi}{7}\\right) = \\cos\\frac{10\\pi}{7} = \\cos\\left(\\pi + \\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\right) = -\\cos\\frac{3\\pi}{7}\\)\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{4} \\left( -1 + \\cos\\frac{2\\pi}{7} + \\cos\\frac{6\\pi}{7} - \\cos\\frac{3\\pi}{7} \\right)\n\\]\n\nHowever, there is a known result for this specific product of cosines:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\frac{2\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{4\\pi}{7} \\cos \\frac{8\\pi}{7} = \\frac{1}{8}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{8}}\\).'
'To find \\(\\sin 2\\theta\\), we start by using the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\left( \\theta + \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right) = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nWe can use the angle addition formula for sine:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\left( \\theta + \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right) = \\sin \\theta \\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4} + \\cos \\theta \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\sin \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} + \\cos \\theta \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2} = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nMultiplying through by \\(\\sqrt{2}\\) to clear the fractions, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3}.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\sin 2\\theta\\), which can be expressed using the double angle formula:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\), we use the identity:\n\n\\[\n(\\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta)^2 = \\sin^2 \\theta + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3}\\right)^2 = 1 + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nCalculating \\(\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{3}\\right)^2\\), we get:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{9} = 1 + 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\), we subtract 1 from both sides:\n\n\\[\n2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = \\frac{2}{9} - 1 = \\frac{2}{9} - \\frac{9}{9} = -\\frac{7}{9}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = -\\frac{7}{9}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{7}{9}}\\).'
'To find the minimum value of the expression \\(\\frac{\\sec^4 \\alpha}{\\tan^2 \\beta} + \\frac{\\sec^4 \\beta}{\\tan^2 \\alpha}\\), we start by rewriting it in terms of sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sec^4 \\alpha}{\\tan^2 \\beta} = \\frac{1}{\\cos^4 \\alpha} \\cdot \\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta}{\\sin^2 \\beta} = \\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta}{\\cos^4 \\alpha \\sin^2 \\beta}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sec^4 \\beta}{\\tan^2 \\alpha} = \\frac{1}{\\cos^4 \\beta} \\cdot \\frac{\\cos^2 \\alpha}{\\sin^2 \\alpha} = \\frac{\\cos^2 \\alpha}{\\cos^4 \\beta \\sin^2 \\alpha}\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 \\beta}{\\cos^4 \\alpha \\sin^2 \\beta} + \\frac{\\cos^2 \\alpha}{\\cos^4 \\beta \\sin^2 \\alpha}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify, let \\(x = \\sec^2 \\alpha\\) and \\(y = \\sec^2 \\beta\\). Then \\(\\cos^2 \\alpha = \\frac{1}{x}\\) and \\(\\cos^2 \\beta = \\frac{1}{y}\\). Also, \\(\\tan^2 \\alpha = x - 1\\) and \\(\\tan^2 \\beta = y - 1\\). The expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{x^2}{y - 1} + \\frac{y^2}{x - 1}\n\\]\n\nTo find the minimum value, we apply the AM-GM inequality:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{x^2}{y - 1} + \\frac{y^2}{x - 1} \\geq 2 \\sqrt{\\frac{x^2}{y - 1} \\cdot \\frac{y^2}{x - 1}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= 2 \\sqrt{\\frac{x^2 y^2}{(y - 1)(x - 1)}}\n\\]\n\nTo simplify further, assume \\(x = y\\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{x^2}{x - 1} + \\frac{x^2}{x - 1} = 2 \\cdot \\frac{x^2}{x - 1}\n\\]\n\nLet \\(z = x - 1\\), so \\(x = z + 1\\). The expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cdot \\frac{(z + 1)^2}{z} = 2 \\left( z + 2 + \\frac{1}{z} \\right)\n\\]\n\nBy AM-GM, \\(z + \\frac{1}{z} \\geq 2\\), so:\n\n\\[\n2 \\left( z + 2 + \\frac{1}{z} \\right) \\geq 2(2 + 2) = 8\n\\]\n\nThus, the minimum value of the original expression is \\(\\boxed{8}\\).'
'To find the volume of the tetrahedron with vertices \\(A = (0,1,2)\\), \\(B = (3,0,1)\\), \\(C = (4,3,6)\\), and \\(D = (2,3,2)\\), we can use the formula for the volume of a tetrahedron given by four points \\((x_1, y_1, z_1)\\), \\((x_2, y_2, z_2)\\), \\((x_3, y_3, z_3)\\), and \\((x_4, y_4, z_4)\\):\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\nx_1 & y_1 & z_1 & 1 \\\\\nx_2 & y_2 & z_2 & 1 \\\\\nx_3 & y_3 & z_3 & 1 \\\\\nx_4 & y_4 & z_4 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the coordinates of the points \\(A\\), \\(B\\), \\(C\\), and \\(D\\) into the determinant, we have:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left| \\begin{vmatrix}\n0 & 1 & 2 & 1 \\\\\n3 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\\\\n4 & 3 & 6 & 1 \\\\\n2 & 3 & 2 & 1\n\\end{vmatrix} \\right|\n\\]\n\nTo compute this determinant, we expand along the first row:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{6} \\left( 0 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 & 1 \\\\ 3 & 6 & 1 \\\\ 3 & 2 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} - 1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 1 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 6 & 1 \\\\ 2 & 2 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} + 2 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 1 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} - 1 \\cdot \\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculating each of the 3x3 determinants:\n\n1. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 1 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 6 & 1 \\\\ 2 & 2 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} = 3(6 \\cdot 1 - 1 \\cdot 2) - 1(4 \\cdot 1 - 1 \\cdot 2) + 1(4 \\cdot 2 - 6 \\cdot 2)\\)\n   \\[\n   = 3(6 - 2) - 1(4 - 2) + 1(8 - 12) = 3 \\cdot 4 - 2 - 4 = 12 - 2 - 4 = 6\n   \\]\n\n2. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 1 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 1 \\end{vmatrix} = 3(3 \\cdot 1 - 1 \\cdot 3) - 0(4 \\cdot 1 - 1 \\cdot 2) + 1(4 \\cdot 3 - 3 \\cdot 2)\\)\n   \\[\n   = 3(3 - 3) + 0 + 1(12 - 6) = 0 + 6 = 6\n   \\]\n\n3. \\(\\begin{vmatrix} 3 & 0 & 1 \\\\ 4 & 3 & 6 \\\\ 2 & 3 & 2 \\end{vmatrix} = 3(3 \\cdot 2 - 6 \\cdot 3) - 0(4 \\cdot 2 - 6 \\cdot 2) + 1(4 \\cdot 3 - 3 \\cdot 2)\\)\n   \\[\n   = 3(6 - 18) + 0 + 1(12 - 6) = 3(-12) + 6 = -36 + 6 = -30\n   \\]\n\nSubstituting back into the volume formula:\n\n\\[\nV = \\frac{1}{6} \\left( -1 \\cdot 6 + 2 \\cdot 6 - 1 \\cdot (-30) \\right) = \\frac{1}{6} \\left( -6 + 12 + 30 \\right) = \\frac{1}{6} \\times 36 = 6\n\\]\n\nThus, the volume of the tetrahedron is \\(\\boxed{6}\\).'
'To solve \\(\\tan \\left( 2 \\arctan \\frac{1}{5} - \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right)\\), we can use the tangent subtraction formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a - b) = \\frac{\\tan a - \\tan b}{1 + \\tan a \\tan b}\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = 2 \\arctan \\frac{1}{5}\\) and \\(b = \\frac{\\pi}{4}\\). We know that \\(\\tan \\frac{\\pi}{4} = 1\\).\n\nFirst, we need to find \\(\\tan(2 \\arctan \\frac{1}{5})\\). Using the double angle formula for tangent:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(2x) = \\frac{2 \\tan x}{1 - \\tan^2 x}\n\\]\n\nLet \\(x = \\arctan \\frac{1}{5}\\), so \\(\\tan x = \\frac{1}{5}\\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(2x) = \\frac{2 \\cdot \\frac{1}{5}}{1 - \\left(\\frac{1}{5}\\right)^2} = \\frac{\\frac{2}{5}}{1 - \\frac{1}{25}} = \\frac{\\frac{2}{5}}{\\frac{24}{25}} = \\frac{2}{5} \\cdot \\frac{25}{24} = \\frac{10}{24} = \\frac{5}{12}\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute back into the tangent subtraction formula:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\left( 2 \\arctan \\frac{1}{5} - \\frac{\\pi}{4} \\right) = \\frac{\\frac{5}{12} - 1}{1 + \\frac{5}{12} \\cdot 1}\n\\]\n\nSimplify the expression:\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{\\frac{5}{12} - \\frac{12}{12}}{1 + \\frac{5}{12}} = \\frac{\\frac{-7}{12}}{\\frac{17}{12}} = \\frac{-7}{12} \\cdot \\frac{12}{17} = \\frac{-7}{17}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{-7}{17}}\\).'
"To solve for the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\), we need to evaluate each of the cross products in the given expression and then solve for \\(\\mathbf{v}\\).\n\nLet's start with the first term:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i}]\n\\]\nUsing the vector triple product identity \\(\\mathbf{a} \\times (\\mathbf{b} \\times \\mathbf{c}) = (\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{c}) \\mathbf{b} - (\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}) \\mathbf{c}\\), we have:\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i} = (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{i}) - (\\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{i})\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{i} = -\\mathbf{k}\n\\]\nSo,\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i} = \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{i} + \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\nNow, apply the identity:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times [\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{i} + \\mathbf{k}] = \\mathbf{i} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{i}) + \\mathbf{i} \\times \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{i}) = (\\mathbf{i} \\cdot \\mathbf{i}) \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{i} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{i} = \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{i} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{i}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times \\mathbf{k} = -\\mathbf{j}\n\\]\nThus,\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{j}) \\times \\mathbf{i}] = \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{i} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{i} - \\mathbf{j}\n\\]\n\nNext, consider the second term:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{j} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j}]\n\\]\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j} = \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{j} - \\mathbf{k} \\times \\mathbf{j}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{k} \\times \\mathbf{j} = -\\mathbf{i}\n\\]\nSo,\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j} = \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{i}\n\\]\nNow, apply the identity:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{j} \\times [\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{i}] = \\mathbf{j} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{j}) + \\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{i}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{j} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{j}) = (\\mathbf{j} \\cdot \\mathbf{j}) \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{j} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{j} = \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{j} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{j}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{j} \\times \\mathbf{i} = \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\nThus,\n\\[\n\\mathbf{j} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{k}) \\times \\mathbf{j}] = \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{j} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\n\nFinally, consider the third term:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{k} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k}]\n\\]\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k} = \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{k} - \\mathbf{i} \\times \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{i} \\times \\mathbf{k} = -\\mathbf{j}\n\\]\nSo,\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k} = \\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{k} + \\mathbf{j}\n\\]\nNow, apply the identity:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{k} \\times [\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{k} + \\mathbf{j}] = \\mathbf{k} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{k}) + \\mathbf{k} \\times \\mathbf{j}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{k} \\times (\\mathbf{v} \\times \\mathbf{k}) = (\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{k}) \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{k} = \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{k} \\times \\mathbf{j} = \\mathbf{i}\n\\]\nThus,\n\\[\n\\mathbf{k} \\times [(\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{i}) \\times \\mathbf{k}] = \\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{k} + \\mathbf{i}\n\\]\n\nNow, sum all three terms:\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{i} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{i} - \\mathbf{j}) + (\\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{j} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{k}) + (\\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{k} + \\mathbf{i}) = \\mathbf{0}\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\\[\n3\\mathbf{v} - (\\mathbf{i} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{i} - (\\mathbf{j} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{j} - (\\mathbf{k} \\cdot \\mathbf{v}) \\mathbf{k} + \\mathbf{i} - \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{k} = \\mathbf{0}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n3\\mathbf{v} - \\mathbf{v} + \\mathbf{i} - \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{k} = \\mathbf{0}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\n2\\mathbf{v} + \\mathbf{i} - \\mathbf{j} + \\mathbf{k} = \\mathbf{0}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n\\[\n2\\mathbf{v} = -\\mathbf{i} + \\mathbf{j} - \\mathbf{k}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} = -\\frac{1}{2}\\mathbf{i} + \\frac{1}{2}\\mathbf{j} - \\frac{1}{2}\\mathbf{k}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\left(-\\frac{1}{2}, \\frac{1}{2}, -\\frac{1}{2}\\right)}\n\\]"
'To find the projection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto a vector \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 1 \\cdot 8 + (-2) \\cdot 1 = 8 - 2 = 6\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 8 \\cdot 8 + 1 \\cdot 1 = 64 + 1 = 65\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the projection:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{6}{65} \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{48}{65} \\\\ \\frac{6}{65} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the projection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{48}{65} \\\\ \\frac{6}{65} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}\\) in triangle \\(ABC\\) where \\(O\\) is the circumcenter.\n\nFirst, let's understand the geometric setup. The circumcenter \\(O\\) of a triangle is equidistant from all three vertices \\(A\\), \\(B\\), and \\(C\\). Therefore, \\(OA = OB = OC = R\\), where \\(R\\) is the circumradius of the triangle.\n\nThe vector \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) is simply the position vector of point \\(A\\) with respect to \\(O\\). Since \\(O\\) is the circumcenter, \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) is a vector of length \\(R\\) pointing from \\(O\\) to \\(A\\).\n\nThe vector \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\) is the vector from \\(B\\) to \\(C\\). We need to find the dot product of \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) with \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\).\n\nSince \\(O\\) is the circumcenter, the vector \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) is perpendicular to the line \\(BC\\) if \\(A\\) is on the circle and \\(B\\) and \\(C\\) are points on the circle. This is because the perpendicular from the center of a circle to a chord bisects the chord, and in this case, since \\(A\\) is a vertex, the perpendicularity implies that \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) is perpendicular to \\(\\overrightarrow{BC}\\).\n\nTherefore, the dot product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\cdot \\overrightarrow{BC}\\) is zero because the vectors are perpendicular.\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{0}\\)."
"To solve the equation \\(\\cos^{3}3x+\\cos^{3}5x= 8\\cos^{3}4x\\cos^{3}x\\), we first explore potential values of \\(x\\) within the given range \\(100^\\circ < x < 200^\\circ\\).\n\nLet's consider the symmetry and periodicity of the cosine function. The cosine function has a period of \\(360^\\circ\\), and we are looking for solutions in the interval \\(100^\\circ < x < 200^\\circ\\).\n\nWe can try specific values of \\(x\\) that might simplify the equation. A natural choice is to test \\(x = 120^\\circ\\), \\(x = 135^\\circ\\), and \\(x = 150^\\circ\\), as these angles are often involved in trigonometric identities.\n\n1. **Testing \\(x = 120^\\circ\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos 3x = \\cos 360^\\circ = 1, \\quad \\cos 5x = \\cos 600^\\circ = \\cos 240^\\circ = -\\frac{1}{2}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\cos 4x = \\cos 480^\\circ = \\cos 120^\\circ = -\\frac{1}{2}, \\quad \\cos x = \\cos 120^\\circ = -\\frac{1}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting these into the equation:\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos^3 3x + \\cos^3 5x = 1^3 + \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3 = 1 - \\frac{1}{8} = \\frac{7}{8}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   8 \\cos^3 4x \\cos^3 x = 8 \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3 \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3 = 8 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{8}\\right) \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{8}\\right) = \\frac{1}{8}\n   \\]\n\n   The values do not match, so \\(x = 120^\\circ\\) is not a solution.\n\n2. **Testing \\(x = 135^\\circ\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos 3x = \\cos 405^\\circ = \\cos 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}, \\quad \\cos 5x = \\cos 675^\\circ = \\cos 315^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\cos 4x = \\cos 540^\\circ = \\cos 180^\\circ = -1, \\quad \\cos x = \\cos 135^\\circ = -\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting these into the equation:\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos^3 3x + \\cos^3 5x = \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)^3 + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)^3 = 2 \\cdot \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)^3 = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2}}{8} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   8 \\cos^3 4x \\cos^3 x = 8 \\cdot (-1)^3 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)^3 = 8 \\cdot (-1) \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{8}\\right) = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\n   \\]\n\n   The values match, so \\(x = 135^\\circ\\) is a solution.\n\n3. **Testing \\(x = 150^\\circ\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos 3x = \\cos 450^\\circ = \\cos 90^\\circ = 0, \\quad \\cos 5x = \\cos 750^\\circ = \\cos 30^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   \\cos 4x = \\cos 600^\\circ = \\cos 240^\\circ = -\\frac{1}{2}, \\quad \\cos x = \\cos 150^\\circ = -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n   \\]\n\n   Substituting these into the equation:\n\n   \\[\n   \\cos^3 3x + \\cos^3 5x = 0^3 + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^3 = \\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{8}\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   8 \\cos^3 4x \\cos^3 x = 8 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^3 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^3 = 8 \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{1}{8}\\right) \\cdot \\left(-\\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{8}\\right) = \\frac{3\\sqrt{3}}{8}\n   \\]\n\n   The values match, so \\(x = 150^\\circ\\) is a solution.\n\nThus, the solutions are \\(x = 135^\\circ\\) and \\(x = 150^\\circ\\). The sum of these values is:\n\n\\[\n135^\\circ + 150^\\circ = 285^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of the values of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{285}\\)."
'To find the set of vectors \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) such that the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to determine when the projection is zero. The projection of \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}}{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe dot product \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(2x + y\\), and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 4 + 1 = 5\\). Therefore, the projection is:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{2x + y}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nFor this projection to be \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2x + y}{5} = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n2x + y = 0.\n\\]\n\nThus, the set of vectors \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) that satisfy this condition lie on the line described by the equation:\n\n\\[\ny = -2x.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the equation of the line is \\(y = -2x\\).'
"To solve the problem, we start by analyzing the given equation:\n\n\\[ z^6 + z^4 + z^2 + 1 = 0. \\]\n\nWe can factor this equation by recognizing it as a sum of powers of \\( z^2 \\):\n\n\\[ (z^2)^3 + (z^2)^2 + z^2 + 1 = 0. \\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[ (z^2 + 1)^3 = 0. \\]\n\nHowever, this is incorrect. Let's try another approach. Notice that:\n\n\\[ z^6 + z^4 + z^2 + 1 = (z^2 + 1)(z^4 + 1) = 0. \\]\n\nThus, the roots of the equation are the solutions to:\n\n1. \\( z^2 + 1 = 0 \\)\n2. \\( z^4 + 1 = 0 \\)\n\nSolving \\( z^2 + 1 = 0 \\), we get:\n\n\\[ z^2 = -1 \\]\n\\[ z = i, -i. \\]\n\nSolving \\( z^4 + 1 = 0 \\), we get:\n\n\\[ z^4 = -1 \\]\n\\[ z = e^{i\\pi/4}, e^{3i\\pi/4}, e^{5i\\pi/4}, e^{7i\\pi/4}. \\]\n\nThese roots are the 8th roots of unity, specifically the odd ones:\n\n\\[ z = e^{i\\pi/4}, e^{3i\\pi/4}, e^{5i\\pi/4}, e^{7i\\pi/4}. \\]\n\nNow, we have the roots \\( i, -i, e^{i\\pi/4}, e^{3i\\pi/4}, e^{5i\\pi/4}, e^{7i\\pi/4} \\).\n\nThese roots form a regular octagon in the complex plane, but we only consider the vertices corresponding to the roots of the given equation, which are \\( i, -i, e^{i\\pi/4}, e^{3i\\pi/4}, e^{5i\\pi/4}, e^{7i\\pi/4} \\).\n\nThe vertices of the polygon are:\n\n- \\( i = e^{i\\pi/2} \\)\n- \\( -i = e^{3i\\pi/2} \\)\n- \\( e^{i\\pi/4} \\)\n- \\( e^{3i\\pi/4} \\)\n- \\( e^{5i\\pi/4} \\)\n- \\( e^{7i\\pi/4} \\)\n\nThese points are evenly spaced on the unit circle, forming a regular hexagon. The side length of a regular hexagon inscribed in a unit circle is the distance between consecutive vertices, which is:\n\n\\[ |e^{i\\theta} - e^{i(\\theta + \\pi/3)}| = |e^{i\\theta}(1 - e^{i\\pi/3})| = |1 - e^{i\\pi/3}|. \\]\n\nCalculating this distance:\n\n\\[ 1 - e^{i\\pi/3} = 1 - \\left(\\frac{1}{2} + i\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right) = \\frac{1}{2} - i\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}. \\]\n\nThe magnitude is:\n\n\\[ \\left|\\frac{1}{2} - i\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right| = \\sqrt{\\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{4} + \\frac{3}{4}} = \\sqrt{1} = 1. \\]\n\nThus, the side length of the hexagon is 1. Since there are 6 sides, the sum of the squares of the side lengths is:\n\n\\[ 6 \\times 1^2 = 6. \\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of the squares of the side lengths of the polygon is \\(\\boxed{6}\\)."
'In triangle \\(ABC\\), we know that the sum of the angles is \\(180^\\circ\\), so \\(A + B + C = 180^\\circ\\). Therefore, \\(\\sin C = \\sin(180^\\circ - A - B) = \\sin(A + B)\\).\n\nWe can use the sine addition formula:\n\\[\n\\sin(A + B) = \\sin A \\cos B + \\cos A \\sin B.\n\\]\n\nWe are given \\(\\sin A = \\frac{3}{5}\\) and \\(\\sin B = \\frac{24}{25}\\). We need to find \\(\\cos A\\) and \\(\\cos B\\).\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we find:\n\\[\n\\cos A = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 A} = \\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{3}{5}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{1 - \\frac{9}{25}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{16}{25}} = \\frac{4}{5}.\n\\]\n\nSimilarly,\n\\[\n\\cos B = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 B} = \\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{24}{25}\\right)^2} = \\sqrt{1 - \\frac{576}{625}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{49}{625}} = \\frac{7}{25}.\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these values into the sine addition formula:\n\\[\n\\sin(A + B) = \\left(\\frac{3}{5}\\right)\\left(\\frac{7}{25}\\right) + \\left(\\frac{4}{5}\\right)\\left(\\frac{24}{25}\\right).\n\\]\n\nCalculate each term:\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{5} \\cdot \\frac{7}{25} = \\frac{21}{125},\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{4}{5} \\cdot \\frac{24}{25} = \\frac{96}{125}.\n\\]\n\nAdd these results:\n\\[\n\\sin(A + B) = \\frac{21}{125} + \\frac{96}{125} = \\frac{117}{125}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the possible value of \\(\\sin C\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{117}{125}}\\).'
'To find \\(\\det (\\mathbf{7A})\\), we use the property of determinants that states \\(\\det (c\\mathbf{A}) = c^n \\det (\\mathbf{A})\\), where \\(c\\) is a scalar and \\(n\\) is the order of the square matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\).\n\nAssuming \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is an \\(n \\times n\\) matrix, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{7A}) = 7^n \\det (\\mathbf{A})\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\det (\\mathbf{A}) = -1\\), we substitute this into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{7A}) = 7^n \\times (-1) = -7^n\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\det (\\mathbf{7A}) = -7^n\\), where \\(n\\) is the order of the matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the rotation matrix that transforms the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 8 \\end{pmatrix}\\). Once we have the rotation matrix, we can apply it to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to find the resulting vector.\n\nFirst, let's find the angle of rotation. The angle \\(\\theta\\) between two vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x_1 \\\\ y_1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x_2 \\\\ y_2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) can be found using the dot product formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{x_1 x_2 + y_1 y_2}{\\sqrt{x_1^2 + y_1^2} \\sqrt{x_2^2 + y_2^2}}\n\\]\n\nFor the vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 8 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{(-4)(1) + (7)(8)}{\\sqrt{(-4)^2 + 7^2} \\sqrt{1^2 + 8^2}} = \\frac{-4 + 56}{\\sqrt{16 + 49} \\sqrt{1 + 64}} = \\frac{52}{\\sqrt{65} \\cdot \\sqrt{65}} = \\frac{52}{65}\n\\]\n\nNow, let's find the sine of the angle using the cross product (in 2D, the cross product magnitude is the determinant):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\frac{-4 \\cdot 8 - 7 \\cdot 1}{\\sqrt{65} \\cdot \\sqrt{65}} = \\frac{-32 - 7}{65} = \\frac{-39}{65}\n\\]\n\nThe rotation matrix \\(R\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta & -\\sin \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{52}{65} & \\frac{39}{65} \\\\ -\\frac{39}{65} & \\frac{52}{65} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, apply this rotation matrix to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\n\\[\nR \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{52}{65} & \\frac{39}{65} \\\\ -\\frac{39}{65} & \\frac{52}{65} \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the resulting vector:\n\n\\[\n= \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{52}{65} \\cdot (-1) + \\frac{39}{65} \\cdot 3 \\\\ -\\frac{39}{65} \\cdot (-1) + \\frac{52}{65} \\cdot 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n= \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{52}{65} + \\frac{117}{65} \\\\ \\frac{39}{65} + \\frac{156}{65} \\end{pmatrix}\n= \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{65}{65} \\\\ \\frac{195}{65} \\end{pmatrix}\n= \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the rotation takes the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\)."
"To solve the problem, we start with the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{3} \\cos \\theta - \\sin \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nWe want to find \\(\\sqrt{3} \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\). To do this, we can use the identity for the cosine of a sum:\n\n\\[\na \\cos \\theta + b \\sin \\theta = \\sqrt{a^2 + b^2} \\cos(\\theta - \\phi),\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\cos \\phi = \\frac{a}{\\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}}\\) and \\(\\sin \\phi = \\frac{b}{\\sqrt{a^2 + b^2}}\\).\n\nFirst, let's rewrite the given equation in this form:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{3} \\cos \\theta - \\sin \\theta = \\sqrt{(\\sqrt{3})^2 + (-1)^2} \\cos(\\theta - \\phi).\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\(\\sqrt{(\\sqrt{3})^2 + (-1)^2}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{3^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{3 + 1} = \\sqrt{4} = 2.\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cos(\\theta - \\phi) = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\cos(\\theta - \\phi)\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos(\\theta - \\phi) = \\frac{1}{6}.\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\sqrt{3} \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\). Using the same identity:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{3} \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta = \\sqrt{(\\sqrt{3})^2 + 1^2} \\cos(\\theta + \\phi).\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\(\\sqrt{(\\sqrt{3})^2 + 1^2}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{3^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{3 + 1} = \\sqrt{4} = 2.\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cos(\\theta + \\phi).\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\cos(\\theta - \\phi) = \\frac{1}{6}\\), we use the identity for cosine of sum and difference:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(\\theta + \\phi) = \\cos(\\theta - \\phi).\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cos(\\theta + \\phi) = \\frac{1}{6}\\).\n\nFinally, the expression \\(\\sqrt{3} \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\) becomes:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cos(\\theta + \\phi) = 2 \\times \\frac{1}{6} = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\sqrt{3} \\sin \\theta + \\cos \\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{3}}\\)."
