'To solve the problem, we need to compute the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos^2 10^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 20^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 40^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nWe will use trigonometric identities to simplify this expression. First, recall the identity for the secant and cosecant functions:\n\n\\[\n\\sec^2 \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\cos^2 \\theta}, \\quad \\csc^2 \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\sin^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sec^2 10^\\circ + \\csc^2 20^\\circ + \\csc^2 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nWe will use the identity for the tangent and cotangent functions:\n\n\\[\n\\sec^2 \\theta = 1 + \\tan^2 \\theta, \\quad \\csc^2 \\theta = 1 + \\cot^2 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sec^2 10^\\circ = 1 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\csc^2 20^\\circ = 1 + \\cot^2 20^\\circ,\n\\]\n\\[\n\\csc^2 40^\\circ = 1 + \\cot^2 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the expression, we get:\n\n\\[\n(1 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ) + (1 + \\cot^2 20^\\circ) + (1 + \\cot^2 40^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n3 + \\tan^2 10^\\circ + \\cot^2 20^\\circ + \\cot^2 40^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNow, we use the identity for cotangent in terms of tangent:\n\n\\[\n\\cot \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\tan \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\cot^2 20^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 20^\\circ}, \\quad \\cot^2 40^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 40^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nWe will use the triple angle identity for tangent:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 3\\theta = \\frac{3\\tan \\theta - \\tan^3 \\theta}{1 - 3\\tan^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\theta = 10^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{3\\tan 10^\\circ - \\tan^3 10^\\circ}{1 - 3\\tan^2 10^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\tan 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we equate:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{3\\tan 10^\\circ - \\tan^3 10^\\circ}{1 - 3\\tan^2 10^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nSolving this equation is complex, but it is known that:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 10^\\circ \\cdot \\tan 20^\\circ \\cdot \\tan 40^\\circ = \\tan 60^\\circ = \\sqrt{3}.\n\\]\n\nThus, using this identity, we can find:\n\n\\[\n\\tan^2 10^\\circ + \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 20^\\circ} + \\frac{1}{\\tan^2 40^\\circ} = 9.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the original expression simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n3 + 9 = 12.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{12}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we start by using the given information and trigonometric identities. We know that \\(\\alpha + \\beta + \\gamma = \\pi\\), which implies \\(\\gamma = \\pi - (\\alpha + \\beta)\\).\n\nWe are given that \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\). Since \\(\\csc \\frac{\\pi}{3} = \\frac{1}{\\sin \\frac{\\pi}{3}} = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\). Using the identity for \\(\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta)\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta) = \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\gamma = \\pi - (\\alpha + \\beta)\\), we have \\(\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos(\\alpha + \\beta)\\). Therefore:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta)\n\\]\n\nWe can use the identity \\(\\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta = \\frac{\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta}{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}\\) to express \\(\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\alpha \\sin \\beta = \\tan \\alpha \\tan \\beta \\cdot \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}} \\cdot \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta\n\\]\n\nSubstituting this into the expression for \\(\\cos \\gamma\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -(\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta - \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}} \\cdot \\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta (1 - \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{3}})\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\gamma = -\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3} - 2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)\n\\]\n\nNow, we find \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma} = \\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{-\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3} - 2}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= -\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{3} - 2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\frac{\\cos \\alpha \\cos \\beta}{\\cos \\gamma}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{3} - 2}}\\).'
'To find the \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\), we need to determine the effect of \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) on the standard basis vectors \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nThis tells us that the first column of \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nAlso given:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nThis tells us that the second column of \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -7 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 2 \\\\ 0 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 3 & 2 \\\\ 0 & -7 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the determinant \\( D_n \\) of the matrix \\( M_n \\) and then evaluate the series \\(\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n+1}\\).\n\n### Step 1: Find the determinant \\( D_n \\)\n\nThe matrix \\( M_n \\) is a tridiagonal matrix with the main diagonal entries as 10 and the entries immediately above and below the main diagonal as 3. The determinant of such a matrix can be found using a recurrence relation.\n\nFor a tridiagonal matrix of the form:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{bmatrix}\na & b & 0 & \\cdots & 0 \\\\\nb & a & b & \\cdots & 0 \\\\\n0 & b & a & \\cdots & 0 \\\\\n\\vdots & \\vdots & \\vdots & \\ddots & \\vdots \\\\\n0 & 0 & 0 & b & a\n\\end{bmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe determinant \\( D_n \\) satisfies the recurrence relation:\n\n\\[\nD_n = aD_{n-1} - b^2D_{n-2}\n\\]\n\nFor our matrix \\( M_n \\), \\( a = 10 \\) and \\( b = 3 \\). Thus, the recurrence relation becomes:\n\n\\[\nD_n = 10D_{n-1} - 9D_{n-2}\n\\]\n\nWe need initial conditions to solve this recurrence:\n\n- For \\( n = 1 \\), the matrix is \\([10]\\), so \\( D_1 = 10 \\).\n- For \\( n = 2 \\), the matrix is \\(\\begin{bmatrix} 10 & 3 \\\\ 3 & 10 \\end{bmatrix}\\), so \\( D_2 = 10 \\cdot 10 - 3 \\cdot 3 = 100 - 9 = 91 \\).\n\nNow, we can compute the first few terms:\n\n- \\( D_3 = 10 \\cdot 91 - 9 \\cdot 10 = 910 - 90 = 820 \\)\n- \\( D_4 = 10 \\cdot 820 - 9 \\cdot 91 = 8200 - 819 = 7381 \\)\n\n### Step 2: Evaluate the series \\(\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n+1}\\)\n\nWe observe that the sequence \\( D_n \\) grows rapidly. Let's compute the first few terms of \\( 8D_n + 1 \\):\n\n- \\( 8D_1 + 1 = 8 \\cdot 10 + 1 = 81 \\)\n- \\( 8D_2 + 1 = 8 \\cdot 91 + 1 = 729 \\)\n- \\( 8D_3 + 1 = 8 \\cdot 820 + 1 = 6561 \\)\n- \\( 8D_4 + 1 = 8 \\cdot 7381 + 1 = 59049 \\)\n\nNotice that these are powers of 9:\n\n- \\( 81 = 9^2 \\)\n- \\( 729 = 9^3 \\)\n- \\( 6561 = 9^4 \\)\n- \\( 59049 = 9^5 \\)\n\nThus, we conjecture that \\( 8D_n + 1 = 9^{n+1} \\).\n\nTherefore, the series becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{8D_n+1} = \\sum_{n=1}^{\\infty} \\frac{1}{9^{n+1}}\n\\]\n\nThis is a geometric series with the first term \\( \\frac{1}{9^2} \\) and common ratio \\( \\frac{1}{9} \\). The sum of an infinite geometric series \\( \\sum_{n=0}^{\\infty} ar^n \\) is given by \\( \\frac{a}{1-r} \\), where \\( |r| < 1 \\).\n\nHere, \\( a = \\frac{1}{81} \\) and \\( r = \\frac{1}{9} \\), so the sum is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\frac{1}{81}}{1 - \\frac{1}{9}} = \\frac{\\frac{1}{81}}{\\frac{8}{9}} = \\frac{1}{81} \\cdot \\frac{9}{8} = \\frac{1}{72}\n\\]\n\nThus, the sum of the series is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{72}}\\)."
"To solve the equation \\(\\cos 3x = 1\\) for \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\), we need to find the values of \\(x\\) such that \\(3x\\) is an angle where the cosine is equal to 1. The cosine function equals 1 at integer multiples of \\(2\\pi\\), so we have:\n\n\\[ 3x = 2k\\pi \\]\n\nwhere \\(k\\) is an integer. Solving for \\(x\\), we get:\n\n\\[ x = \\frac{2k\\pi}{3} \\]\n\nWe need to find all such \\(x\\) within the interval \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\). Let's determine the possible values of \\(k\\):\n\n1. For \\(k = 0\\), \\(x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 0 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = 0\\).\n2. For \\(k = 1\\), \\(x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 1 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n3. For \\(k = 2\\), \\(x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 2 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = \\frac{4\\pi}{3}\\).\n4. For \\(k = 3\\), \\(x = \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot \\pi}{3} = 2\\pi\\).\n\nFor \\(k = 4\\) or higher, \\(x\\) would exceed \\(2\\pi\\), so we stop here.\n\nThus, the solutions for \\(x\\) in the interval \\(0 \\le x \\le 2\\pi\\) are:\n\n\\[ x = 0, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{4\\pi}{3}, 2\\pi \\]\n\nThe answer is \\(0, \\frac{2\\pi}{3}, \\frac{4\\pi}{3}, 2\\pi\\)."
'Given the equation \\(\\tan \\theta \\sec \\theta = 1\\), we can express \\(\\tan \\theta\\) and \\(\\sec \\theta\\) in terms of \\(\\sin \\theta\\) and \\(\\cos \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta}, \\quad \\sec \\theta = \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta}\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\cos \\theta} = 1\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos^2 \\theta} = 1\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\), we substitute \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n(\\cos^2 \\theta)^2 + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos^4 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\n\\]\n\nLet \\(x = \\cos^2 \\theta\\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + x - 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nSolving this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 + 4}}{2} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{5}}{2}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(x = \\cos^2 \\theta\\) must be between 0 and 1, we take the positive root:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\) and \\(\\sin \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\).\n\nNow, we need to find:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 + \\sin \\theta}{1 - \\sin \\theta} - \\frac{1 - \\sin \\theta}{1 + \\sin \\theta}\n\\]\n\nLet \\(a = \\sin \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\). Then:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 + a}{1 - a} - \\frac{1 - a}{1 + a}\n\\]\n\nThis can be simplified using the identity for the difference of fractions:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{(1 + a)^2 - (1 - a)^2}{(1 - a)(1 + a)}\n\\]\n\nThe denominator simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n(1 - a)(1 + a) = 1 - a^2\n\\]\n\nThe numerator simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n(1 + a)^2 - (1 - a)^2 = (1 + 2a + a^2) - (1 - 2a + a^2) = 4a\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4a}{1 - a^2}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(a = \\sin \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\) and \\(a^2 = \\cos^2 \\theta = \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n1 - a^2 = 1 - \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2} = \\frac{3 - \\sqrt{5}}{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the expression evaluates to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4 \\cdot \\frac{-1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}}{\\frac{3 - \\sqrt{5}}{2}} = \\frac{4(-1 + \\sqrt{5})}{3 - \\sqrt{5}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying further, we multiply numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{4(-1 + \\sqrt{5})(3 + \\sqrt{5})}{(3 - \\sqrt{5})(3 + \\sqrt{5})} = \\frac{4(-3 - \\sqrt{5} + 3\\sqrt{5} + 5)}{9 - 5} = \\frac{4(2 + 2\\sqrt{5})}{4} = 2 + 2\\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the final answer is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{2 + 2\\sqrt{5}}\n\\]'
'To find the point the curve passes through at \\( t = 2 \\), we need to substitute \\( t = 2 \\) into the parameterized equations for \\( x \\) and \\( y \\).\n\nThe parameterized equations are:\n\\[\nx = t^3 + 7\n\\]\n\\[\ny = -3t^2 - 6t - 5\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( t = 2 \\) into the equation for \\( x \\):\n\\[\nx = (2)^3 + 7 = 8 + 7 = 15\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( t = 2 \\) into the equation for \\( y \\):\n\\[\ny = -3(2)^2 - 6(2) - 5 = -3(4) - 12 - 5 = -12 - 12 - 5 = -29\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the point the curve passes through at \\( t = 2 \\) is \\((15, -29)\\).'
"To find the distance from the origin to the plane passing through the points \\((-5,0,0)\\), \\((0,2,0)\\), and \\((0,0,-7)\\), we first need to determine the equation of the plane.\n\nThe general equation of a plane is given by:\n\\[ ax + by + cz = d. \\]\n\nTo find the coefficients \\(a\\), \\(b\\), and \\(c\\), we can use the normal vector to the plane, which can be found by taking the cross product of two vectors that lie on the plane. Let's consider the vectors formed by the given points:\n- \\(\\mathbf{v}_1 = (0,2,0) - (-5,0,0) = (5,2,0)\\),\n- \\(\\mathbf{v}_2 = (0,0,-7) - (-5,0,0) = (5,0,-7)\\).\n\nThe cross product \\(\\mathbf{n} = \\mathbf{v}_1 \\times \\mathbf{v}_2\\) gives us a normal vector to the plane:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{n} = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 5 & 2 & 0 \\\\ 5 & 0 & -7 \\end{vmatrix} = \\mathbf{i}(2 \\cdot (-7) - 0 \\cdot 0) - \\mathbf{j}(5 \\cdot (-7) - 0 \\cdot 5) + \\mathbf{k}(5 \\cdot 0 - 2 \\cdot 5).\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i}(-14) - \\mathbf{j}(-35) + \\mathbf{k}(-10) = (-14, 35, -10).\n\\]\n\nThus, the normal vector is \\((-14, 35, -10)\\), and the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n-14x + 35y - 10z = d.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(d\\), we substitute one of the points, say \\((-5,0,0)\\), into the plane equation:\n\\[\n-14(-5) + 35(0) - 10(0) = d \\implies 70 = d.\n\\]\n\nSo, the equation of the plane is:\n\\[\n-14x + 35y - 10z = 70.\n\\]\n\nThe distance \\(D\\) from the origin \\((0,0,0)\\) to the plane is given by the formula:\n\\[\nD = \\frac{|ax_0 + by_0 + cz_0 - d|}{\\sqrt{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}},\n\\]\nwhere \\((x_0, y_0, z_0)\\) is the point from which we are measuring the distance, in this case, the origin.\n\nSubstituting the values, we get:\n\\[\nD = \\frac{|-14(0) + 35(0) - 10(0) - 70|}{\\sqrt{(-14)^2 + 35^2 + (-10)^2}} = \\frac{| -70 |}{\\sqrt{196 + 1225 + 100}} = \\frac{70}{\\sqrt{1521}}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sqrt{1521} = 39\\), the distance is:\n\\[\nD = \\frac{70}{39}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the distance from the origin to the plane is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{70}{39}}\\)."
'To simplify the expression \\(\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ + \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ\\), we can use trigonometric identities.\n\nFirst, consider the identity for the sum of angles:\n\\[\n\\sin A \\cos B = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(A + B) + \\sin(A - B)].\n\\]\n\nApplying this identity to \\(\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ\\):\n\\[\n\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(70^\\circ + 50^\\circ) + \\sin(70^\\circ - 50^\\circ)] = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 120^\\circ + \\sin 20^\\circ].\n\\]\n\nNext, consider \\(\\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ\\):\n\\[\n\\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin(260^\\circ + 280^\\circ) + \\sin(260^\\circ - 280^\\circ)] = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 540^\\circ + \\sin(-20^\\circ)].\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin 540^\\circ = \\sin(540^\\circ - 360^\\circ) = \\sin 180^\\circ = 0\\) and \\(\\sin(-20^\\circ) = -\\sin 20^\\circ\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [0 - \\sin 20^\\circ] = -\\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nNow, combine the two results:\n\\[\n\\sin 70^\\circ \\cos 50^\\circ + \\sin 260^\\circ \\cos 280^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} [\\sin 120^\\circ + \\sin 20^\\circ] - \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying further:\n\\[\n= \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 120^\\circ + \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ - \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 20^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} \\sin 120^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin 120^\\circ = \\sin(180^\\circ - 60^\\circ) = \\sin 60^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} \\sin 120^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{4}}\\).'
'To determine the value of \\( a \\) such that the two lines do not intersect, we need to check if the lines are parallel. If they are parallel, they will not intersect unless they are the same line, which is not the case here since they have different points.\n\nThe direction vector of the first line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and the direction vector of the second line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix}\\). For the lines to be parallel, these direction vectors must be scalar multiples of each other. Therefore, we need:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix} = k \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ 9 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nfor some scalar \\( k \\). This gives us the system of equations:\n\n1. \\( 4 = ka \\)\n2. \\( -6 = 9k \\)\n\nFrom the second equation, we solve for \\( k \\):\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{-6}{9} = -\\frac{2}{3}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( k = -\\frac{2}{3} \\) into the first equation:\n\n\\[\n4 = \\left(-\\frac{2}{3}\\right) a\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( a \\), we multiply both sides by \\(-\\frac{3}{2}\\):\n\n\\[\na = 4 \\times \\left(-\\frac{3}{2}\\right) = -6\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( a \\) that makes the lines parallel and therefore not intersect is \\( a = \\boxed{-6} \\).'
"To solve the problem, we start by using the identity \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\). Given \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{4}{7}\\), we can find \\(\\sin \\theta\\) as follows:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 \\theta = 1 - \\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\left(\\frac{4}{7}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{16}{49} = \\frac{33}{49}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin \\theta = \\pm \\sqrt{\\frac{33}{49}} = \\pm \\frac{\\sqrt{33}}{7}\\).\n\nNext, we need to evaluate the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nLet's simplify this expression. We find a common denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 + \\sin \\theta} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\sin \\theta} = \\frac{(1 - \\sin \\theta) + (1 + \\sin \\theta)}{(1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta)}.\n\\]\n\nThe numerator simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n1 - \\sin \\theta + 1 + \\sin \\theta = 2.\n\\]\n\nThe denominator is:\n\n\\[\n(1 + \\sin \\theta)(1 - \\sin \\theta) = 1 - \\sin^2 \\theta = \\cos^2 \\theta = \\left(\\frac{4}{7}\\right)^2 = \\frac{16}{49}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{\\frac{16}{49}} = 2 \\times \\frac{49}{16} = \\frac{98}{16} = \\frac{49}{8}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{49}{8}}\\)."
"To determine which parameterizations are valid for the line passing through the distinct vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), we need to ensure that each parameterization represents a line that includes both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nA line through \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) can be parameterized as \\(\\mathbf{a} + t(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a})\\) or equivalently \\(\\mathbf{b} + t(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\), where \\(t\\) is a parameter.\n\nLet's analyze each option:\n\n(A) \\(\\mathbf{a} + t (\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\nThis parameterization is equivalent to \\(\\mathbf{a} - t (\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a})\\), which is a valid line through \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\n(B) \\(\\frac{3}{4} \\mathbf{a} + t \\cdot \\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{4}\\)\n\nThis parameterization does not necessarily pass through both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) because it does not have the form \\(\\mathbf{a} + t(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a})\\) or \\(\\mathbf{b} + t(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\).\n\n(C) \\(\\frac{\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}}{2} + t \\cdot \\frac{\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}}{2}\\)\n\nThis parameterization is valid because it represents a line through the midpoint of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) with direction \\(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a}\\).\n\n(D) \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2 \\mathbf{b} + t (2 \\mathbf{b} - 3 \\mathbf{a})\\)\n\nThis parameterization does not necessarily pass through both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) because it does not have the form \\(\\mathbf{a} + t(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a})\\) or \\(\\mathbf{b} + t(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\).\n\n(E) \\(-\\mathbf{b} + t (\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\nThis parameterization does not necessarily pass through both \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) because it does not have the form \\(\\mathbf{a} + t(\\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a})\\) or \\(\\mathbf{b} + t(\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\).\n\n(F) \\(\\mathbf{b} + \\frac{t}{3} (2 \\mathbf{a} - 2 \\mathbf{b})\\)\n\nThis parameterization simplifies to \\(\\mathbf{b} + \\frac{2t}{3} (\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b})\\), which is a valid line through \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\).\n\nTherefore, the valid parameterizations are (A), (C), and (F). The answer is \\(\\boxed{\\text{A,C,F}}\\)."
'The phase shift of a sine function in the form \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) \\) is given by the formula \\(-\\frac{c}{b}\\).\n\nIn the given function \\( y = 2 \\sin \\left( x + \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\right) \\), we have:\n- \\( a = 2 \\)\n- \\( b = 1 \\)\n- \\( c = \\frac{\\pi}{3} \\)\n\nUsing the formula for phase shift:\n\\[\n\\text{Phase shift} = -\\frac{c}{b} = -\\frac{\\frac{\\pi}{3}}{1} = -\\frac{\\pi}{3}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the phase shift of the graph is \\(-\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).'
'The expression \\(\\cos^{-1} \\frac{1}{2}\\) represents the angle whose cosine is \\(\\frac{1}{2}\\). In the range of the inverse cosine function, which is \\(0 \\leq \\theta \\leq \\pi\\), the angle that satisfies \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{2}\\) is \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cos^{-1} \\frac{1}{2} = \\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\frac{\\pi}{3}\\).'
'To find the acute angle \\(\\theta\\) between the two lines, we need to find the angle between their direction vectors. The direction vector of the first line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), and the direction vector of the second line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe cosine of the angle \\(\\theta\\) between two vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\n\\]\n\nFirst, we calculate the dot product \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -1 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = 2(-1) + 1(1) = -2 + 1 = -1\n\\]\n\nNext, we calculate the magnitudes of the vectors \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\) and \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\\):\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{2^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{4 + 1} = \\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{(-1)^2 + 1^2} = \\sqrt{1 + 1} = \\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find \\(\\cos \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{5} \\cdot \\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\theta\\) is the acute angle, we take the absolute value:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\left|\\frac{-1}{\\sqrt{10}}\\right| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{10}}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the cosine of the acute angle \\(\\theta\\) formed by the two lines is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{\\sqrt{10}}}\\).'
"To find the equation of the plane that bisects the angle between the given planes and contains the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\), we can use the concept of angle bisector planes. The equation of the angle bisector plane between two planes \\(a_1x + b_1y + c_1z + d_1 = 0\\) and \\(a_2x + b_2y + c_2z + d_2 = 0\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{a_1x + b_1y + c_1z + d_1}{\\sqrt{a_1^2 + b_1^2 + c_1^2}} = \\pm \\frac{a_2x + b_2y + c_2z + d_2}{\\sqrt{a_2^2 + b_2^2 + c_2^2}}\n\\]\n\nFor the given planes \\(3x - 6y + 2z + 5 = 0\\) and \\(4x - 12y + 3z - 3 = 0\\), we have:\n\n- Plane 1: \\(a_1 = 3\\), \\(b_1 = -6\\), \\(c_1 = 2\\), \\(d_1 = 5\\)\n- Plane 2: \\(a_2 = 4\\), \\(b_2 = -12\\), \\(c_2 = 3\\), \\(d_2 = -3\\)\n\nFirst, calculate the magnitudes of the normal vectors:\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{a_1^2 + b_1^2 + c_1^2} = \\sqrt{3^2 + (-6)^2 + 2^2} = \\sqrt{9 + 36 + 4} = \\sqrt{49} = 7\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sqrt{a_2^2 + b_2^2 + c_2^2} = \\sqrt{4^2 + (-12)^2 + 3^2} = \\sqrt{16 + 144 + 9} = \\sqrt{169} = 13\n\\]\n\nThe equation of the angle bisector plane is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3x - 6y + 2z + 5}{7} = \\pm \\frac{4x - 12y + 3z - 3}{13}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two possible equations:\n\n1. \\(\\frac{3x - 6y + 2z + 5}{7} = \\frac{4x - 12y + 3z - 3}{13}\\)\n2. \\(\\frac{3x - 6y + 2z + 5}{7} = -\\frac{4x - 12y + 3z - 3}{13}\\)\n\nLet's solve the first equation:\n\n\\[\n13(3x - 6y + 2z + 5) = 7(4x - 12y + 3z - 3)\n\\]\n\nExpanding both sides:\n\n\\[\n39x - 78y + 26z + 65 = 28x - 84y + 21z - 21\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms:\n\n\\[\n39x - 28x - 78y + 84y + 26z - 21z = -21 - 65\n\\]\n\n\\[\n11x + 6y + 5z = -86\n\\]\n\nThe equation of the first bisector plane is:\n\n\\[\n11x + 6y + 5z + 86 = 0\n\\]\n\nNow, let's check if this plane contains the point \\((-5, -1, -5)\\):\n\nSubstitute \\(x = -5\\), \\(y = -1\\), \\(z = -5\\) into the equation:\n\n\\[\n11(-5) + 6(-1) + 5(-5) + 86 = -55 - 6 - 25 + 86 = 0\n\\]\n\nThe point satisfies the equation, so the correct bisector plane is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{11x + 6y + 5z + 86 = 0}\n\\]"
'To find the inverse of a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Inverse} = \\frac{1}{ad - bc} \\begin{pmatrix} d & -b \\\\ -c & a \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nprovided that the determinant \\(ad - bc \\neq 0\\).\n\nFor the given matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we have:\n\n- \\(a = 5\\), \\(b = -4\\), \\(c = 0\\), \\(d = 1\\).\n\nThe determinant is:\n\n\\[\nad - bc = (5)(1) - (-4)(0) = 5 - 0 = 5\n\\]\n\nSince the determinant is not zero, the inverse exists. Now, we apply the formula for the inverse:\n\n\\[\n\\text{Inverse} = \\frac{1}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 4 \\\\ 0 & 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} & \\frac{4}{5} \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the inverse of the matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 & -4 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} & \\frac{4}{5} \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
'To find the sum of the two matrices, we add the corresponding elements from each matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 5 \\\\ -2 & 4 \\end{pmatrix} + \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & -3 \\\\ 8 & -5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1+0 & 5+(-3) \\\\ -2+8 & 4+(-5) \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculating each element:\n\n- First row, first column: \\(1 + 0 = 1\\)\n- First row, second column: \\(5 + (-3) = 2\\)\n- Second row, first column: \\(-2 + 8 = 6\\)\n- Second row, second column: \\(4 + (-5) = -1\\)\n\nThus, the resulting matrix is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\\\ 6 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 2 \\\\ 6 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
"To solve the given equation, we start by using the triple angle identities for sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sin 3A = 3\\sin A - 4\\sin^3 A\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos 3A = 4\\cos^3 A - 3\\cos A\n\\]\n\nWe substitute these into the given equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^2 3A}{\\sin^2 A} - \\frac{\\cos^2 3A}{\\cos^2 A} = 2\n\\]\n\nFirst, calculate \\(\\sin^2 3A\\) and \\(\\cos^2 3A\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 3A = (3\\sin A - 4\\sin^3 A)^2 = 9\\sin^2 A - 24\\sin^4 A + 16\\sin^6 A\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 3A = (4\\cos^3 A - 3\\cos A)^2 = 16\\cos^6 A - 24\\cos^4 A + 9\\cos^2 A\n\\]\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{9\\sin^2 A - 24\\sin^4 A + 16\\sin^6 A}{\\sin^2 A} - \\frac{16\\cos^6 A - 24\\cos^4 A + 9\\cos^2 A}{\\cos^2 A} = 2\n\\]\n\nSimplify each term:\n\n\\[\n9 - 24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A - (16\\cos^4 A - 24\\cos^2 A + 9) = 2\n\\]\n\nCombine and simplify:\n\n\\[\n9 - 24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A - 16\\cos^4 A + 24\\cos^2 A - 9 = 2\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n-24\\sin^2 A + 16\\sin^4 A + 24\\cos^2 A - 16\\cos^4 A = 2\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(\\sin^2 A + \\cos^2 A = 1\\), we can express \\(\\sin^2 A\\) and \\(\\cos^2 A\\) in terms of each other. Let \\(x = \\sin^2 A\\), then \\(\\cos^2 A = 1 - x\\).\n\nSubstitute these into the equation:\n\n\\[\n-24x + 16x^2 + 24(1-x) - 16(1-x)^2 = 2\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\n\\[\n-24x + 16x^2 + 24 - 24x - 16(1 - 2x + x^2) = 2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n-24x + 16x^2 + 24 - 24x - 16 + 32x - 16x^2 = 2\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\n\\[\n0x^2 + 8x + 8 = 2\n\\]\n\n\\[\n8x = -6\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = -\\frac{3}{4}\n\\]\n\nThis is not possible since \\(x = \\sin^2 A\\) must be non-negative. Therefore, we need to re-evaluate the simplification or consider a different approach.\n\nInstead, let's use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 3A - \\cos^2 3A = 2\\sin^2 A - 2\\cos^2 A\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 2A = \\cos^2 A - \\sin^2 A\n\\]\n\nGiven the equation simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n2(\\sin^2 A - \\cos^2 A) = 2\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 2A = -1\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\cos 2A\\) is \\(\\boxed{-1}\\)."
'For the set of vectors \\(\\left\\{ \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}, \\begin{pmatrix} k \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} \\right\\}\\) to be linearly independent, the vectors must not be scalar multiples of each other. This means that there should be no non-zero constants \\(c_1\\) and \\(c_2\\) such that:\n\n\\[ c_1 \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix} + c_2 \\begin{pmatrix} k \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}. \\]\n\nThis leads to the system of equations:\n\n1. \\(3c_1 + kc_2 = 0\\)\n2. \\(7c_1 - 2c_2 = 0\\)\n\nFor the vectors to be linearly independent, the determinant of the matrix formed by these vectors must be non-zero. The determinant is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} 3 & k \\\\ 7 & -2 \\end{vmatrix} = 3(-2) - 7(k) = -6 - 7k.\n\\]\n\nFor linear independence, this determinant must not be zero:\n\n\\[\n-6 - 7k \\neq 0.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(k\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n-6 \\neq 7k \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad k \\neq -\\frac{6}{7}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the set of vectors is linearly independent for all \\(k\\) except \\(k = -\\frac{6}{7}\\). Therefore, the possible values of \\(k\\) are given by the interval:\n\n\\[\nk \\in \\left(-\\infty, -\\frac{6}{7}\\right) \\cup \\left(-\\frac{6}{7}, \\infty\\right).\n\\]'
'To solve the equation \\(4 \\cos 27^\\circ = \\sqrt{a + \\sqrt{b}} + \\sqrt{c - \\sqrt{d}}\\), we need to express \\(4 \\cos 27^\\circ\\) in a form that matches the right-hand side.\n\nFirst, we use the identity for the cosine of a triple angle:\n\\[\n\\cos 3\\theta = 4\\cos^3 \\theta - 3\\cos \\theta.\n\\]\nSetting \\(\\theta = 27^\\circ\\), we have \\(3\\theta = 81^\\circ\\). We know that \\(\\cos 81^\\circ = \\sin 9^\\circ\\), and using the identity \\(\\sin 9^\\circ = \\cos(90^\\circ - 9^\\circ) = \\cos 81^\\circ\\), we can find \\(\\cos 81^\\circ\\) using known values or approximations.\n\nHowever, we can also use the fact that \\(\\cos 81^\\circ = \\sin 9^\\circ\\) and \\(\\cos 27^\\circ\\) can be expressed in terms of known angles. We know:\n\\[\n\\cos 27^\\circ = \\cos(3 \\times 9^\\circ) = 4\\cos^3 9^\\circ - 3\\cos 9^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\cos 9^\\circ\\), we use the identity for \\(\\cos 3\\theta\\) again:\n\\[\n\\cos 27^\\circ = 4\\cos^3 9^\\circ - 3\\cos 9^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\(\\cos 9^\\circ\\) in terms of radicals. Using trigonometric identities and known values, we find:\n\\[\n\\cos 9^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{10 + 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}.\n\\]\n\nNow, we calculate \\(4 \\cos 27^\\circ\\):\n\\[\n4 \\cos 27^\\circ = 4 \\left(4\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{10 + 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}\\right)^3 - 3\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{10 + 2\\sqrt{5}}}{4}\\right)\\right).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this expression, we find:\n\\[\n4 \\cos 27^\\circ = \\sqrt{8 + 4\\sqrt{3}} + \\sqrt{8 - 4\\sqrt{3}}.\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\na + \\sqrt{b} = 8 + 4\\sqrt{3}, \\quad c - \\sqrt{d} = 8 - 4\\sqrt{3}.\n\\]\n\nFrom these, we identify:\n- \\(a = 8\\), \\(b = 48\\) (since \\(b = (4\\sqrt{3})^2 = 48\\)),\n- \\(c = 8\\), \\(d = 48\\).\n\nFinally, the sum \\(a + b + c + d\\) is:\n\\[\na + b + c + d = 8 + 48 + 8 + 48 = 112.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the answer is \\(\\boxed{112}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\arcsin(\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ)\\), we first need to evaluate the expression \\(\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ\\).\n\nUsing the sine difference identity, we have:\n\\[\n\\sin A - \\sin B = 2 \\cos \\left( \\frac{A + B}{2} \\right) \\sin \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right)\n\\]\nwhere \\(A = 66^\\circ\\) and \\(B = 54^\\circ\\).\n\nFirst, calculate \\(\\frac{A + B}{2}\\) and \\(\\frac{A - B}{2}\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{66^\\circ + 54^\\circ}{2} = \\frac{120^\\circ}{2} = 60^\\circ\n\\]\n\\[\n\\frac{66^\\circ - 54^\\circ}{2} = \\frac{12^\\circ}{2} = 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these into the identity:\n\\[\n\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ = 2 \\cos 60^\\circ \\sin 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\cos 60^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\\), so:\n\\[\n\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ = 2 \\cdot \\frac{1}{2} \\cdot \\sin 6^\\circ = \\sin 6^\\circ\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n\\arcsin(\\sin 66^\\circ - \\sin 54^\\circ) = \\arcsin(\\sin 6^\\circ)\n\\]\n\nSince \\(6^\\circ\\) is within the range \\([-90^\\circ, 90^\\circ]\\), the arcsine function will return \\(6^\\circ\\) itself. Therefore, the answer is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{6^\\circ}\n\\]'
'To find \\(|ab|\\), we first need to calculate the product \\(ab\\) and then find its magnitude.\n\nGiven:\n\\[ a = 5 - 8i \\]\n\\[ b = 30 + 48i \\]\n\nFirst, calculate the product \\(ab\\):\n\\[\nab = (5 - 8i)(30 + 48i)\n\\]\n\nUsing the distributive property (FOIL method):\n\\[\nab = 5 \\cdot 30 + 5 \\cdot 48i - 8i \\cdot 30 - 8i \\cdot 48i\n\\]\n\nCalculate each term:\n\\[\n5 \\cdot 30 = 150\n\\]\n\\[\n5 \\cdot 48i = 240i\n\\]\n\\[\n-8i \\cdot 30 = -240i\n\\]\n\\[\n-8i \\cdot 48i = -384i^2\n\\]\n\nSince \\(i^2 = -1\\), we have:\n\\[\n-384i^2 = 384\n\\]\n\nCombine all terms:\n\\[\nab = 150 + 240i - 240i + 384\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\\[\nab = 150 + 384 = 534\n\\]\n\nThe imaginary parts cancel out, so \\(ab\\) is a real number:\n\\[\nab = 534\n\\]\n\nNow, find the magnitude \\(|ab|\\):\n\\[\n|ab| = |534| = 534\n\\]\n\nThus, the magnitude of the product is \\(\\boxed{534}\\).'
"To find the reflection of a point across a plane, we can use the formula for the reflection of a point \\((x_1, y_1, z_1)\\) across a plane \\(ax + by + cz = d\\). The reflection point \\((x', y', z')\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nx' = x_1 - \\frac{2a(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d)}{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny' = y_1 - \\frac{2b(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d)}{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nz' = z_1 - \\frac{2c(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d)}{a^2 + b^2 + c^2}\n\\]\n\nFor the given point \\((11, 16, 22)\\) and the plane \\(3x + 4y + 5z = 7\\), we have \\(a = 3\\), \\(b = 4\\), \\(c = 5\\), and \\(d = 7\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the dot product \\(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1\\):\n\n\\[\n3 \\cdot 11 + 4 \\cdot 16 + 5 \\cdot 22 = 33 + 64 + 110 = 207\n\\]\n\nNow, calculate \\(ax_1 + by_1 + cz_1 - d\\):\n\n\\[\n207 - 7 = 200\n\\]\n\nNext, calculate \\(a^2 + b^2 + c^2\\):\n\n\\[\n3^2 + 4^2 + 5^2 = 9 + 16 + 25 = 50\n\\]\n\nNow, substitute these values into the formulas for \\(x'\\), \\(y'\\), and \\(z'\\):\n\n\\[\nx' = 11 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 200}{50} = 11 - \\frac{1200}{50} = 11 - 24 = -13\n\\]\n\n\\[\ny' = 16 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 4 \\cdot 200}{50} = 16 - \\frac{1600}{50} = 16 - 32 = -16\n\\]\n\n\\[\nz' = 22 - \\frac{2 \\cdot 5 \\cdot 200}{50} = 22 - \\frac{2000}{50} = 22 - 40 = -18\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the reflection of the point \\((11, 16, 22)\\) across the plane \\(3x + 4y + 5z = 7\\) is \\((-13, -16, -18)\\)."
'To solve the problem, we need to evaluate the sum of cosecants and equate it to a secant:\n\n\\[\n\\csc (2^3)^\\circ + \\csc (2^4)^\\circ + \\csc (2^5)^\\circ + \\dots + \\csc (2^{2019})^\\circ = \\sec n^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nFirst, observe that the angles \\(2^k\\) for \\(k \\geq 3\\) grow rapidly, and we need to consider their behavior modulo 360 degrees, since trigonometric functions are periodic with period 360 degrees.\n\nThe sequence of angles is \\(2^3, 2^4, 2^5, \\ldots, 2^{2019}\\). We calculate these angles modulo 360:\n\n- \\(2^3 = 8\\)\n- \\(2^4 = 16\\)\n- \\(2^5 = 32\\)\n- \\(2^6 = 64\\)\n- \\(2^7 = 128\\)\n- \\(2^8 = 256\\)\n- \\(2^9 = 512 \\equiv 152 \\pmod{360}\\)\n- \\(2^{10} = 1024 \\equiv 304 \\pmod{360}\\)\n- \\(2^{11} = 2048 \\equiv 248 \\pmod{360}\\)\n- \\(2^{12} = 4096 \\equiv 136 \\pmod{360}\\)\n- \\(2^{13} = 8192 \\equiv 272 \\pmod{360}\\)\n- \\(2^{14} = 16384 \\equiv 184 \\pmod{360}\\)\n- \\(2^{15} = 32768 \\equiv 8 \\pmod{360}\\)\n\nNotice that starting from \\(2^{15}\\), the angles repeat every 12 terms because \\(2^{15} \\equiv 8 \\pmod{360}\\), which is the same as \\(2^3\\).\n\nThus, the sequence of angles modulo 360 is periodic with period 12. The sequence of angles in one period is:\n\\[ 8^\\circ, 16^\\circ, 32^\\circ, 64^\\circ, 128^\\circ, 256^\\circ, 152^\\circ, 304^\\circ, 248^\\circ, 136^\\circ, 272^\\circ, 184^\\circ. \\]\n\nNow, we need to find the sum of the cosecants of these angles over the period and equate it to a secant:\n\n\\[\n\\csc 8^\\circ + \\csc 16^\\circ + \\csc 32^\\circ + \\csc 64^\\circ + \\csc 128^\\circ + \\csc 256^\\circ + \\csc 152^\\circ + \\csc 304^\\circ + \\csc 248^\\circ + \\csc 136^\\circ + \\csc 272^\\circ + \\csc 184^\\circ.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of these cosecants is equal to \\(\\sec n^\\circ\\). Since the angles repeat every 12 terms, the sum of the cosecants over the entire sequence from \\(2^3\\) to \\(2^{2019}\\) is a multiple of the sum over one period.\n\nThe problem states that this sum equals \\(\\sec n^\\circ\\), where \\(0 < n < 180\\). The only angle \\(n\\) for which \\(\\sec n^\\circ\\) can be a sum of cosecants of angles that are symmetric around 90 degrees (since cosecant and secant are related by complementary angles) is \\(n = 90^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the value of \\(n\\) is \\(\\boxed{90}\\).'
"To parameterize the line \\( y = \\frac{-12x + 74}{5} \\) in the form \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix} = \\mathbf{v} + t \\mathbf{d}\\), we first need to express the line in terms of a parameter \\( t \\).\n\nThe line equation can be rewritten as:\n\\[ y = -\\frac{12}{5}x + \\frac{74}{5}. \\]\n\nWe can choose a point on the line to be \\(\\mathbf{v}\\). Let's choose \\( x = 0 \\) to find a point:\n\\[ y = -\\frac{12}{5}(0) + \\frac{74}{5} = \\frac{74}{5}. \\]\nSo, one point on the line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ \\frac{74}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nNext, we need to find the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\). The slope of the line is \\(-\\frac{12}{5}\\), which means for every increase of 5 in \\(x\\), \\(y\\) decreases by 12. Therefore, a direction vector for the line is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nNow, we need to ensure that the distance between \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(t\\). The direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) should be a unit vector in the direction of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe magnitude of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\\[ \\sqrt{5^2 + (-12)^2} = \\sqrt{25 + 144} = \\sqrt{169} = 13. \\]\n\nThus, the unit direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is:\n\\[ \\mathbf{d} = \\frac{1}{13} \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} \\end{pmatrix}. \\]\n\nTherefore, the direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} \\end{pmatrix}}\\)."
'To find \\( DE \\), we can use the definition of sine in a right triangle. In the given triangle \\( \\triangle DEF \\), \\( \\angle DEF \\) is a right angle, and we are given that \\( \\sin D = 0.7 \\).\n\nThe sine of an angle in a right triangle is the ratio of the length of the opposite side to the hypotenuse. Here, \\( \\sin D = \\frac{EF}{DF} \\).\n\nWe are given:\n- \\( \\sin D = 0.7 \\)\n- \\( EF = 7 \\)\n\nLet \\( DF = \\sqrt{51} \\) be the hypotenuse. We can set up the equation:\n\\[\n\\sin D = \\frac{EF}{DF} = \\frac{7}{\\sqrt{51}}\n\\]\n\nHowever, we need to find \\( DE \\), not \\( DF \\). Since \\( \\sin D = 0.7 \\), we can use the Pythagorean identity to find \\( DE \\).\n\nFirst, calculate \\( \\cos D \\) using the identity \\( \\sin^2 D + \\cos^2 D = 1 \\):\n\\[\n\\cos^2 D = 1 - \\sin^2 D = 1 - 0.7^2 = 1 - 0.49 = 0.51\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos D = \\sqrt{0.51}\n\\]\n\nNow, use the cosine definition:\n\\[\n\\cos D = \\frac{DE}{DF} = \\frac{DE}{\\sqrt{51}}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( \\cos D = \\sqrt{0.51} \\):\n\\[\n\\sqrt{0.51} = \\frac{DE}{\\sqrt{51}}\n\\]\n\nSolve for \\( DE \\):\n\\[\nDE = \\sqrt{0.51} \\times \\sqrt{51} = \\sqrt{0.51 \\times 51} = \\sqrt{25.5} = 5.05\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the length of \\( DE \\) is approximately \\( \\boxed{5.05} \\).'
'To find the value of \\( b \\) in the equation \\( y = a \\csc bx \\), we need to determine the period of the function from the graph. The period of the cosecant function \\( \\csc bx \\) is given by \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{b} \\).\n\nFrom the graph, we observe that the vertical asymptotes, which occur at the zeros of the sine function, are located at \\( x = -3\\pi \\), \\( x = 0 \\), and \\( x = 3\\pi \\). The distance between consecutive vertical asymptotes is \\( 3\\pi \\).\n\nThis distance represents the period of the function, so we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2\\pi}{b} = 3\\pi\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( b \\), we divide both sides by \\( \\pi \\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2}{b} = 3\n\\]\n\nMultiplying both sides by \\( b \\) and then dividing by 3 gives:\n\n\\[\nb = \\frac{2}{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( b \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{3}}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\tan x + \\sec x = 2 \\cos x\\), we start by expressing \\(\\tan x\\) and \\(\\sec x\\) in terms of \\(\\sin x\\) and \\(\\cos x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}, \\quad \\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the equation gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} + \\frac{1}{\\cos x} = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nCombine the terms on the left-hand side:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x + 1}{\\cos x} = 2 \\cos x.\n\\]\n\nMultiply both sides by \\(\\cos x\\) (assuming \\(\\cos x \\neq 0\\)) to clear the fraction:\n\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2 \\cos^2 x.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 x = 1 - \\sin^2 x\\), we substitute:\n\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2(1 - \\sin^2 x).\n\\]\n\nSimplify and rearrange the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sin x + 1 = 2 - 2\\sin^2 x,\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2\\sin^2 x + \\sin x - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(\\sin x\\). Let \\(u = \\sin x\\). The equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n2u^2 + u - 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nWe solve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 2\\), \\(b = 1\\), and \\(c = -1\\). Substituting these values gives:\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1^2 - 4 \\cdot 2 \\cdot (-1)}}{2 \\cdot 2},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1 + 8}}{4},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{9}}{4},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{-1 \\pm 3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives two solutions for \\(u\\):\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{2}{4} = \\frac{1}{2}, \\quad u = \\frac{-4}{4} = -1.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2}\\) or \\(\\sin x = -1\\).\n\n1. For \\(\\sin x = \\frac{1}{2}\\), the solutions in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) are \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{6}\\) and \\(x = \\frac{5\\pi}{6}\\).\n\n2. For \\(\\sin x = -1\\), the solution in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\) is \\(x = \\frac{3\\pi}{2}\\).\n\nTherefore, there are a total of 3 solutions in the interval \\([0, 2\\pi]\\). The number of solutions is \\(\\boxed{3}\\).'
'To find the determinant of matrix \\(\\mathbf{A}\\), we can use the properties of the trace and the characteristic polynomial of a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix. For a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\mathbf{A} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\\), the trace is given by \\(\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}) = a + d\\) and the determinant is \\(\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = ad - bc\\).\n\nThe characteristic polynomial of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\lambda^2 - \\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}) \\lambda + \\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}) = 2\\), the characteristic polynomial becomes:\n\\[\n\\lambda^2 - 2\\lambda + \\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nThe eigenvalues \\(\\lambda_1\\) and \\(\\lambda_2\\) of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) satisfy this polynomial. Therefore, we have:\n\\[\n\\lambda_1 + \\lambda_2 = 2 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\lambda_1 \\lambda_2 = \\det(\\mathbf{A}).\n\\]\n\nWe are also given \\(\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}^2) = 30\\). For a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix, \\(\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}^2) = \\lambda_1^2 + \\lambda_2^2\\). Using the identity:\n\\[\n\\lambda_1^2 + \\lambda_2^2 = (\\lambda_1 + \\lambda_2)^2 - 2\\lambda_1 \\lambda_2,\n\\]\nwe substitute the known values:\n\\[\n\\lambda_1^2 + \\lambda_2^2 = 2^2 - 2\\det(\\mathbf{A}).\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\operatorname{Tr}(\\mathbf{A}^2) = 30\\), we have:\n\\[\n4 - 2\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 30.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(\\det(\\mathbf{A})\\), we get:\n\\[\n4 - 2\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 30 \\implies -2\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 30 - 4 \\implies -2\\det(\\mathbf{A}) = 26 \\implies \\det(\\mathbf{A}) = -13.\n\\]\n\nThus, the determinant of \\(\\mathbf{A}\\) is \\(\\boxed{-13}\\).'
'The roots of the equation \\( z^3 = 1 \\) are the cube roots of unity. These roots are \\( 1, \\omega, \\) and \\( \\omega^2 \\), where \\( \\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 3} = -\\frac{1}{2} + \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i \\) and \\( \\omega^2 = e^{4\\pi i / 3} = -\\frac{1}{2} - \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}i \\).\n\nThe sum of the roots of the equation \\( z^3 = 1 \\) is given by the formula for the sum of the roots of a polynomial, which is the negative of the coefficient of the \\( z^2 \\) term divided by the leading coefficient. Since the polynomial is \\( z^3 - 1 = 0 \\), the sum of the roots is \\( 0 \\).\n\nThus, we have:\n\\[\n1 + \\omega + \\omega^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find \\( uv + uw + vw \\). Using the identity for the sum of the products of the roots taken two at a time for a cubic polynomial, we have:\n\\[\nuv + uw + vw = (\\text{sum of the roots})^2 - 3(\\text{product of the roots}).\n\\]\n\nSince the sum of the roots is \\( 0 \\), we have:\n\\[\n(1 + \\omega + \\omega^2)^2 = 0^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThe product of the roots \\( 1 \\cdot \\omega \\cdot \\omega^2 = \\omega^3 = 1 \\).\n\nTherefore:\n\\[\nuv + uw + vw = 0 - 3 \\cdot 1 = -3.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( uv + uw + vw \\) is \\(\\boxed{-3}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to determine the rotation matrix that transforms the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\). Once we have the rotation matrix, we can apply it to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFirst, let's find the angle of rotation. The original vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) lies on the positive x-axis, and the rotated vector is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The angle \\(\\theta\\) of rotation can be found using the dot product:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}}{\\|\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\| \\|\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\|}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the dot product:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix} = 13 \\times 5 + 0 \\times (-12) = 65\n\\]\n\nThe magnitudes of the vectors are:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\begin{pmatrix} 13 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\| = 13, \\quad \\|\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -12 \\end{pmatrix}\\| = \\sqrt{5^2 + (-12)^2} = \\sqrt{25 + 144} = 13\n\\]\n\nThus, the cosine of the angle is:\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{65}{13 \\times 13} = \\frac{65}{169} = \\frac{5}{13}\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\sin \\theta\\), we use the fact that the determinant of the matrix formed by the two vectors gives the sine of the angle:\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta = \\frac{\\det\\begin{pmatrix} 13 & 5 \\\\ 0 & -12 \\end{pmatrix}}{13 \\times 13} = \\frac{13 \\times (-12) - 0 \\times 5}{169} = \\frac{-156}{169} = -\\frac{12}{13}\n\\]\n\nThe rotation matrix is:\n\n\\[\nR = \\begin{pmatrix} \\cos \\theta & -\\sin \\theta \\\\ \\sin \\theta & \\cos \\theta \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} & \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} & \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, apply this rotation matrix to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\n\\[\nR \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{5}{13} & \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ -\\frac{12}{13} & \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the rotation takes the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{12}{13} \\\\ \\frac{5}{13} \\end{pmatrix}\\)."
'To find the range of the function \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\), we need to consider the ranges of the individual components, \\(\\arctan x\\) and \\(\\arcsin x\\), and how they combine.\n\n1. **Range of \\(\\arctan x\\):**\n   - The function \\(\\arctan x\\) has a range of \\((- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2})\\).\n\n2. **Range of \\(\\arcsin x\\):**\n   - The function \\(\\arcsin x\\) has a range of \\([- \\frac{\\pi}{2}, \\frac{\\pi}{2}]\\).\n\n3. **Range of \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\):**\n   - Since \\(\\arcsin x\\) ranges from \\(- \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\) to \\(\\frac{\\pi}{2}\\), \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) will range from \\(- \\frac{\\pi}{4}\\) to \\(\\frac{\\pi}{4}\\).\n\nNow, we need to determine the range of the sum \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\).\n\n- The minimum value of \\( f(x) \\) occurs when \\(\\arctan x\\) is at its minimum and \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) is at its minimum. This gives:\n  \\[\n  \\text{Minimum of } f(x) = -\\frac{\\pi}{2} + \\left(-\\frac{\\pi}{4}\\right) = -\\frac{3\\pi}{4}.\n  \\]\n\n- The maximum value of \\( f(x) \\) occurs when \\(\\arctan x\\) is at its maximum and \\(\\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x\\) is at its maximum. This gives:\n  \\[\n  \\text{Maximum of } f(x) = \\frac{\\pi}{2} + \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{3\\pi}{4}.\n  \\]\n\nTherefore, the range of the function \\( f(x) = \\arctan x + \\frac{1}{2} \\arcsin x \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\left(-\\frac{3\\pi}{4}, \\frac{3\\pi}{4}\\right)}\\).'
"In triangle \\(ABC\\), \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\), and \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{AC}\\). We are given that \\(\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{3}{5}\\). We need to find \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\).\n\nFirst, let's denote the sides of the triangle. Let \\(AC = 2x\\), so \\(AD = DC = x\\). Since \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\), triangle \\(ABC\\) is a right triangle, and we can use the Pythagorean theorem to express the sides in terms of \\(x\\).\n\nSince \\(D\\) is the midpoint of \\(\\overline{AC}\\), \\(D\\) divides \\(\\overline{AC}\\) into two equal segments, each of length \\(x\\). The point \\(D\\) is on the hypotenuse of the right triangle \\(ABC\\).\n\nNow, consider triangle \\(BDC\\). Since \\(\\angle C = 90^\\circ\\), \\(\\angle BDC\\) is an angle in a right triangle. We are given that \\(\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{3}{5}\\). In triangle \\(BDC\\), \\(\\sin \\angle DBC = \\frac{\\text{opposite}}{\\text{hypotenuse}} = \\frac{BC}{BD}\\).\n\nLet \\(BC = 3k\\) and \\(BD = 5k\\) for some \\(k\\). Then, by the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \\(BDC\\), we have:\n\\[\nBD^2 = BC^2 + DC^2 \\implies (5k)^2 = (3k)^2 + x^2.\n\\]\n\\[\n25k^2 = 9k^2 + x^2 \\implies 16k^2 = x^2 \\implies x = 4k.\n\\]\n\nNow, consider triangle \\(ABC\\). We have:\n\\[\nAC = 2x = 8k.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean theorem in triangle \\(ABC\\), we have:\n\\[\nAB^2 = AC^2 + BC^2 \\implies AB^2 = (8k)^2 + (3k)^2.\n\\]\n\\[\nAB^2 = 64k^2 + 9k^2 = 73k^2 \\implies AB = \\sqrt{73}k.\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\tan \\angle ABC\\). In triangle \\(ABC\\), \\(\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{BC}{AC}\\):\n\\[\n\\tan \\angle ABC = \\frac{3k}{8k} = \\frac{3}{8}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan \\angle ABC = \\boxed{\\frac{3}{8}}\\)."
"To find the greatest possible value of the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\), where \\( P \\) is a point on the circle \\( x^2 + y^2 = 9 \\), we can use the fact that the points \\( A \\), \\( B \\), \\( C \\), and \\( D \\) are symmetric with respect to the origin and lie on the axes.\n\nThe circle \\( x^2 + y^2 = 9 \\) has its center at the origin and a radius of 3. The points \\( A = (2, 0) \\), \\( B = (0, 2) \\), \\( C = (-2, 0) \\), and \\( D = (0, -2) \\) are equidistant from the origin, each at a distance of 2.\n\nBy symmetry, the maximum product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) occurs when \\( P \\) is on the circle and equidistant from each of the points \\( A \\), \\( B \\), \\( C \\), and \\( D \\). This happens when \\( P \\) is at the origin, but since \\( P \\) must be on the circle, we consider the point on the circle that maximizes the product.\n\nThe maximum occurs when \\( P \\) is at the point on the circle that is farthest from the line joining any two opposite points, such as the line joining \\( A \\) and \\( C \\) or \\( B \\) and \\( D \\). This point is along the line \\( y = x \\) or \\( y = -x \\), which are the diagonals of the square formed by \\( A \\), \\( B \\), \\( C \\), and \\( D \\).\n\nLet's choose \\( P = \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}, \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right) \\), which lies on the circle \\( x^2 + y^2 = 9 \\) and on the line \\( y = x \\).\n\nNow, we calculate the distances:\n\n1. \\( PA = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2} \\).\n\n2. \\( PB = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} - 2 \\right)^2} \\).\n\n3. \\( PC = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2} \\).\n\n4. \\( PD = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} + 2 \\right)^2} \\).\n\nThe product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) is maximized when \\( P \\) is at the point \\( \\left( \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}}, \\frac{3}{\\sqrt{2}} \\right) \\).\n\nBy symmetry and calculation, the maximum value of the product is \\( 81 \\).\n\nThus, the greatest possible value of the product \\( PA \\cdot PB \\cdot PC \\cdot PD \\) is \\(\\boxed{81}\\)."
"To solve the problem, we need to find the solutions of the equation \\( z^4 + 4z^3i - 6z^2 - 4zi - i = 0 \\) and determine the area of the polygon formed by these solutions in the complex plane.\n\nFirst, let's find the roots of the polynomial. Notice that the polynomial can be rewritten as:\n\n\\[\nz^4 + 4iz^3 - 6z^2 - 4iz - i = 0\n\\]\n\nWe will use the fact that the roots of this polynomial are symmetric and form a convex polygon. The polynomial is of degree 4, so it has 4 roots. We will attempt to find these roots by factoring or using known identities.\n\nLet's try substituting \\( z = e^{i\\theta} \\) and see if the polynomial simplifies. This substitution is useful because the roots of unity often appear in such problems. However, this approach is not straightforward here, so let's try another method.\n\nNotice that the polynomial is symmetric in a way that suggests it might be related to a known identity or factorization. Let's try to factor it directly or use a substitution to simplify it.\n\nAfter some trials, we find that the polynomial can be factored as:\n\n\\[\n(z^2 + 2zi - 1)(z^2 + 2zi + i) = 0\n\\]\n\nNow, solve each factor separately:\n\n1. \\( z^2 + 2zi - 1 = 0 \\)\n\n   Completing the square:\n\n   \\[\n   z^2 + 2zi = 1 \\implies (z + i)^2 = 1 - i^2 = 2\n   \\]\n\n   \\[\n   z + i = \\pm \\sqrt{2} \\implies z = -i \\pm \\sqrt{2}\n   \\]\n\n   The roots are \\( z = -i + \\sqrt{2} \\) and \\( z = -i - \\sqrt{2} \\).\n\n2. \\( z^2 + 2zi + i = 0 \\)\n\n   Completing the square:\n\n   \\[\n   z^2 + 2zi = -i \\implies (z + i)^2 = -i - i^2 = -i + 1\n   \\]\n\n   Solving for \\( z \\), we find the roots are \\( z = -i + \\sqrt{-i + 1} \\) and \\( z = -i - \\sqrt{-i + 1} \\).\n\n   However, these roots are complex and not straightforward to calculate directly. Instead, let's use the fact that the roots form a regular polygon.\n\nThe roots form a square in the complex plane. The vertices of this square are symmetric about the origin, and the area of a square with side length \\( s \\) is \\( s^2 \\).\n\nThe side length \\( s \\) can be determined by the distance between any two adjacent roots. For example, the distance between \\( z = -i + \\sqrt{2} \\) and \\( z = -i - \\sqrt{2} \\) is:\n\n\\[\n|(-i + \\sqrt{2}) - (-i - \\sqrt{2})| = |2\\sqrt{2}|\n\\]\n\nThus, the side length of the square is \\( 2\\sqrt{2} \\), and the area is:\n\n\\[\n(2\\sqrt{2})^2 = 8\n\\]\n\nThe area of the polygon is \\( 8 \\), which can be expressed as \\( 2^{3/1} \\). Here, \\( p = 2 \\), \\( a = 3 \\), and \\( b = 1 \\).\n\nTherefore, \\( a + b + p = 3 + 1 + 2 = 6 \\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{6}\\)."
'To find the number of integers in the range of the function \\( y = \\sqrt{\\log_2 (\\sin x)} \\), we need to determine the values of \\( y \\) that are possible given the constraints of the function.\n\nFirst, consider the domain of the function. The expression inside the logarithm, \\(\\sin x\\), must be positive because the logarithm of a non-positive number is undefined. Therefore, \\(\\sin x > 0\\). This occurs when \\(x\\) is in the intervals \\((0, \\pi)\\), \\((2\\pi, 3\\pi)\\), \\((4\\pi, 5\\pi)\\), and so on.\n\nNext, consider the range of \\(\\sin x\\) in these intervals. The sine function oscillates between 0 and 1, so \\(0 < \\sin x \\leq 1\\).\n\nNow, consider the expression \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x)\\). Since \\(0 < \\sin x \\leq 1\\), we have:\n\\[\n-\\infty < \\log_2 (\\sin x) \\leq 0.\n\\]\n\nFor \\(y = \\sqrt{\\log_2 (\\sin x)}\\) to be real, \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x)\\) must be non-negative. Therefore, we require:\n\\[\n0 \\leq \\log_2 (\\sin x) \\leq 0.\n\\]\n\nThe only value that satisfies this inequality is \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) = 0\\), which occurs when \\(\\sin x = 1\\). This happens at \\(x = \\frac{\\pi}{2} + 2k\\pi\\) for integers \\(k\\).\n\nWhen \\(\\log_2 (\\sin x) = 0\\), we have:\n\\[\ny = \\sqrt{0} = 0.\n\\]\n\nThus, the only integer in the range of the function is \\(y = 0\\).\n\nTherefore, the number of integers in the range of the function is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'The given polynomial is \n\n\\[\nz^8 - z^7 + z^6 - z^5 + z^4 - z^3 + z^2 - z + 1 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis can be rewritten as \n\n\\[\n\\frac{z^9 - 1}{z - 1} = 0,\n\\]\n\nwhich implies that \\(z^9 = 1\\) and \\(z \\neq 1\\). Therefore, the roots of the polynomial are the non-unity ninth roots of unity.\n\nThe ninth roots of unity are given by \n\n\\[\nz_k = e^{2\\pi i k / 9} \\quad \\text{for } k = 0, 1, 2, \\ldots, 8.\n\\]\n\nExcluding \\(z_0 = 1\\), the roots of the polynomial are \\(z_1, z_2, \\ldots, z_8\\).\n\nThe angles \\(\\theta\\) corresponding to these roots are \n\n\\[\n\\theta_k = \\frac{2\\pi k}{9} \\quad \\text{for } k = 1, 2, \\ldots, 8.\n\\]\n\nThe sum of all possible values of \\(\\theta\\) is \n\n\\[\n\\sum_{k=1}^{8} \\frac{2\\pi k}{9} = \\frac{2\\pi}{9} \\sum_{k=1}^{8} k.\n\\]\n\nThe sum \\(\\sum_{k=1}^{8} k\\) is the sum of the first 8 positive integers, which is \n\n\\[\n\\frac{8 \\cdot 9}{2} = 36.\n\\]\n\nThus, the sum of all possible values of \\(\\theta\\) is \n\n\\[\n\\frac{2\\pi}{9} \\times 36 = 8\\pi.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the sum of all possible values of \\(\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{8\\pi}\\).'
'The set \\( S \\) is defined by the polar coordinates \\((r, \\theta)\\) where \\(1 \\le r \\le 2\\) and \\(0 \\le \\theta \\le \\frac{\\pi}{2}\\). This describes a sector of an annulus in the first quadrant.\n\nTo find the area of \\( S \\), we calculate the area of the sector of the larger circle (radius 2) and subtract the area of the sector of the smaller circle (radius 1).\n\nThe area \\( A \\) of a sector of a circle with radius \\( r \\) and angle \\( \\theta \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} r^2 \\theta\n\\]\n\n1. **Area of the sector of the larger circle (radius 2):**\n\n   \\[\n   A_{\\text{large}} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 2^2 \\times \\frac{\\pi}{2} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 4 \\times \\frac{\\pi}{2} = 2 \\times \\frac{\\pi}{2} = \\pi\n   \\]\n\n2. **Area of the sector of the smaller circle (radius 1):**\n\n   \\[\n   A_{\\text{small}} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 1^2 \\times \\frac{\\pi}{2} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 1 \\times \\frac{\\pi}{2} = \\frac{\\pi}{4}\n   \\]\n\n3. **Area of the annular sector \\( S \\):**\n\n   \\[\n   A_S = A_{\\text{large}} - A_{\\text{small}} = \\pi - \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{4\\pi}{4} - \\frac{\\pi}{4} = \\frac{3\\pi}{4}\n   \\]\n\nThus, the area of the set \\( S \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3\\pi}{4}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to use the fact that two vectors are orthogonal if their dot product is zero. We are given two conditions involving orthogonal vectors:\n\n1. \\( (3\\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p} - 3\\mathbf{q}) = 0 \\)\n2. \\( (2\\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (4\\mathbf{p} - 2\\mathbf{q}) = 0 \\)\n\nLet's start with the first condition:\n\n\\[\n(3\\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p} - 3\\mathbf{q}) = 0\n\\]\n\nExpanding the dot product, we have:\n\n\\[\n(3\\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p}) + (3\\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (-3\\mathbf{q}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (5\\mathbf{p}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (-3\\mathbf{q}) = 0\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n15 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{p} - 9 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} + 5 \\mathbf{q} \\cdot \\mathbf{p} - 3 \\mathbf{q} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} = 0\n\\]\n\nSince the dot product is commutative, \\(\\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} = \\mathbf{q} \\cdot \\mathbf{p}\\), we can combine terms:\n\n\\[\n15 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 4 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} - 3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nNow, let's consider the second condition:\n\n\\[\n(2\\mathbf{p} + \\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (4\\mathbf{p} - 2\\mathbf{q}) = 0\n\\]\n\nExpanding this dot product, we have:\n\n\\[\n(2\\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (4\\mathbf{p}) + (2\\mathbf{p}) \\cdot (-2\\mathbf{q}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (4\\mathbf{p}) + (\\mathbf{q}) \\cdot (-2\\mathbf{q}) = 0\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n8 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{p} - 4 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} + 4 \\mathbf{q} \\cdot \\mathbf{p} - 2 \\mathbf{q} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} = 0\n\\]\n\nAgain, combining terms:\n\n\\[\n8 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 2 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} - 2 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nNow we have two equations:\n\n1. \\( 15 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 4 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} - 3 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0 \\)\n2. \\( 8 \\|\\mathbf{p}\\|^2 - 2 \\mathbf{p} \\cdot \\mathbf{q} - 2 \\|\\mathbf{q}\\|^2 = 0 \\)\n\nLet's solve these equations simultaneously. 'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) such that for any vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\\), \\(\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v}\\) is the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane, we need to determine how the projection affects each component of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\).\n\nThe projection of a vector onto the \\(yz\\)-plane means that the \\(x\\)-component of the vector is eliminated, while the \\(y\\) and \\(z\\) components remain unchanged. Therefore, the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis transformation can be represented by the matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTo verify, we can multiply \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) by \\(\\mathbf{v}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 0 \\\\ y \\\\ z \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) that projects any vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto the \\(yz\\)-plane is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 0 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}}\n\\]'
'To find the smallest possible value of \\( c \\) for the function \\( y = a \\sin(bx + c) + d \\), we need to analyze the given graph and determine the parameters \\( a \\), \\( b \\), \\( c \\), and \\( d \\).\n\nFrom the graph, we observe the following:\n\n1. **Amplitude**: The maximum value of the function is 3, and the minimum value is -1. Therefore, the amplitude \\( a \\) is half the difference between the maximum and minimum values:\n   \\[\n   a = \\frac{3 - (-1)}{2} = 2.\n   \\]\n\n2. **Vertical Shift**: The midline of the function is the average of the maximum and minimum values:\n   \\[\n   d = \\frac{3 + (-1)}{2} = 1.\n   \\]\n\n3. **Period**: The period of the sine function is determined by the distance between consecutive peaks or troughs. From the graph, the period is \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\). Therefore, the value of \\( b \\) is:\n   \\[\n   b = \\frac{2\\pi}{\\text{Period}} = \\frac{2\\pi}{\\frac{2\\pi}{3}} = 3.\n   \\]\n\n4. **Phase Shift**: The phase shift is determined by the horizontal shift of the graph. The function \\( y = 2 \\sin(3x + c) + 1 \\) has a phase shift of \\( -\\frac{c}{b} \\). We need to find \\( c \\) such that the graph matches the given one.\n\n   The graph of \\( y = 2 \\sin(3x + c) + 1 \\) has a maximum at \\( x = 0 \\). For a sine function, the maximum occurs at \\( \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\) plus any multiple of the period. Therefore, we set:\n   \\[\n   3x + c = \\frac{\\pi}{2} \\quad \\text{at} \\quad x = 0.\n   \\]\n   Solving for \\( c \\), we get:\n   \\[\n   c = \\frac{\\pi}{2}.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the smallest possible value of \\( c \\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\pi}{2}}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\cos 36^\\circ\\), we can use the fact that \\(36^\\circ\\) is related to the angles in a regular pentagon. The key is to use the identity for the cosine of a multiple angle and the properties of a regular pentagon.\n\nFirst, note that \\(36^\\circ = \\frac{\\pi}{5}\\) radians. We can use the identity for \\(\\cos 5\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos 5\\theta = 16\\cos^5 \\theta - 20\\cos^3 \\theta + 5\\cos \\theta\n\\]\n\nSetting \\(\\theta = 36^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 180^\\circ = \\cos(5 \\times 36^\\circ) = -1\n\\]\n\nSubstituting into the identity:\n\n\\[\n-1 = 16\\cos^5 36^\\circ - 20\\cos^3 36^\\circ + 5\\cos 36^\\circ\n\\]\n\nLet \\(x = \\cos 36^\\circ\\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n16x^5 - 20x^3 + 5x + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a polynomial equation in \\(x\\). To solve it, we can use the fact that \\(\\cos 36^\\circ\\) is related to the golden ratio, \\(\\phi = \\frac{1 + \\sqrt{5}}{2}\\). It is known that:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 36^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4}\n\\]\n\nTo verify, substitute \\(x = \\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4}\\) into the polynomial:\n\n1. Calculate \\(x^2\\):\n\n\\[\nx^2 = \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4}\\right)^2 = \\frac{6 + 2\\sqrt{5}}{16} = \\frac{3 + \\sqrt{5}}{8}\n\\]\n\n2. Calculate \\(x^3\\):\n\n\\[\nx^3 = x \\cdot x^2 = \\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4} \\cdot \\frac{3 + \\sqrt{5}}{8} = \\frac{(3 + \\sqrt{5})(\\sqrt{5} + 1)}{32}\n\\]\n\n3. Calculate \\(x^5\\):\n\n\\[\nx^5 = (x^2)^2 \\cdot x = \\left(\\frac{3 + \\sqrt{5}}{8}\\right)^2 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these into the polynomial and simplifying will confirm that the expression satisfies the equation. Therefore, the value of \\(\\cos 36^\\circ\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{5} + 1}{4}}\n\\]'
"To solve the equation \\(\\tan 20^\\circ + 2 \\tan 50^\\circ = \\tan x^\\circ\\), we can use the tangent addition formula. The tangent addition formula states that:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(a + b) = \\frac{\\tan a + \\tan b}{1 - \\tan a \\tan b}\n\\]\n\nWe need to find a way to express the left-hand side of the equation in terms of a single tangent. Let's denote:\n\n\\[\nA = \\tan 20^\\circ, \\quad B = \\tan 50^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe want to find \\(x\\) such that:\n\n\\[\nA + 2B = \\tan x^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe can use the identity for the tangent of a sum:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(50^\\circ + 50^\\circ) = \\frac{2B}{1 - B^2}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 100^\\circ = \\frac{2B}{1 - B^2}\n\\]\n\nNow, let's consider the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(70^\\circ) = \\tan(90^\\circ - 20^\\circ) = \\cot(20^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{A}\n\\]\n\nWe know that:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 70^\\circ = \\tan(50^\\circ + 20^\\circ) = \\frac{B + A}{1 - AB}\n\\]\n\nEquating the two expressions for \\(\\tan 70^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{A} = \\frac{B + A}{1 - AB}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n1 - AB = A(B + A)\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\n1 = AB + A^2 + AB\n\\]\n\n\\[\n1 = 2AB + A^2\n\\]\n\nNow, let's solve the original equation:\n\n\\[\nA + 2B = \\tan x^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe can use the tangent addition formula to express this as:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(20^\\circ + 50^\\circ) = \\frac{A + B}{1 - AB}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 70^\\circ = \\frac{A + B}{1 - AB}\n\\]\n\nWe know that:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 70^\\circ = \\frac{1}{A}\n\\]\n\nThus, the smallest positive value of \\(x\\) that satisfies the equation is:\n\n\\[\nx = 70^\\circ\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the smallest positive value of \\(x\\) is \\(\\boxed{70^\\circ}\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to find the areas of triangles \\( \\triangle ADB \\) and \\( \\triangle ABC \\) and then compute their ratio.\n\nFirst, let's find the area of \\( \\triangle ABC \\). We can use Heron's formula to find the area. The side lengths are \\( AB = \\sqrt{30} \\), \\( AC = \\sqrt{6} \\), and \\( BC = \\sqrt{15} \\).\n\nThe semi-perimeter \\( s \\) is given by:\n\\[\ns = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, the area \\( [ABC] \\) is:\n\\[\n[ABC] = \\sqrt{s(s - \\sqrt{30})(s - \\sqrt{6})(s - \\sqrt{15})}\n\\]\n\nCalculating \\( s \\):\n\\[\ns = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, let's calculate each term:\n\\[\ns - \\sqrt{30} = \\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{15} - \\sqrt{30}}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\ns - \\sqrt{6} = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{15} - \\sqrt{6}}{2}\n\\]\n\\[\ns - \\sqrt{15} = \\frac{\\sqrt{30} + \\sqrt{6} - \\sqrt{15}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, let's find the area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\). Since \\( \\overline{AD} \\) bisects \\( \\overline{BC} \\) and \\( \\angle ADB \\) is a right angle, \\( D \\) is the midpoint of \\( \\overline{BC} \\).\n\nThe length of \\( \\overline{BD} = \\overline{DC} = \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2} \\).\n\nSince \\( \\angle ADB = 90^\\circ \\), \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is a right triangle. The area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is:\n\\[\n[ADB] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times AD \\times BD\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean theorem in \\( \\triangle ADB \\):\n\\[\nAD^2 + BD^2 = AB^2\n\\]\n\\[\nAD^2 + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2}\\right)^2 = 30\n\\]\n\\[\nAD^2 + \\frac{15}{4} = 30\n\\]\n\\[\nAD^2 = 30 - \\frac{15}{4} = \\frac{120}{4} - \\frac{15}{4} = \\frac{105}{4}\n\\]\n\\[\nAD = \\frac{\\sqrt{105}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is:\n\\[\n[ADB] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{105}}{2} \\times \\frac{\\sqrt{15}}{2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{105 \\times 15}}{8} = \\frac{\\sqrt{1575}}{8}\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find the ratio:\n\\[\n\\frac{[ADB]}{[ABC]} = \\frac{\\frac{\\sqrt{1575}}{8}}{\\sqrt{s(s - \\sqrt{30})(s - \\sqrt{6})(s - \\sqrt{15})}}\n\\]\n\nHowever, we can simplify this by noting that since \\( \\angle ADB = 90^\\circ \\) and \\( D \\) is the midpoint, the area of \\( \\triangle ADB \\) is half of the area of \\( \\triangle ABC \\).\n\nThus, the ratio is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{1}{2}}\n\\]"
"To solve this problem, we need to consider an isosceles triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) with \\( AB = AC \\) and vertex angle \\( \\angle BAC = \\theta \\). The altitudes intersect on the incircle, which implies a specific geometric configuration.\n\nLet's denote the incenter of the triangle as \\( I \\), and the altitudes from \\( B \\) and \\( C \\) as \\( BD \\) and \\( CE \\), respectively. Since the altitudes intersect on the incircle, the incenter \\( I \\) lies on both altitudes \\( BD \\) and \\( CE \\).\n\nIn an isosceles triangle, the incenter \\( I \\) lies on the angle bisector of the vertex angle \\( \\angle BAC \\). Therefore, \\( I \\) is also the midpoint of the segment joining the feet of the altitudes \\( D \\) and \\( E \\) on \\( AC \\) and \\( AB \\), respectively.\n\nThe condition that the altitudes intersect on the incircle implies that the inradius \\( r \\) is equal to the distance from the incenter \\( I \\) to the base \\( BC \\). This is a special case where the triangle is not only isosceles but also has a specific relationship between its sides and angles.\n\nTo find the cosine of the vertex angle \\( \\theta \\), we can use the fact that the inradius \\( r \\) is given by the formula:\n\\[\nr = \\frac{A}{s}\n\\]\nwhere \\( A \\) is the area of the triangle and \\( s \\) is the semiperimeter.\n\nFor an isosceles triangle with base \\( BC = a \\) and equal sides \\( AB = AC = b \\), the semiperimeter \\( s \\) is:\n\\[\ns = \\frac{a + 2b}{2}\n\\]\n\nThe area \\( A \\) of the triangle can be expressed using the base \\( a \\) and the height \\( h \\) from \\( A \\) to \\( BC \\):\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} a h\n\\]\n\nThe height \\( h \\) can be found using the Pythagorean theorem in the right triangle formed by the altitude:\n\\[\nh = b \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area \\( A \\) becomes:\n\\[\nA = \\frac{1}{2} a b \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2}\n\\]\n\nThe inradius \\( r \\) is then:\n\\[\nr = \\frac{\\frac{1}{2} a b \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2}}{\\frac{a + 2b}{2}} = \\frac{a b \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2}}{a + 2b}\n\\]\n\nSince the altitudes intersect on the incircle, the inradius \\( r \\) is also equal to the height from the incenter to the base \\( BC \\), which is:\n\\[\nr = b \\cos \\frac{\\theta}{2}\n\\]\n\nEquating the two expressions for \\( r \\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{a b \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2}}{a + 2b} = b \\cos \\frac{\\theta}{2}\n\\]\n\nDividing both sides by \\( b \\) and simplifying, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{a \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2}}{a + 2b} = \\cos \\frac{\\theta}{2}\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\( \\sin \\frac{\\theta}{2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1 - \\cos \\theta}{2}} \\) and \\( \\cos \\frac{\\theta}{2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1 + \\cos \\theta}{2}} \\), we can solve for \\( \\cos \\theta \\).\n\nAfter simplifying, we find that:\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the cosine of the vertex angle is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{3}}\\)."
'To find the ordered pair \\((a, b)\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{R} = a \\mathbf{P} + b \\mathbf{I}\\), where \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) is the projection matrix onto a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{R}\\) is the reflection matrix over the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\), we start by understanding the forms of these matrices.\n\nThe projection matrix \\(\\mathbf{P}\\) onto a vector \\(\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} v_1 \\\\ v_2 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{P} = \\frac{1}{v_1^2 + v_2^2} \\begin{pmatrix} v_1^2 & v_1 v_2 \\\\ v_1 v_2 & v_2^2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThe reflection matrix \\(\\mathbf{R}\\) over the vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) is given by:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{R} = 2\\mathbf{P} - \\mathbf{I},\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\mathbf{I}\\) is the identity matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 & 0 \\\\ 0 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nWe want to express \\(\\mathbf{R}\\) in the form \\(a \\mathbf{P} + b \\mathbf{I}\\). Comparing this with the expression for \\(\\mathbf{R}\\), we have:\n\\[\n2\\mathbf{P} - \\mathbf{I} = a \\mathbf{P} + b \\mathbf{I}.\n\\]\n\nBy equating coefficients, we find:\n- For \\(\\mathbf{P}\\): \\(a = 2\\),\n- For \\(\\mathbf{I}\\): \\(b = -1\\).\n\nThus, the ordered pair \\((a, b)\\) is \\(\\boxed{(2, -1)}\\).'
"In right triangle \\( DEF \\), with \\(\\angle E = 90^\\circ\\), we know that \\(\\angle D + \\angle F = 90^\\circ\\). We are given that \\(\\tan D = 3\\sin D\\).\n\nLet's express \\(\\tan D\\) and \\(\\sin D\\) in terms of the sides of the triangle. Let \\( DE = a \\), \\( EF = b \\), and \\( DF = c \\) (the hypotenuse).\n\n\\[\n\\tan D = \\frac{DE}{EF} = \\frac{a}{b}\n\\]\n\\[\n\\sin D = \\frac{DE}{DF} = \\frac{a}{c}\n\\]\n\nThe given equation is:\n\\[\n\\tan D = 3\\sin D \\implies \\frac{a}{b} = 3 \\cdot \\frac{a}{c}\n\\]\n\nAssuming \\( a \\neq 0 \\), we can divide both sides by \\( a \\):\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{b} = \\frac{3}{c}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\\[\nc = 3b\n\\]\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\sin F\\). Since \\(\\angle F = 90^\\circ - \\angle D\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\sin F = \\cos D = \\frac{EF}{DF} = \\frac{b}{c}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\( c = 3b \\) into the expression for \\(\\sin F\\):\n\\[\n\\sin F = \\frac{b}{3b} = \\frac{1}{3}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\sin F\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{1}{3}}\\)."
'To compute \\(\\tan 22.5^\\circ\\), we can use the half-angle identity for tangent. The half-angle identity is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\tan \\frac{\\theta}{2} = \\frac{1 - \\cos \\theta}{\\sin \\theta}\n\\]\n\nFor \\(\\theta = 45^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 22.5^\\circ = \\tan \\frac{45^\\circ}{2}\n\\]\n\nWe know that \\(\\cos 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\) and \\(\\sin 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\). Plugging these into the half-angle identity, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 22.5^\\circ = \\frac{1 - \\cos 45^\\circ}{\\sin 45^\\circ} = \\frac{1 - \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}{\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 22.5^\\circ = \\frac{2 - \\sqrt{2}}{\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]\n\nTo rationalize the denominator, multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\(\\sqrt{2}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan 22.5^\\circ = \\frac{(2 - \\sqrt{2})\\sqrt{2}}{2} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{2} - 2}{2} = \\sqrt{2} - 1\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\tan 22.5^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{2} - 1}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\sin \\left( \\tan^{-1} (x) + \\cot^{-1} \\left( \\frac{1}{x} \\right) \\right) = \\frac{1}{3}\\), we start by simplifying the expression inside the sine function.\n\nFirst, note that \\(\\tan^{-1}(x)\\) is the angle \\(\\theta\\) such that \\(\\tan(\\theta) = x\\). Similarly, \\(\\cot^{-1}\\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right)\\) is the angle \\(\\phi\\) such that \\(\\cot(\\phi) = \\frac{1}{x}\\). Since \\(\\cot(\\phi) = \\frac{1}{\\tan(\\phi)}\\), we have \\(\\tan(\\phi) = x\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\tan^{-1}(x)\\) and \\(\\cot^{-1}\\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right)\\) are actually the same angle, meaning \\(\\theta = \\phi\\). Therefore, the expression simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\sin \\left( \\tan^{-1}(x) + \\cot^{-1}\\left(\\frac{1}{x}\\right) \\right) = \\sin(2\\theta)\n\\]\nwhere \\(\\theta = \\tan^{-1}(x)\\).\n\nUsing the double angle identity for sine, we have:\n\\[\n\\sin(2\\theta) = 2 \\sin(\\theta) \\cos(\\theta)\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\theta = \\tan^{-1}(x)\\), we can express \\(\\sin(\\theta)\\) and \\(\\cos(\\theta)\\) in terms of \\(x\\):\n- \\(\\sin(\\theta) = \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}}\\)\n- \\(\\cos(\\theta) = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}}\\)\n\nThus:\n\\[\n\\sin(2\\theta) = 2 \\cdot \\frac{x}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}} \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{1 + x^2}} = \\frac{2x}{1 + x^2}\n\\]\n\nSetting this equal to \\(\\frac{1}{3}\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{2x}{1 + x^2} = \\frac{1}{3}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\\[\n6x = 1 + x^2\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we get:\n\\[\nx^2 - 6x + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation, which we can solve using the quadratic formula:\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\nwhere \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = -6\\), and \\(c = 1\\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\\[\nx = \\frac{6 \\pm \\sqrt{36 - 4}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\pm \\sqrt{32}}{2} = \\frac{6 \\pm 4\\sqrt{2}}{2} = 3 \\pm 2\\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the solutions are \\(x = 3 + 2\\sqrt{2}\\) and \\(x = 3 - 2\\sqrt{2}\\). Thus, the solutions to the equation are:\n\\[\n\\boxed{3 + 2\\sqrt{2}, 3 - 2\\sqrt{2}}\n\\]'
'To solve this problem, we need to find \\(\\cos^2 \\angle GOM\\), where \\(G\\) is a vertex of a regular heptagon \\(ABCDEFG\\) with center \\(O\\), and \\(M\\) is the centroid of triangle \\(ABD\\).\n\nFirst, note that in a regular heptagon, the central angle between any two consecutive vertices is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{7}\\).
