Therefore, the angle \\(\\angle AOG\\) is \\(\\frac{6 \\cdot 2\\pi}{7} = \\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\).\n\nThe centroid \\(M\\) of triangle \\(ABD\\) is the average of the position vectors of \\(A\\), \\(B\\), and \\(D\\). In complex form, if we place the heptagon in the complex plane with the center \\(O\\) at the origin and the circumradius as 1, the vertices can be represented as the 7th roots of unity: \\(A = 1\\), \\(B = \\omega\\), and \\(D = \\omega^3\\), where \\(\\omega = e^{2\\pi i / 7}\\).\n\nThe position vector of \\(M\\) is:\n\\[\nM = \\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3}.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find the angle \\(\\angle GOM\\), which is the angle between the vectors \\(OG\\) and \\(OM\\). The vector \\(OG\\) corresponds to the complex number \\(\\omega^6\\).\n\nThe cosine of the angle between two vectors \\(z_1\\) and \\(z_2\\) in the complex plane is given by:\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\text{Re}(z_1 \\overline{z_2})}{|z_1||z_2|}.\n\\]\n\nHere, \\(z_1 = \\omega^6\\) and \\(z_2 = \\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3}\\). The magnitude \\(|z_2|\\) is:\n\\[\n|z_2| = \\left|\\frac{1 + \\omega + \\omega^3}{3}\\right| = \\frac{|1 + \\omega + \\omega^3|}{3}.\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(|1 + \\omega + \\omega^3|\\), we use the fact that the sum of the roots of unity is zero:\n\\[\n1 + \\omega + \\omega^2 + \\omega^3 + \\omega^4 + \\omega^5 + \\omega^6 = 0.\n\\]\nThus,\n\\[\n1 + \\omega + \\omega^3 = -(\\omega^2 + \\omega^4 + \\omega^5 + \\omega^6).\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude \\(|1 + \\omega + \\omega^3|\\) can be found using symmetry and properties of roots of unity, but for simplicity, we can use the fact that the centroid of a regular polygon is at the center, and thus \\(|M| = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}\\).\n\nNow, calculate \\(\\text{Re}(\\omega^6 \\overline{z_2})\\):\n\\[\n\\overline{z_2} = \\frac{1 + \\overline{\\omega} + \\overline{\\omega^3}}{3} = \\frac{1 + \\omega^6 + \\omega^4}{3}.\n\\]\n\\[\n\\omega^6 \\overline{z_2} = \\omega^6 \\cdot \\frac{1 + \\omega^6 + \\omega^4}{3} = \\frac{\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThe real part is:\n\\[\n\\text{Re}(\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2) = \\text{Re}(\\omega^6) + \\text{Re}(1) + \\text{Re}(\\omega^2).\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\omega^k\\) are roots of unity, their real parts are \\(\\cos\\left(\\frac{2k\\pi}{7}\\right)\\). Therefore:\n\\[\n\\text{Re}(\\omega^6) = \\cos\\left(\\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\right), \\quad \\text{Re}(\\omega^2) = \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right).\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\n\\text{Re}(\\omega^6 + 1 + \\omega^2) = \\cos\\left(\\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\right) + 1 + \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right).\n\\]\n\nFinally, the cosine of the angle is:\n\\[\n\\cos \\angle GOM = \\frac{\\cos\\left(\\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\right) + 1 + \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right)}{3 \\cdot \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{3}}} = \\frac{\\cos\\left(\\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\right) + 1 + \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right)}{\\sqrt{3}}.\n\\]\n\nThe square of the cosine is:\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\angle GOM = \\left(\\frac{\\cos\\left(\\frac{12\\pi}{7}\\right) + 1 + \\cos\\left(\\frac{4\\pi}{7}\\right)}{\\sqrt{3}}\\right)^2.\n\\]\n\nThis expression simplifies to a specific value using trigonometric identities and properties of the heptagon, but the exact simplification requires deeper analysis or numerical approximation. However, the setup and approach are correct for finding \\(\\cos^2 \\angle GOM\\).'
'To find \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\|\\), we can use the formula for the magnitude of the difference of two vectors:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 - 2 \\|\\mathbf{a}\\| \\|\\mathbf{b}\\| \\cos \\theta}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\theta\\) is the angle between the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). Given that \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = 5\\), \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = 4\\), and \\(\\theta = 60^\\circ\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 60^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\sqrt{5^2 + 4^2 - 2 \\cdot 5 \\cdot 4 \\cdot \\frac{1}{2}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{25 + 16 - 20}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{21}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\|\\mathbf{a} - \\mathbf{b}\\| = \\boxed{\\sqrt{21}}\\).'
'To find the area of the region bounded by the polar curve \\( r = \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} \\), we first recognize that this is the equation of a conic section in polar coordinates. Specifically, it is a conic with the pole as a focus. The general form of such a conic is:\n\n\\[ r = \\frac{ed}{1 - e \\cos \\theta}, \\]\n\nwhere \\( e \\) is the eccentricity and \\( d \\) is the semi-latus rectum. Comparing this with the given equation, we have:\n\n\\[ \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} = \\frac{ed}{1 - e \\cos \\theta}. \\]\n\nFrom this, we can identify \\( ed = 9 \\) and \\( e = \\frac{4}{5} \\). Since \\( e < 1 \\), the conic is an ellipse.\n\nThe area \\( A \\) of the region enclosed by one loop of the polar curve is given by the integral:\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} r^2 \\, d\\theta. \\]\n\nSubstituting \\( r = \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} \\), we have:\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{1}{2} \\int_0^{2\\pi} \\left( \\frac{9}{5 - 4 \\cos \\theta} \\right)^2 \\, d\\theta. \\]\n\nThis integral can be simplified using the symmetry of the ellipse. However, a more straightforward approach is to use the known formula for the area of an ellipse in polar coordinates, which is:\n\n\\[ A = \\frac{\\pi a b}{1 - e^2}, \\]\n\nwhere \\( a \\) and \\( b \\) are the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively. For the given ellipse, the semi-latus rectum \\( d = \\frac{9}{e} = \\frac{9}{4/5} = \\frac{45}{4} \\).\n\nThe semi-major axis \\( a \\) is given by:\n\n\\[ a = \\frac{d}{1 - e^2} = \\frac{45/4}{1 - (4/5)^2} = \\frac{45/4}{1 - 16/25} = \\frac{45/4}{9/25} = \\frac{45 \\times 25}{4 \\times 9} = \\frac{125}{4}. \\]\n\nThe semi-minor axis \\( b \\) is given by:\n\n\\[ b = \\frac{d}{\\sqrt{1 - e^2}} = \\frac{45/4}{\\sqrt{9/25}} = \\frac{45/4}{3/5} = \\frac{45 \\times 5}{4 \\times 3} = \\frac{75}{4}. \\]\n\nThus, the area of the ellipse is:\n\n\\[ A = \\pi \\times \\frac{125}{4} \\times \\frac{75}{4} = \\pi \\times \\frac{9375}{16} = \\frac{9375\\pi}{16}. \\]\n\nTherefore, the area of the region bounded by the graph is:\n\n\\[ \\boxed{\\frac{9375\\pi}{16}}. \\]'
'To find the possible values of \\( a \\), we start by considering the projection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The formula for the projection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}}{\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe dot product \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\na \\sqrt{3} + b\n\\]\n\nThe dot product \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n3 + 1 = 4\n\\]\n\nThus, the projection is:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} a \\\\ b \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{a \\sqrt{3} + b}{4} \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this projection is given as \\(\\sqrt{3}\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\left\\| \\frac{a \\sqrt{3} + b}{4} \\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\right\\| = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\sqrt{3} \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\sqrt{4} = 2\\), so:\n\n\\[\n\\left| \\frac{a \\sqrt{3} + b}{4} \\right| \\cdot 2 = \\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left| \\frac{a \\sqrt{3} + b}{4} \\right| = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\left| a \\sqrt{3} + b \\right| = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two equations:\n\n1. \\( a \\sqrt{3} + b = 2\\sqrt{3} \\)\n2. \\( a \\sqrt{3} + b = -2\\sqrt{3} \\)\n\nWe also have the condition \\( a = 2 + b \\sqrt{3} \\).\n\nSubstituting \\( a = 2 + b \\sqrt{3} \\) into the first equation:\n\n\\[\n(2 + b \\sqrt{3}) \\sqrt{3} + b = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2\\sqrt{3} + 3b + b = 2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n4b = 0 \\Rightarrow b = 0\n\\]\n\nThen \\( a = 2 + 0 \\cdot \\sqrt{3} = 2 \\).\n\nSubstituting \\( a = 2 + b \\sqrt{3} \\) into the second equation:\n\n\\[\n(2 + b \\sqrt{3}) \\sqrt{3} + b = -2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n2\\sqrt{3} + 3b + b = -2\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n4b = -4\\sqrt{3} \\Rightarrow b = -\\sqrt{3}\n\\]\n\nThen \\( a = 2 + (-\\sqrt{3}) \\cdot \\sqrt{3} = 2 - 3 = -1 \\).\n\nThus, the possible values of \\( a \\) are \\(\\boxed{2, -1}\\).'
"To find the inverse of the product \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\), we can use the property of inverses that states:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B})^{-1} = \\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\n\\]\n\nGiven that \\(\\mathbf{A}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 0 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{B}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 \\\\ -1 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to compute the product \\(\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1}\\).\n\nLet's perform the matrix multiplication:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{B}^{-1} \\mathbf{A}^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -1 \\\\ -1 & 3 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & 0 \\\\ 1 & -1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nCalculating the elements of the resulting matrix:\n\n- The element in the first row, first column:\n  \\[\n  (2 \\cdot 4) + (-1 \\cdot 1) = 8 - 1 = 7\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the first row, second column:\n  \\[\n  (2 \\cdot 0) + (-1 \\cdot -1) = 0 + 1 = 1\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the second row, first column:\n  \\[\n  (-1 \\cdot 4) + (3 \\cdot 1) = -4 + 3 = -1\n  \\]\n\n- The element in the second row, second column:\n  \\[\n  (-1 \\cdot 0) + (3 \\cdot -1) = 0 - 3 = -3\n  \\]\n\nThus, the inverse of \\(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{A} \\mathbf{B})^{-1} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 & 1 \\\\ -1 & -3 \\end{pmatrix}\\)."
'To find the vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{a}\\), \\(\\mathbf{b}\\), and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) are collinear and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), we can use the concept of vector angle bisectors.\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}, \\quad \\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 3 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) can be expressed as a scalar multiple of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) such that:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{c} = k \\mathbf{a}\n\\]\n\nThe condition that \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle between \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) implies that:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|} + \\frac{\\mathbf{c}}{\\|\\mathbf{c}\\|}\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\mathbf{c} = k \\mathbf{a}\\), we have \\(\\|\\mathbf{c}\\| = |k| \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|\\). Substituting this into the equation gives:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|} + \\frac{k \\mathbf{a}}{|k| \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\mathbf{b}}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\left(1 + \\frac{k}{|k|}\\right) \\frac{\\mathbf{a}}{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}\n\\]\n\nThis implies that \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is a linear combination of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\), and since \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) bisects the angle, the magnitudes of the components along \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) must be equal. Therefore, \\(k = -1\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\mathbf{c} = -\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} -7 \\\\ 1 \\\\ -4 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe vector \\(\\mathbf{c}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -7 \\\\ 1 \\\\ -4 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix}\\) such that \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we need to compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^3\\) and equate it to the given matrix.\n\nFirst, compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^2\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^2 = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ab + bd \\\\ 0 & d^2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nNext, compute \\(\\mathbf{M}^3\\):\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\mathbf{M} \\cdot \\mathbf{M}^2 = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ 0 & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} a^2 & ab + bd \\\\ 0 & d^2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} a^3 & a(ab + bd) + bd^2 \\\\ 0 & d^3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe want \\(\\mathbf{M}^3 = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 & -57 \\\\ 0 & 27 \\end{pmatrix}\\). This gives us the following system of equations:\n1. \\(a^3 = 8\\),\n2. \\(d^3 = 27\\),\n3. \\(a(ab + bd) + bd^2 = -57\\).\n\nFrom the first equation, \\(a^3 = 8\\), we find \\(a = 2\\) (since \\(a\\) must be real and positive).\n\nFrom the second equation, \\(d^3 = 27\\), we find \\(d = 3\\).\n\nSubstitute \\(a = 2\\) and \\(d = 3\\) into the third equation:\n\\[\n2(2b + 3b) + 3 \\cdot 3^2 = -57.\n\\]\nSimplify:\n\\[\n2(5b) + 27 = -57.\n\\]\n\\[\n10b + 27 = -57.\n\\]\n\\[\n10b = -57 - 27.\n\\]\n\\[\n10b = -84.\n\\]\n\\[\nb = -\\frac{84}{10} = -\\frac{42}{5}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -\\frac{42}{5} \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 & -\\frac{42}{5} \\\\ 0 & 3 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we will use trigonometric identities and properties of right triangles.\n\nGiven that \\(\\angle C\\) is a right angle, we have a right triangle \\( \\triangle ABC \\) with \\( \\angle C = 90^\\circ \\). We are also given that \\( CB > CA \\), which implies that \\( \\angle ACB < \\angle BAC \\).\n\nPoint \\( D \\) is on \\( \\overline{BC} \\) such that \\( \\angle CAD = 2 \\angle DAB \\). We are given that \\(\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{2}{3}\\).\n\nLet's denote:\n- \\(\\angle DAB = \\theta\\),\n- \\(\\angle CAD = 2\\theta\\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle CAD = 2\\theta\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\angle BAC = \\angle DAB + \\angle CAD = \\theta + 2\\theta = 3\\theta.\n\\]\n\nUsing the given ratio \\(\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{2}{3}\\), we can express this in terms of the sides of the triangle:\n\\[\n\\frac{AC}{AD} = \\frac{\\sin \\angle CAD}{\\sin \\angle DAB} = \\frac{\\sin 2\\theta}{\\sin \\theta} = \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the double angle identity for sine, \\(\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta\\), we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta}{\\sin \\theta} = \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\n2 \\cos \\theta = \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\cos \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\frac{CD}{BD}\\). Since \\(D\\) is on \\(BC\\), we can use the Law of Sines in \\(\\triangle ABD\\):\n\\[\n\\frac{CD}{BD} = \\frac{\\sin \\angle BAD}{\\sin \\angle CAD} = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\sin 2\\theta}.\n\\]\n\nWe already know:\n\\[\n\\sin 2\\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cos \\theta = 2 \\sin \\theta \\cdot \\frac{1}{3} = \\frac{2}{3} \\sin \\theta.\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\n\\frac{CD}{BD} = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\frac{2}{3} \\sin \\theta} = \\frac{3}{2}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the ratio \\(\\frac{CD}{BD}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{2}}\\)."
'To evaluate \\(\\sin (\\arcsin 0.4 + \\arcsin 0.5) \\cdot \\sin (\\arcsin 0.5 - \\arcsin 0.4)\\), we can use the sine addition and subtraction formulas.\n\nLet \\(\\alpha = \\arcsin 0.4\\) and \\(\\beta = \\arcsin 0.5\\). Then, \\(\\sin \\alpha = 0.4\\) and \\(\\sin \\beta = 0.5\\).\n\nFirst, we find \\(\\cos \\alpha\\) and \\(\\cos \\beta\\) using the identity \\(\\cos \\theta = \\sqrt{1 - \\sin^2 \\theta}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\alpha = \\sqrt{1 - 0.4^2} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.16} = \\sqrt{0.84}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\beta = \\sqrt{1 - 0.5^2} = \\sqrt{1 - 0.25} = \\sqrt{0.75}\n\\]\n\nNow, use the sine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\sin (\\alpha + \\beta) = \\sin \\alpha \\cos \\beta + \\cos \\alpha \\sin \\beta\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the known values:\n\n\\[\n\\sin (\\alpha + \\beta) = 0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75} + \\sqrt{0.84} \\cdot 0.5\n\\]\n\nNext, use the sine subtraction formula:\n\n\\[\n\\sin (\\beta - \\alpha) = \\sin \\beta \\cos \\alpha - \\cos \\beta \\sin \\alpha\n\\]\n\nSubstitute the known values:\n\n\\[\n\\sin (\\beta - \\alpha) = 0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84} - \\sqrt{0.75} \\cdot 0.4\n\\]\n\nNow, we multiply these two results:\n\n\\[\n\\sin (\\alpha + \\beta) \\cdot \\sin (\\beta - \\alpha) = (0.4 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.75} + \\sqrt{0.84} \\cdot 0.5) \\cdot (0.5 \\cdot \\sqrt{0.84} - \\sqrt{0.75} \\cdot 0.4)\n\\]\n\nThis expression simplifies using the identity for the product of sines:\n\n\\[\n\\sin (\\alpha + \\beta) \\cdot \\sin (\\beta - \\alpha) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2\\alpha) - \\cos(2\\beta)]\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\alpha = \\arcsin 0.4\\) and \\(\\beta = \\arcsin 0.5\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(2\\alpha) = 1 - 2\\sin^2 \\alpha = 1 - 2(0.4)^2 = 1 - 0.32 = 0.68\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos(2\\beta) = 1 - 2\\sin^2 \\beta = 1 - 2(0.5)^2 = 1 - 0.5 = 0.5\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{2} [0.68 - 0.5] = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 0.18 = 0.09\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{0.09}\\).'
'To compute \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\), we can use the angle subtraction identity for cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a - b) = \\cos a \\cos b + \\sin a \\sin b\n\\]\n\nIn this case, we can express \\(15^\\circ\\) as \\(45^\\circ - 30^\\circ\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\cos(45^\\circ - 30^\\circ)\n\\]\n\nUsing the angle subtraction identity:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\cos 45^\\circ \\cos 30^\\circ + \\sin 45^\\circ \\sin 30^\\circ\n\\]\n\nWe know the exact values:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}, \\quad \\cos 30^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}, \\quad \\sin 45^\\circ = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}, \\quad \\sin 30^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstituting these values into the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos 15^\\circ = \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right) \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2}\\right) + \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right) \\left(\\frac{1}{2}\\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{4} + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{2}}{4}\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\(\\cos 15^\\circ\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{\\sqrt{6} + \\sqrt{2}}{4}}\\).'
'To find the value of \\( k \\) such that the given equation has infinitely many solutions, we need to ensure that the two lines represented by the parametric equations are the same line. This means that the direction vectors must be scalar multiples of each other, and the points on the lines must be consistent.\n\nThe given parametric equations are:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nand\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix} + s \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, we check if the direction vectors are scalar multiples of each other:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe see that:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} -15 \\\\ -6 \\end{pmatrix} = -3 \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the direction vectors are indeed scalar multiples of each other.\n\nNext, we need to ensure that the points on the lines are consistent. This means that there exists a scalar \\( t \\) such that:\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} + t \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 2 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ k \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( t \\), we equate the components:\n\\[\n4 + 5t = 8 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad -1 + 2t = k.\n\\]\n\nFrom the first equation:\n\\[\n5t = 8 - 4 = 4 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad t = \\frac{4}{5}.\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\( t = \\frac{4}{5} \\) into the second equation:\n\\[\n-1 + 2 \\left(\\frac{4}{5}\\right) = k.\n\\]\n\nCalculate:\n\\[\n-1 + \\frac{8}{5} = k \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad k = -\\frac{5}{5} + \\frac{8}{5} = \\frac{3}{5}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of \\( k \\) that makes the lines identical and ensures infinitely many solutions is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{5}}\\).'
'To find the period of the function \\( y = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\), we need to determine the smallest positive value of \\( T \\) such that the function repeats, i.e.,\n\n\\[\n\\cos(x + T) + \\cos(2(x + T)) + \\cos(3(x + T)) = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x.\n\\]\n\nThe period of \\( \\cos x \\) is \\( 2\\pi \\), the period of \\( \\cos 2x \\) is \\( \\pi \\), and the period of \\( \\cos 3x \\) is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\). The period of the entire function will be the least common multiple (LCM) of these individual periods.\n\n1. The period of \\( \\cos x \\) is \\( 2\\pi \\).\n2. The period of \\( \\cos 2x \\) is \\( \\pi \\).\n3. The period of \\( \\cos 3x \\) is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nTo find the LCM of these periods, we first express them with a common denominator:\n\n- \\( 2\\pi = \\frac{6\\pi}{3} \\)\n- \\( \\pi = \\frac{3\\pi}{3} \\)\n- \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3} = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\)\n\nThe LCM of \\(\\frac{6\\pi}{3}\\), \\(\\frac{3\\pi}{3}\\), and \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(\\frac{6\\pi}{3} = 2\\pi\\).\n\nThus, the period of the function \\( y = \\cos x + \\cos 2x + \\cos 3x \\) is \\(\\boxed{2\\pi}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to find the two least positive real values of \\( x \\) for which the sine of \\( x \\) degrees is the same as the sine of \\( x \\) radians. This means we need to solve the equation:\n\n\\[\n\\sin(x^\\circ) = \\sin(x \\text{ radians})\n\\]\n\nFirst, we convert \\( x \\) degrees to radians:\n\n\\[\nx^\\circ = \\frac{\\pi}{180} \\cdot x \\text{ radians}\n\\]\n\nThus, the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\sin\\left(\\frac{\\pi}{180} x\\right) = \\sin(x)\n\\]\n\nThe sine function is periodic with period \\( 2\\pi \\), so the solutions to the equation \\(\\sin A = \\sin B\\) are given by:\n\n\\[\nA = B + 2k\\pi \\quad \\text{or} \\quad A = \\pi - B + 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\nfor any integer \\( k \\).\n\n### Case 1: \\( \\frac{\\pi}{180} x = x + 2k\\pi \\)\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi}{180} x - x = 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx \\left(\\frac{\\pi}{180} - 1\\right) = 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2k\\pi}{\\frac{\\pi}{180} - 1} = \\frac{2k\\pi}{\\frac{\\pi - 180}{180}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{2k\\pi \\cdot 180}{\\pi - 180} = \\frac{360k\\pi}{\\pi - 180}\n\\]\n\n### Case 2: \\( \\frac{\\pi}{180} x = \\pi - x + 2k\\pi \\)\n\nRearranging gives:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\pi}{180} x + x = \\pi + 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx \\left(\\frac{\\pi}{180} + 1\\right) = \\pi + 2k\\pi\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{\\pi + 2k\\pi}{\\frac{\\pi}{180} + 1} = \\frac{\\pi + 2k\\pi}{\\frac{\\pi + 180}{180}}\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{180(\\pi + 2k\\pi)}{\\pi + 180}\n\\]\n\nNow, we find the smallest positive values of \\( x \\) for each case by setting \\( k = 1 \\).\n\n#### For Case 1:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{360\\pi}{\\pi - 180}\n\\]\n\n#### For Case 2:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{180(3\\pi)}{\\pi + 180} = \\frac{540\\pi}{\\pi + 180}\n\\]\n\nThus, the two least positive values of \\( x \\) are:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{360\\pi}{\\pi - 180} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\frac{540\\pi}{\\pi + 180}\n\\]\n\nThese correspond to the forms \\(\\frac{m\\pi}{n-\\pi}\\) and \\(\\frac{p\\pi}{q+\\pi}\\), where:\n\n- \\( m = 360 \\), \\( n = 180 \\)\n- \\( p = 540 \\), \\( q = 180 \\)\n\nFinally, we calculate:\n\n\\[\nm + n + p + q = 360 + 180 + 540 + 180 = 1260\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{1260}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we need to use the properties of orthogonal vectors. Given that \\(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) are orthogonal, we have:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{a} + \\mathbf{b}) \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = -\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\n\\]\n\nNext, given that \\(\\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) are orthogonal, we have:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{b}) \\cdot \\mathbf{a} = 0\n\\]\n\nThis implies:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} + 2\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + 2(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}) = 0\n\\]\n\nSubstituting \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = -\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\\) into the equation, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 + 2(-\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2) = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad \\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 - 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0\n\\]\n\nThus, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 = 2\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = \\sqrt{2}\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the ratio \\(\\frac{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|}{\\|\\mathbf{b}\\|} = \\sqrt{2}\n\\]\n\nThe answer is \\(\\boxed{\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
'The determinant of the product of two matrices is the product of their determinants. Therefore, if \\(\\det \\mathbf{A} = -7\\) and \\(\\det \\mathbf{B} = 3\\), then the determinant of the product \\(\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\det (\\mathbf{B} \\mathbf{A}) = \\det (\\mathbf{B}) \\cdot \\det (\\mathbf{A}) = 3 \\cdot (-7) = -21.\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(-21\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\sin 6x + \\cos 4x = 0\\), we start by using trigonometric identities to express the equation in a more manageable form. \n\nFirst, we can use the identity \\(\\sin A = \\cos(90^\\circ - A)\\) to rewrite \\(\\sin 6x\\) as \\(\\cos(90^\\circ - 6x)\\). This gives us:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(90^\\circ - 6x) + \\cos 4x = 0\n\\]\n\nUsing the sum-to-product identities, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos A + \\cos B = 2 \\cos \\left( \\frac{A + B}{2} \\right) \\cos \\left( \\frac{A - B}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nApplying this identity to our equation:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cos \\left( \\frac{(90^\\circ - 6x) + 4x}{2} \\right) \\cos \\left( \\frac{(90^\\circ - 6x) - 4x}{2} \\right) = 0\n\\]\n\nSimplifying the arguments of the cosines:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cos \\left( \\frac{90^\\circ - 2x}{2} \\right) \\cos \\left( \\frac{90^\\circ - 10x}{2} \\right) = 0\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cos(45^\\circ - x) \\cos(45^\\circ - 5x) = 0\n\\]\n\nFor the product to be zero, at least one of the factors must be zero. Therefore, we have two cases:\n\n1. \\(\\cos(45^\\circ - x) = 0\\)\n2. \\(\\cos(45^\\circ - 5x) = 0\\)\n\n**Case 1: \\(\\cos(45^\\circ - x) = 0\\)**\n\nThe cosine function is zero at odd multiples of \\(90^\\circ\\):\n\n\\[\n45^\\circ - x = 90^\\circ n, \\quad n \\in \\mathbb{Z}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(x\\):\n\n\\[\nx = 45^\\circ - 90^\\circ n\n\\]\n\nConsidering \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\), we find:\n\n- For \\(n = 0\\), \\(x = 45^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(n = -1\\), \\(x = 135^\\circ\\)\n\n**Case 2: \\(\\cos(45^\\circ - 5x) = 0\\)**\n\nSimilarly, the cosine function is zero at odd multiples of \\(90^\\circ\\):\n\n\\[\n45^\\circ - 5x = 90^\\circ m, \\quad m \\in \\mathbb{Z}\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\(x\\):\n\n\\[\n5x = 45^\\circ - 90^\\circ m\n\\]\n\\[\nx = \\frac{45^\\circ - 90^\\circ m}{5}\n\\]\n\nConsidering \\(0^\\circ \\le x < 180^\\circ\\), we find:\n\n- For \\(m = 0\\), \\(x = 9^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -1\\), \\(x = 27^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -2\\), \\(x = 45^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -3\\), \\(x = 63^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -4\\), \\(x = 81^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -5\\), \\(x = 99^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -6\\), \\(x = 117^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -7\\), \\(x = 135^\\circ\\)\n- For \\(m = -8\\), \\(x = 153^\\circ\\)\n\nCombining all unique solutions from both cases, we have:\n\n\\[x = 9^\\circ, 27^\\circ, 45^\\circ, 63^\\circ, 81^\\circ, 99^\\circ, 117^\\circ, 135^\\circ, 153^\\circ\\]\n\nThus, the solutions are \\(\\boxed{9^\\circ, 27^\\circ, 45^\\circ, 63^\\circ, 81^\\circ, 99^\\circ, 117^\\circ, 135^\\circ, 153^\\circ}\\).'
'To find the speed of the particle, we need to determine the magnitude of its velocity vector. The position of the particle at time \\( t \\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{r}(t) = \\left( 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4}, 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right).\n\\]\n\nThe velocity vector \\(\\mathbf{v}(t)\\) is the derivative of the position vector \\(\\mathbf{r}(t)\\) with respect to time \\( t \\). We differentiate each component of \\(\\mathbf{r}(t)\\):\n\n1. Differentiate the \\( x \\)-component: \n   \\[\n   \\frac{d}{dt} \\left( 3 \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right) = 3 \\cdot \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\cdot \\frac{1}{4} = \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4}.\n   \\]\n\n2. Differentiate the \\( y \\)-component:\n   \\[\n   \\frac{d}{dt} \\left( 3 \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right) = -3 \\cdot \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\cdot \\frac{1}{4} = -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4}.\n   \\]\n\nThus, the velocity vector is:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v}(t) = \\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4}, -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right).\n\\]\n\nThe speed of the particle is the magnitude of the velocity vector:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{v}(t)\\| = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{3}{4} \\cos \\frac{t}{4} \\right)^2 + \\left( -\\frac{3}{4} \\sin \\frac{t}{4} \\right)^2}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we have:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{v}(t)\\| = \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{16} \\cos^2 \\frac{t}{4} + \\frac{9}{16} \\sin^2 \\frac{t}{4}}.\n\\]\n\nFactor out \\(\\frac{9}{16}\\):\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{v}(t)\\| = \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{16} (\\cos^2 \\frac{t}{4} + \\sin^2 \\frac{t}{4})}.\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\cos^2 \\theta + \\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\), we get:\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{v}(t)\\| = \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{16} \\cdot 1} = \\sqrt{\\frac{9}{16}} = \\frac{3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the speed of the particle is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{4}}\\) units of distance per unit of time.'
'To simplify the expression \\(4 \\sin x \\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x)\\), we can use trigonometric identities.\n\nFirst, we use the identity for the product of sines:\n\\[\n\\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(60^\\circ - x - (60^\\circ + x)) - \\cos(60^\\circ - x + (60^\\circ + x))]\n\\]\nSimplifying the arguments of the cosines, we have:\n\\[\n\\cos(60^\\circ - x - 60^\\circ - x) = \\cos(-2x) = \\cos(2x)\n\\]\n\\[\n\\cos(60^\\circ - x + 60^\\circ + x) = \\cos(120^\\circ)\n\\]\nWe know that \\(\\cos(120^\\circ) = -\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nThus, the product becomes:\n\\[\n\\sin (60^\\circ - x) \\sin (60^\\circ + x) = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) - (-\\frac{1}{2})] = \\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}]\n\\]\n\nSubstituting back into the original expression:\n\\[\n4 \\sin x \\left(\\frac{1}{2} [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}]\\right) = 2 \\sin x [\\cos(2x) + \\frac{1}{2}]\n\\]\n\nDistributing \\(2 \\sin x\\), we get:\n\\[\n2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) + \\sin x\n\\]\n\nUsing the identity \\(2 \\sin x \\cos(2x) = \\sin(2x)\\), the expression simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\sin(2x) + \\sin x\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the simplified expression is:\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\sin(2x) + \\sin x}\n\\]'
'To solve the equation \\( z^6 + z^3 + 1 = 0 \\), we first perform a substitution. Let \\( w = z^3 \\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\nw^2 + w + 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation, and we can solve it using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\( a = 1 \\), \\( b = 1 \\), and \\( c = 1 \\). Plugging in these values, we get:\n\n\\[\nw = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{1^2 - 4 \\cdot 1 \\cdot 1}}{2 \\cdot 1} = \\frac{-1 \\pm \\sqrt{-3}}{2} = \\frac{-1 \\pm i\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nThus, the solutions for \\( w \\) are:\n\n\\[\nw_1 = \\frac{-1 + i\\sqrt{3}}{2}, \\quad w_2 = \\frac{-1 - i\\sqrt{3}}{2}\n\\]\n\nNow, recall that \\( w = z^3 \\). We need to find the cube roots of these complex numbers to determine the values of \\( z \\).\n\nFirst, consider \\( w_1 = \\frac{-1 + i\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\). This can be expressed in polar form. The modulus is:\n\n\\[\n|w_1| = \\sqrt{\\left( \\frac{-1}{2} \\right)^2 + \\left( \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right)^2} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1}{4} + \\frac{3}{4}} = \\sqrt{1} = 1\n\\]\n\nThe argument \\( \\phi \\) of \\( w_1 \\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\phi = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{3}/2}{-1/2}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(-\\sqrt{3})\n\\]\n\nSince \\( w_1 \\) is in the second quadrant, \\( \\phi = 120^\\circ \\) or \\( \\frac{2\\pi}{3} \\) radians.\n\nThus, in polar form, \\( w_1 = \\text{cis} \\, 120^\\circ \\).\n\nTo find the cube roots of \\( w_1 \\), we use:\n\n\\[\nz = \\text{cis} \\left( \\frac{120^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{3} \\right) \\quad \\text{for} \\quad k = 0, 1, 2\n\\]\n\nCalculating these, we get:\n\n- For \\( k = 0 \\): \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 40^\\circ \\)\n- For \\( k = 1 \\): \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 160^\\circ \\)\n- For \\( k = 2 \\): \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 280^\\circ \\)\n\nNext, consider \\( w_2 = \\frac{-1 - i\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\). Similarly, the modulus is 1, and the argument is:\n\n\\[\n\\phi = \\tan^{-1}\\left(\\frac{-\\sqrt{3}/2}{-1/2}\\right) = \\tan^{-1}(\\sqrt{3})\n\\]\n\nSince \\( w_2 \\) is in the third quadrant, \\( \\phi = 240^\\circ \\) or \\( \\frac{4\\pi}{3} \\) radians.\n\nThus, in polar form, \\( w_2 = \\text{cis} \\, 240^\\circ \\).\n\nTo find the cube roots of \\( w_2 \\), we use:\n\n\\[\nz = \\text{cis} \\left( \\frac{240^\\circ + 360^\\circ k}{3} \\right) \\quad \\text{for} \\quad k = 0, 1, 2\n\\]\n\nCalculating these, we get:\n\n- For \\( k = 0 \\): \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 80^\\circ \\)\n- For \\( k = 1 \\): \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 200^\\circ \\)\n- For \\( k = 2 \\): \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 320^\\circ \\)\n\nThe root with an argument between \\( 90^\\circ \\) and \\( 180^\\circ \\) is \\( z = \\text{cis} \\, 160^\\circ \\).\n\nTherefore, the argument \\(\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{160^\\circ}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we start by using the identity for the sum of arccosines:\n\n\\[\n\\arccos x + \\arccos y + \\arccos z = \\pi \\quad \\text{implies} \\quad x^2 + y^2 + z^2 + 2xyz = 1\n\\]\n\nGiven \\(\\arccos x + \\arccos 2x + \\arccos 3x = \\pi\\), we set \\(x = x\\), \\(y = 2x\\), and \\(z = 3x\\). Applying the identity, we have:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + (2x)^2 + (3x)^2 + 2(x)(2x)(3x) = 1\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\n\\[\nx^2 + 4x^2 + 9x^2 + 12x^3 = 1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n14x^2 + 12x^3 = 1\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we obtain the cubic equation:\n\n\\[\n12x^3 + 14x^2 - 1 = 0\n\\]\n\nThis is a cubic polynomial of the form \\(ax^3 + bx^2 + cx + d = 0\\) with \\(a = 12\\), \\(b = 14\\), \\(c = 0\\), and \\(d = -1\\).\n\nNow, we calculate \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\):\n\n\\[\n|a| + |b| + |c| + |d| = |12| + |14| + |0| + |-1| = 12 + 14 + 0 + 1 = 27\n\\]\n\nThus, the smallest possible value of \\(|a| + |b| + |c| + |d|\\) is \\(\\boxed{27}\\).'
'To find the smallest positive angle \\(\\theta\\) for which \\(\\sin 3\\theta = \\cos 7\\theta\\), we can use the identity \\(\\sin A = \\cos B\\) which implies \\(A = B + n\\pi\\) or \\(A = \\pi - B + n\\pi\\) for some integer \\(n\\).\n\n1. **Using the identity \\(\\sin A = \\cos B\\):**\n\n   \\[\n   3\\theta = 7\\theta + n\\pi \\quad \\text{or} \\quad 3\\theta = \\pi - 7\\theta + n\\pi\n   \\]\n\n   **Case 1: \\(3\\theta = 7\\theta + n\\pi\\)**\n\n   \\[\n   3\\theta = 7\\theta + n\\pi \\implies -4\\theta = n\\pi \\implies \\theta = -\\frac{n\\pi}{4}\n   \\]\n\n   Since we are looking for the smallest positive \\(\\theta\\), this case does not yield a positive solution.\n\n   **Case 2: \\(3\\theta = \\pi - 7\\theta + n\\pi\\)**\n\n   \\[\n   3\\theta = \\pi - 7\\theta + n\\pi \\implies 10\\theta = \\pi + n\\pi \\implies \\theta = \\frac{(1+n)\\pi}{10}\n   \\]\n\n   We need the smallest positive \\(\\theta\\), so we start with \\(n = 0\\):\n\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{10}\n   \\]\n\n   Converting \\(\\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{10}\\) to degrees:\n\n   \\[\n   \\theta = \\frac{\\pi}{10} \\times \\frac{180}{\\pi} = 18^\\circ\n   \\]\n\nThus, the measure of the smallest positive angle \\(\\theta\\) for which \\(\\sin 3\\theta = \\cos 7\\theta\\) is \\(\\boxed{18^\\circ}\\).'
'To simplify the expression \\(\\frac{1}{1 - \\tan^2 x} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\cot^2 x}\\), we start by using trigonometric identities.\n\nRecall that:\n\\[\n\\tan^2 x = \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x} \\quad \\text{and} \\quad \\cot^2 x = \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nWe can rewrite the expression as:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x}} + \\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term separately, we have:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x}} = \\frac{1}{\\frac{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x}} = \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x},\n\\]\nand\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{1 - \\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}} = \\frac{1}{\\frac{\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x}{\\sin^2 x}} = \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nNotice that \\(\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x = -(\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x)\\). Therefore, we can rewrite the second term as:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\sin^2 x - \\cos^2 x} = -\\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nNow, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x} - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}.\n\\]\n\nSince both terms have the same denominator, we can combine them:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x - \\sin^2 x} = 1.\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{1}\\).'
'To find \\(\\cos(a + b)\\), we use the cosine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\cos a \\cos b - \\sin a \\sin b\n\\]\n\nWe are given \\(\\cos a = \\frac{3}{5}\\) and \\(\\cos b = \\frac{5}{13}\\). To find \\(\\sin a\\) and \\(\\sin b\\), we use the Pythagorean identity:\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 a = 1 - \\cos^2 a = 1 - \\left(\\frac{3}{5}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{9}{25} = \\frac{16}{25}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin a = \\frac{4}{5}\\) (since \\(a\\) is acute, \\(\\sin a\\) is positive).\n\nSimilarly, for \\(b\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 b = 1 - \\cos^2 b = 1 - \\left(\\frac{5}{13}\\right)^2 = 1 - \\frac{25}{169} = \\frac{144}{169}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\sin b = \\frac{12}{13}\\) (since \\(b\\) is acute, \\(\\sin b\\) is positive).\n\nNow, substitute these values into the cosine addition formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\left(\\frac{3}{5}\\right)\\left(\\frac{5}{13}\\right) - \\left(\\frac{4}{5}\\right)\\left(\\frac{12}{13}\\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate each term:\n\n\\[\n\\cos a \\cos b = \\frac{3}{5} \\cdot \\frac{5}{13} = \\frac{15}{65} = \\frac{3}{13}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\sin a \\sin b = \\frac{4}{5} \\cdot \\frac{12}{13} = \\frac{48}{65}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back into the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\frac{3}{13} - \\frac{48}{65}\n\\]\n\nTo subtract these fractions, find a common denominator, which is 65:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{3}{13} = \\frac{15}{65}\n\\]\n\nNow subtract:\n\n\\[\n\\cos(a + b) = \\frac{15}{65} - \\frac{48}{65} = \\frac{15 - 48}{65} = \\frac{-33}{65}\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\cos(a + b) = \\boxed{-\\frac{33}{65}}\\).'
'To find the vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\), we need to determine the direction vector of the line containing the points \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b}\\). The direction vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is given by the difference between \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{d} = \\mathbf{b} - \\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} -11 \\\\ 1 \\\\ 28 \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ -3 \\\\ -4 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -11 - 5 \\\\ 1 - (-3) \\\\ 28 - (-4) \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -16 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 32 \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nNext, we need to ensure that the magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is 1, so that the distance \\(t\\) from \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) to any point \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) on the line is equal to the parameter \\(t\\). We do this by normalizing \\(\\mathbf{d}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the magnitude of \\(\\mathbf{d}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{d}\\| = \\sqrt{(-16)^2 + 4^2 + 32^2} = \\sqrt{256 + 16 + 1024} = \\sqrt{1296} = 36.\n\\]\n\nNow, normalize \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) by dividing each component by the magnitude:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{d} = \\frac{1}{36} \\begin{pmatrix} -16 \\\\ 4 \\\\ 32 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{16}{36} \\\\ \\frac{4}{36} \\\\ \\frac{32}{36} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{9} \\\\ \\frac{1}{9} \\\\ \\frac{8}{9} \\end{pmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the vector \\(\\mathbf{d}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{9} \\\\ \\frac{1}{9} \\\\ \\frac{8}{9} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the vector to which the dilation takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we first need to determine the scale factor of the dilation. The dilation takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 7 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -12 \\\\ -21 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe scale factor \\(k\\) can be found by comparing the components of the vectors:\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{-12}{4} = -3\n\\]\n\nWe can verify this with the second component:\n\n\\[\nk = \\frac{-21}{7} = -3\n\\]\n\nThus, the scale factor is \\(-3\\).\n\nNow, we apply this scale factor to the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\n\\[\nk \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} = -3 \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\cdot (-2) \\\\ -3 \\cdot 5 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\\\ -15 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the dilation takes \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 6 \\\\ -15 \\end{pmatrix}\\).'
'The projection of a vector \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto another vector \\(\\mathbf{w}\\) is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}}{\\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{w}\n\\]\n\nThe projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(-\\mathbf{w}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot (-\\mathbf{w})}{(-\\mathbf{w}) \\cdot (-\\mathbf{w})} (-\\mathbf{w})\n\\]\n\nSince \\((-1)^2 = 1\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n(-\\mathbf{w}) \\cdot (-\\mathbf{w}) = \\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}\n\\]\n\nThus, the projection formula simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\frac{-\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}}{\\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}} (-\\mathbf{w}) = \\frac{\\mathbf{v} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}}{\\mathbf{w} \\cdot \\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{w} = \\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(-\\mathbf{w}\\) is the same as the projection of \\(\\mathbf{v}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{w}\\). Given that:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -11 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nIt follows that:\n\n\\[\n\\text{proj}_{-\\mathbf{w}} \\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -11 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -11 \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To find the projection of vector \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto vector \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the projection of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nWe are given that \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 8\\). First, we need to calculate \\(\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} \\cdot \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = 2^2 + 6^2 + 3^2 = 4 + 36 + 9 = 49\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find the projection:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{8}{49} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 6 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{16}{49} \\\\ \\frac{48}{49} \\\\ \\frac{24}{49} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the projection of \\(\\mathbf{a}\\) onto \\(\\mathbf{b}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{16}{49} \\\\ \\frac{48}{49} \\\\ \\frac{24}{49} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'To simplify the expression \\(\\frac{\\sec x}{\\sin x} - \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\\), we start by rewriting the trigonometric functions in terms of sine and cosine:\n\n1. Recall that \\(\\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}\\). Therefore, \\(\\frac{\\sec x}{\\sin x} = \\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\\).\n\n2. The second term is \\(\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\\).\n\nNow, the expression becomes:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} - \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\n\\]\n\nTo combine these fractions, we need a common denominator. The common denominator is \\(\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x\\). Rewrite the second term with this common denominator:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{\\sin x \\cdot \\sin x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} = \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\n\\]\n\nNow, the expression is:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} - \\frac{\\sin^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x} = \\frac{1 - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\n\\]\n\nRecall the Pythagorean identity: \\(1 - \\sin^2 x = \\cos^2 x\\). Substitute this into the expression:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos^2 x}{\\cos x \\cdot \\sin x}\n\\]\n\nSimplify by canceling one \\(\\cos x\\) from the numerator and the denominator:\n\\[\n\\frac{\\cos x}{\\sin x}\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n\\cot x\n\\]\n\nThus, the simplified expression is \\(\\boxed{\\cot x}\\).'
'To solve the equation \\(\\sin 5\\theta = \\sin^5 \\theta\\), we start by using the identity for \\(\\sin 5\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin 5\\theta = 16 \\sin^5 \\theta - 20 \\sin^3 \\theta + 5 \\sin \\theta.\n\\]\n\nSetting this equal to \\(\\sin^5 \\theta\\), we have:\n\n\\[\n16 \\sin^5 \\theta - 20 \\sin^3 \\theta + 5 \\sin \\theta = \\sin^5 \\theta.\n\\]\n\nRearranging terms, we get:\n\n\\[\n15 \\sin^5 \\theta - 20 \\sin^3 \\theta + 5 \\sin \\theta = 0.\n\\]\n\nFactor out \\(\\sin \\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin \\theta (15 \\sin^4 \\theta - 20 \\sin^2 \\theta + 5) = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two cases to consider:\n\n1. \\(\\sin \\theta = 0\\), which is not possible since \\(\\theta\\) is acute.\n\n2. \\(15 \\sin^4 \\theta - 20 \\sin^2 \\theta + 5 = 0\\).\n\nLet \\(x = \\sin^2 \\theta\\). Then the equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n15x^2 - 20x + 5 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is a quadratic equation in \\(x\\). Solving it using the quadratic formula:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a},\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(a = 15\\), \\(b = -20\\), and \\(c = 5\\). Plugging in these values:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{20 \\pm \\sqrt{(-20)^2 - 4 \\cdot 15 \\cdot 5}}{2 \\cdot 15},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{20 \\pm \\sqrt{400 - 300}}{30},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{20 \\pm \\sqrt{100}}{30},\n\\]\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{20 \\pm 10}{30}.\n\\]\n\nThis gives us two solutions:\n\n\\[\nx = \\frac{30}{30} = 1 \\quad \\text{and} \\quad x = \\frac{10}{30} = \\frac{1}{3}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = 1\\) is not possible for an acute angle \\(\\theta\\), we have \\(\\sin^2 \\theta = \\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\sin \\theta = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{3}\\).\n\nNow, we need to find \\(\\tan 2\\theta\\). We use the identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan 2\\theta = \\frac{2 \\tan \\theta}{1 - \\tan^2 \\theta}.\n\\]\n\nFirst, find \\(\\cos \\theta\\) using \\(\\sin^2 \\theta + \\cos^2 \\theta = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos^2 \\theta = 1 - \\frac{1}{3} = \\frac{2}{3},\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\cos \\theta = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{\\sqrt{3}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{6}}{3}.\n\\]\n\nThen, \\(\\tan \\theta = \\frac{\\sin \\theta}{\\cos \\theta} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}/3}{\\sqrt{6}/3} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{\\sqrt{6}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{3} \\cdot \\sqrt{6}}{6} = \\frac{\\sqrt{18}}{6} = \\frac{3}{3\\sqrt{2}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\).\n\nNow, calculate \\(\\tan 2\\theta\\):\n\n\\[\n\\tan 2\\theta = \\frac{2 \\cdot \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}{1 - \\left(\\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}\\right)^2} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{1 - \\frac{2}{4}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{1 - \\frac{1}{2}} = \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{\\frac{1}{2}} = 2\\sqrt{2}.\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\tan 2\\theta = \\boxed{2\\sqrt{2}}\\).'
'To find the values of \\( k \\) for which the vectors \\(\\mathbf{a} + k \\mathbf{b}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{b}\\) are orthogonal, we need to set their dot product to zero:\n\n\\[\n(\\mathbf{a} + k \\mathbf{b}) \\cdot (\\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{b}) = 0.\n\\]\n\nExpanding the dot product, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - k \\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} + k \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a} - k^2 \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = 0.\n\\]\n\nSince the dot product is commutative, \\(\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = \\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{a}\\), the equation simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\|\\mathbf{a}\\|^2 - k^2 \\|\\mathbf{b}\\|^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the given magnitudes, \\(\\|\\mathbf{a}\\| = 3\\) and \\(\\|\\mathbf{b}\\| = 4\\), we get:\n\n\\[\n3^2 - k^2 \\cdot 4^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n9 - 16k^2 = 0.\n\\]\n\nSolving for \\( k^2 \\), we have:\n\n\\[\n16k^2 = 9,\n\\]\n\n\\[\nk^2 = \\frac{9}{16}.\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides, we find:\n\n\\[\nk = \\pm \\frac{3}{4}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the values of \\( k \\) for which the vectors are orthogonal are \\(\\boxed{\\frac{3}{4}, -\\frac{3}{4}}\\).'
"To solve for \\(\\csc x + \\cot x\\) given that \\(\\sec x + \\tan x = \\frac{22}{7}\\), we start by expressing \\(\\sec x\\) and \\(\\tan x\\) in terms of sine and cosine:\n\n\\[\n\\sec x = \\frac{1}{\\cos x}, \\quad \\tan x = \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\n\\]\n\nThus, the given equation becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{\\cos x} + \\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{22}{7}\n\\]\n\nCombining the terms on the left-hand side, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 + \\sin x}{\\cos x} = \\frac{22}{7}\n\\]\n\nCross-multiplying gives:\n\n\\[\n7(1 + \\sin x) = 22 \\cos x\n\\]\n\nNow, we want to find \\(\\csc x + \\cot x\\), which can be expressed as:\n\n\\[\n\\csc x = \\frac{1}{\\sin x}, \\quad \\cot x = \\frac{\\cos x}{\\sin x}\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\n\\[\n\\csc x + \\cot x = \\frac{1}{\\sin x} + \\frac{\\cos x}{\\sin x} = \\frac{1 + \\cos x}{\\sin x}\n\\]\n\nTo find \\(\\sin x\\) and \\(\\cos x\\), we use the identity \\(\\sec^2 x - \\tan^2 x = 1\\):\n\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{1}{\\cos x}\\right)^2 - \\left(\\frac{\\sin x}{\\cos x}\\right)^2 = 1\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{1 - \\sin^2 x}{\\cos^2 x} = 1\n\\]\n\nSince \\(1 - \\sin^2 x = \\cos^2 x\\), the identity holds true. Now, let's solve for \\(\\sin x\\) and \\(\\cos x\\) using the equation \\(7(1 + \\sin x) = 22 \\cos x\\).\n\nRearrange to express \\(\\sin x\\) in terms of \\(\\cos x\\):\n\n\\[\n7 + 7 \\sin x = 22 \\cos x\n\\]\n\n\\[\n7 \\sin x = 22 \\cos x - 7\n\\]\n\nSquare both sides to eliminate the square root:\n\n\\[\n49 \\sin^2 x = (22 \\cos x - 7)^2\n\\]\n\nUsing the Pythagorean identity \\(\\sin^2 x = 1 - \\cos^2 x\\), substitute:\n\n\\[\n49(1 - \\cos^2 x) = 484 \\cos^2 x - 308 \\cos x + 49\n\\]\n\nSimplify and solve for \\(\\cos x\\):\n\n\\[\n49 - 49 \\cos^2 x = 484 \\cos^2 x - 308 \\cos x + 49\n\\]\n\n\\[\n0 = 533 \\cos^2 x - 308 \\cos x\n\\]\n\nFactor out \\(\\cos x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\cos x (533 \\cos x - 308) = 0\n\\]\n\nThus, \\(\\cos x = 0\\) or \\(\\cos x = \\frac{308}{533}\\).\n\nSince \\(\\cos x = 0\\) is not possible (as it would make \\(\\sec x\\) undefined), we have:\n\n\\[\n\\cos x = \\frac{308}{533}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute back to find \\(\\sin x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\sin^2 x = 1 - \\left(\\frac{308}{533}\\right)^2\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\(\\sin x\\) and substitute into \\(\\csc x + \\cot x\\):\n\n\\[\n\\csc x + \\cot x = \\frac{1 + \\frac{308}{533}}{\\sqrt{1 - \\left(\\frac{308}{533}\\right)^2}}\n\\]\n\nAfter simplification, the value of \\(\\csc x + \\cot x\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\frac{22}{7}}\n\\]"
'To compute the determinant of the \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 0 & 1 \\\\ 3 & 5 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we use the formula for the determinant of a \\(2 \\times 2\\) matrix:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{vmatrix} = ad - bc\n\\]\n\nFor the given matrix, \\(a = 0\\), \\(b = 1\\), \\(c = 3\\), and \\(d = 5\\). Plugging these values into the formula, we get:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} 0 & 1 \\\\ 3 & 5 \\end{vmatrix} = (0)(5) - (1)(3) = 0 - 3 = -3\n\\]\n\nThus, the determinant is \\(\\boxed{-3}\\).'
'To solve the problem, we need to compute the ratio of the sum of cosines to the sum of sines for angles from \\(1^\\circ\\) to \\(44^\\circ\\):\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\cos n^\\circ}{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\sin n^\\circ}.\n\\]\n\nWe can use the identity for the sum of cosines and sines of an arithmetic sequence. The sum of cosines of angles in an arithmetic sequence is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{N} \\cos n\\theta = \\frac{\\sin\\left(\\frac{N\\theta}{2}\\right) \\cos\\left(\\frac{(N+1)\\theta}{2}\\right)}{\\sin\\left(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\right)}.\n\\]\n\nSimilarly, the sum of sines of angles in an arithmetic sequence is:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{N} \\sin n\\theta = \\frac{\\sin\\left(\\frac{N\\theta}{2}\\right) \\sin\\left(\\frac{(N+1)\\theta}{2}\\right)}{\\sin\\left(\\frac{\\theta}{2}\\right)}.\n\\]\n\nIn our case, \\(\\theta = 1^\\circ\\) and \\(N = 44\\). Therefore, we have:\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\cos n^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin(22^\\circ) \\cos(22.5^\\circ)}{\\sin(0.5^\\circ)}\n\\]\n\nand\n\n\\[\n\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\sin n^\\circ = \\frac{\\sin(22^\\circ) \\sin(22.5^\\circ)}{\\sin(0.5^\\circ)}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the ratio becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\cos n^\\circ}{\\sum_{n=1}^{44} \\sin n^\\circ} = \\frac{\\cos(22.5^\\circ)}{\\sin(22.5^\\circ)} = \\cot(22.5^\\circ).\n\\]\n\nThe value of \\(\\cot(22.5^\\circ)\\) can be found using the half-angle identity:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(22.5^\\circ) = \\sqrt{\\frac{1 - \\cos(45^\\circ)}{1 + \\cos(45^\\circ)}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{1 - \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}{1 + \\frac{\\sqrt{2}}{2}}}.\n\\]\n\nSimplifying this expression:\n\n\\[\n\\tan(22.5^\\circ) = \\sqrt{\\frac{2 - \\sqrt{2}}{2 + \\sqrt{2}}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{(2 - \\sqrt{2})(2 - \\sqrt{2})}{(2 + \\sqrt{2})(2 - \\sqrt{2})}} = \\sqrt{\\frac{6 - 4\\sqrt{2}}{2}} = \\sqrt{2} - 1.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, \\(\\cot(22.5^\\circ) = \\frac{1}{\\tan(22.5^\\circ)} = \\frac{1}{\\sqrt{2} - 1}\\).\n\nRationalizing the denominator:\n\n\\[\n\\cot(22.5^\\circ) = \\frac{\\sqrt{2} + 1}{1} = \\sqrt{2} + 1.\n\\]\n\nThus, the answer is:\n\n\\[\n\\boxed{\\sqrt{2} + 1}.\n\\]'
"To solve this problem, we need to find the line of reflection that takes the point \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\). The line of reflection is the perpendicular bisector of the segment joining these two points.\n\nFirst, let's find the midpoint of the segment joining \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 5 \\\\ 0 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 4 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\):\n\\[\n\\text{Midpoint} = \\left( \\frac{5 + 4}{2}, \\frac{0 + 3}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{9}{2}, \\frac{3}{2} \\right).\n\\]\n\nNext, we find the slope of the line segment joining these two points:\n\\[\n\\text{Slope of segment} = \\frac{3 - 0}{4 - 5} = -3.\n\\]\n\nThe slope of the perpendicular bisector (line of reflection) is the negative reciprocal of \\(-3\\), which is \\(\\frac{1}{3}\\).\n\nNow, we can write the equation of the line of reflection using the point-slope form of a line equation:\n\\[\ny - \\frac{3}{2} = \\frac{1}{3} \\left( x - \\frac{9}{2} \\right).\n\\]\n\nSimplifying, we get:\n\\[\ny = \\frac{1}{3}x - \\frac{3}{2} + \\frac{3}{2} = \\frac{1}{3}x.\n\\]\n\nThis is the equation of the line of reflection: \\(y = \\frac{1}{3}x\\).\n\nNow, we need to find the reflection of the point \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over this line. To do this, we use the formula for reflecting a point \\((x_1, y_1)\\) over a line \\(ax + by + c = 0\\):\n\\[\n(x', y') = \\left( \\frac{x_1(b^2 - a^2) - 2y_1ab - 2ac}{a^2 + b^2}, \\frac{y_1(a^2 - b^2) - 2x_1ab - 2bc}{a^2 + b^2} \\right).\n\\]\n\nFor the line \\(y = \\frac{1}{3}x\\), we rewrite it as:\n\\[\n\\frac{1}{3}x - y = 0 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad x - 3y = 0.\n\\]\n\nHere, \\(a = 1\\), \\(b = -3\\), and \\(c = 0\\).\n\nSubstituting \\((x_1, y_1) = (-2, 3)\\) into the reflection formula, we get:\n\\[\nx' = \\frac{-2((-3)^2 - 1^2) - 2(3)(1)(-3) - 2(1)(0)}{1^2 + (-3)^2} = \\frac{-2(9 - 1) + 18}{10} = \\frac{-16 + 18}{10} = \\frac{2}{10} = \\frac{1}{5},\n\\]\n\\[\ny' = \\frac{3(1^2 - (-3)^2) - 2(-2)(1)(-3) - 2(-3)(0)}{1^2 + (-3)^2} = \\frac{3(1 - 9) + 12}{10} = \\frac{-24 + 12}{10} = \\frac{-12}{10} = -\\frac{6}{5}.\n\\]\n\nThus, the reflection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over the line \\(y = \\frac{1}{3}x\\) is \\(\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{1}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{6}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\\)."
'To find the side length \\( s \\) of the cube, we need to determine the coordinates of the point \\( P = (x, y, z) \\) inside the cube. We are given the distances from \\( P \\) to four vertices of the cube:\n\n1. \\( PA = \\sqrt{70} \\)\n2. \\( PB = \\sqrt{97} \\)\n3. \\( PC = \\sqrt{88} \\)\n4. \\( PE = \\sqrt{43} \\)\n\nThe coordinates of the vertices are:\n- \\( A = (0, 0, 0) \\)\n- \\( B = (s, 0, 0) \\)\n- \\( C = (s, s, 0) \\)\n- \\( E = (0, 0, s) \\)\n\nUsing the distance formula, we can set up the following equations:\n\n1. From \\( PA = \\sqrt{70} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{70} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad x^2 + y^2 + z^2 = 70\n   \\]\n\n2. From \\( PB = \\sqrt{97} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{(x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{97} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad (x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2 = 97\n   \\]\n\n3. From \\( PC = \\sqrt{88} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{(x-s)^2 + (y-s)^2 + z^2} = \\sqrt{88} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad (x-s)^2 + (y-s)^2 + z^2 = 88\n   \\]\n\n4. From \\( PE = \\sqrt{43} \\):\n   \\[\n   \\sqrt{x^2 + y^2 + (z-s)^2} = \\sqrt{43} \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad x^2 + y^2 + (z-s)^2 = 43\n   \\]\n\nNow, we solve these equations step by step:\n\n**Step 1:** Subtract equation 1 from equation 2:\n\\[\n(x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2 - (x^2 + y^2 + z^2) = 97 - 70\n\\]\n\\[\nx^2 - 2sx + s^2 + y^2 + z^2 - x^2 - y^2 - z^2 = 27\n\\]\n\\[\n-2sx + s^2 = 27 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad 2sx = s^2 - 27\n\\]\n\\[\nx = \\frac{s^2 - 27}{2s}\n\\]\n\n**Step 2:** Subtract equation 2 from equation 3:\n\\[\n(x-s)^2 + (y-s)^2 + z^2 - ((x-s)^2 + y^2 + z^2) = 88 - 97\n\\]\n\\[\n(x-s)^2 + y^2 - 2sy + s^2 + z^2 - (x-s)^2 - y^2 - z^2 = -9\n\\]\n\\[\n-2sy + s^2 = -9 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad 2sy = s^2 + 9\n\\]\n\\[\ny = \\frac{s^2 + 9}{2s}\n\\]\n\n**Step 3:** Subtract equation 1 from equation 4:\n\\[\nx^2 + y^2 + (z-s)^2 - (x^2 + y^2 + z^2) = 43 - 70\n\\]\n\\[\nx^2 + y^2 + z^2 - 2sz + s^2 - x^2 - y^2 - z^2 = -27\n\\]\n\\[\n-2sz + s^2 = -27 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad 2sz = s^2 + 27\n\\]\n\\[\nz = \\frac{s^2 + 27}{2s}\n\\]\n\n**Step 4:** Substitute \\( x, y, z \\) into equation 1:\n\\[\n\\left(\\frac{s^2 - 27}{2s}\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{s^2 + 9}{2s}\\right)^2 + \\left(\\frac{s^2 + 27}{2s}\\right)^2 = 70\n\\]\n\nSimplifying each term:\n\\[\n\\frac{(s^2 - 27)^2}{4s^2} + \\frac{(s^2 + 9)^2}{4s^2} + \\frac{(s^2 + 27)^2}{4s^2} = 70\n\\]\n\nCombine and simplify:\n\\[\n\\frac{(s^2 - 27)^2 + (s^2 + 9)^2 + (s^2 + 27)^2}{4s^2} = 70\n\\]\n\nMultiply through by \\( 4s^2 \\):\n\\[\n(s^2 - 27)^2 + (s^2 + 9)^2 + (s^2 + 27)^2 = 280s^2\n\\]\n\nExpanding each square:\n\\[\n(s^4 - 54s^2 + 729) + (s^4 + 18s^2 + 81) + (s^4 + 54s^2 + 729) = 280s^2\n\\]\n\nCombine like terms:\n\\[\n3s^4 + 18s^2 + 1539 = 280s^2\n\\]\n\nRearrange:\n\\[\n3s^4 - 262s^2 + 1539 = 0\n\\]\n\nLet \\( u = s^2 \\), then:\n\\[\n3u^2 - 262u + 1539 = 0\n\\]\n\nSolve this quadratic equation using the quadratic formula:\n\\[\nu = \\frac{-b \\pm \\sqrt{b^2 - 4ac}}{2a} = \\frac{262 \\pm \\sqrt{262^2 - 4 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 1539}}{6}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the discriminant:\n\\[\n262^2 - 4 \\cdot 3 \\cdot 1539 = 68644 - 18468 = 50176\n\\]\n\n\\[\nu = \\frac{262 \\pm \\sqrt{50176}}{6} = \\frac{262 \\pm 224}{6}\n\\]\n\nCalculate the roots:\n\\[\nu_1 = \\frac{262 + 224}{6} = \\frac{486}{6} = 81\n\\]\n\\[\nu_2 = \\frac{262 - 224}{6} = \\frac{38}{6} = \\frac{19}{3}\n\\]\n\nSince \\( u = s^2 \\), we have:\n\\[\ns^2 = 81 \\quad \\Rightarrow \\quad s = 9\n\\]\n\nThus, the side length of the cube is \\( \\boxed{9} \\).'
'To find the plane in which the line lies, we need to determine the values of \\(c\\) and \\(d\\) such that every point on the line satisfies the equation \\(x + y + cz = d\\).\n\nThe line is parameterized by:\n\\[\n\\mathbf{v} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 + t \\\\ 3t \\\\ 1 - t \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\nThis gives us the coordinates:\n- \\(x = 1 + t\\)\n- \\(y = 3t\\)\n- \\(z = 1 - t\\)\n\nSubstitute these into the plane equation:\n\\[\nx + y + cz = d\n\\]\n\\[\n(1 + t) + (3t) + c(1 - t) = d\n\\]\nSimplify the equation:\n\\[\n1 + t + 3t + c - ct = d\n\\]\n\\[\n1 + 4t + c - ct = d\n\\]\nRearrange terms:\n\\[\n1 + c + (4 - c)t = d\n\\]\n\nFor this equation to hold for all \\(t\\), the coefficient of \\(t\\) must be zero, and the constant terms must equal \\(d\\). Therefore, we have:\n1. \\(4 - c = 0\\)\n2. \\(1 + c = d\\)\n\nFrom the first equation, solve for \\(c\\):\n\\[\n4 - c = 0 \\implies c = 4\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(c = 4\\) into the second equation to find \\(d\\):\n\\[\n1 + 4 = d \\implies d = 5\n\\]\n\nThus, the ordered pair \\((c, d)\\) is \\(\\boxed{(4, 5)}\\).'
'To solve this problem, we will use the concept of mass points and the properties of centroids and midpoints in a triangle.\n\nFirst, note that \\(D\\), \\(E\\), and \\(F\\) are the midpoints of sides \\(BC\\), \\(AC\\), and \\(AB\\), respectively. Therefore, \\(AD\\), \\(BE\\), and \\(CF\\) are medians of triangle \\(ABC\\).\n\nThe points \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are the midpoints of segments \\(AD\\), \\(BE\\), and \\(CF\\), respectively. This means that \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are the centroids of the smaller triangles \\(ABD\\), \\(BCE\\), and \\(CAF\\).\n\nIn any triangle, the centroid divides each median into a ratio of \\(2:1\\). Therefore, the centroid \\(G\\) of triangle \\(ABC\\) divides each median into two segments, with the segment connecting the vertex to the centroid being twice as long as the segment connecting the centroid to the midpoint of the opposite side.\n\nSince \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are the centroids of the smaller triangles, they are also the centroids of the entire triangle \\(ABC\\). Therefore, \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) coincide with the centroid \\(G\\) of triangle \\(ABC\\).\n\nNow, we need to compute the expression:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{AQ^2 + AR^2 + BP^2 + BR^2 + CP^2 + CQ^2}{AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2}.\n\\]\n\nSince \\(P\\), \\(Q\\), and \\(R\\) are all the same point \\(G\\), the centroid of triangle \\(ABC\\), we have:\n\n- \\(AQ = AR = AG\\),\n- \\(BP = BR = BG\\),\n- \\(CP = CQ = CG\\).\n\nThe sum of the squares of the distances from the vertices to the centroid \\(G\\) of a triangle is given by the formula:\n\n\\[\nAG^2 + BG^2 + CG^2 = \\frac{1}{3}(AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2).\n\\]\n\nThus, the expression becomes:\n\n\\[\n\\frac{2(AG^2 + BG^2 + CG^2)}{AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2} = \\frac{2 \\cdot \\frac{1}{3}(AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2)}{AB^2 + AC^2 + BC^2} = \\frac{2}{3}.\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of the expression is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2}{3}}\\).'
"To solve this problem, we need to use properties of determinants. We are given that:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ g & h & i \\end{vmatrix} = -7.\n\\]\n\nWe need to find the determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe can use the linearity property of determinants, which states that if a row (or column) of a determinant is a linear combination of other rows (or columns), the determinant can be expressed as the sum of determinants with each term having one of the components of the linear combination.\n\nFirst, consider the second row of the determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ 2d + 5g & 2e + 5h & 2f + 5i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nWe can split the second row into two separate determinants:\n\n\\[\n= \\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ 2d & 2e & 2f \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix} + \\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ 5g & 5h & 5i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nFor the first determinant, we can factor out a 2 from the second row:\n\n\\[\n= 2 \\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nFor the second determinant, we can factor out a 5 from the second row:\n\n\\[\n= 5 \\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ g & h & i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix}.\n\\]\n\nNow, let's evaluate each of these determinants. For the first determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix} = -\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ d & e & f \\\\ g & h & i \\end{vmatrix} = -(-7) = 7.\n\\]\n\nThis is because multiplying a row by a scalar (in this case, -1) changes the sign of the determinant.\n\nFor the second determinant:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{vmatrix} a & b & c \\\\ g & h & i \\\\ -g & -h & -i \\end{vmatrix} = 0.\n\\]\n\nThis is because the second and third rows are linearly dependent (the third row is -1 times the second row), which makes the determinant zero.\n\nPutting it all together:\n\n\\[\n2 \\cdot 7 + 5 \\cdot 0 = 14 + 0 = 14.\n\\]\n\nThus, the value of the determinant is \\(\\boxed{14}\\)."
'To solve this problem, we need to use the fact that the angle bisectors of a triangle intersect at the incenter, and the given condition that \\(\\angle EDF = 90^\\circ\\) implies a specific relationship between the angles of the triangle.\n\nThe key insight is that if \\(\\angle EDF = 90^\\circ\\), then triangle \\(DEF\\) is a right triangle. This occurs when the incenter \\(I\\) of triangle \\(ABC\\) is the orthocenter of triangle \\(DEF\\). This is a special property that occurs when triangle \\(ABC\\) is a right triangle.\n\nIn a right triangle, the incenter lies on the hypotenuse, and the angle bisectors form a right triangle at the incenter. Therefore, \\(\\angle EDF = 90^\\circ\\) implies that triangle \\(ABC\\) is a right triangle.\n\nSince \\(\\angle EDF = 90^\\circ\\), and knowing that the incenter is the orthocenter of triangle \\(DEF\\), we can conclude that \\(\\angle BAC\\) must be \\(90^\\circ\\).\n\nThus, the only possible value for \\(\\angle BAC\\) is \\(\\boxed{90}\\) degrees.'
'To find the area of triangle \\( OAB \\), we can use the cross product of the vectors \\(\\overrightarrow{OA}\\) and \\(\\overrightarrow{OB}\\). The area of the triangle is half the magnitude of the cross product of these two vectors.\n\nGiven:\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{OA} = \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ 2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}, \\quad \\overrightarrow{OB} = \\begin{pmatrix} -3 \\\\ -2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe cross product \\(\\overrightarrow{OA} \\times \\overrightarrow{OB}\\) is calculated as follows:\n\\[\n\\overrightarrow{OA} \\times \\overrightarrow{OB} = \\begin{vmatrix} \\mathbf{i} & \\mathbf{j} & \\mathbf{k} \\\\ 1 & 2 & 3 \\\\ -3 & -2 & 1 \\end{vmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThis determinant expands to:\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i} \\left( 2 \\cdot 1 - 3 \\cdot (-2) \\right) - \\mathbf{j} \\left( 1 \\cdot 1 - 3 \\cdot (-3) \\right) + \\mathbf{k} \\left( 1 \\cdot (-2) - 2 \\cdot (-3) \\right)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i} (2 + 6) - \\mathbf{j} (1 + 9) + \\mathbf{k} (-2 + 6)\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\mathbf{i} \\cdot 8 - \\mathbf{j} \\cdot 10 + \\mathbf{k} \\cdot 4\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ -10 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe magnitude of this vector is:\n\\[\n\\left\\| \\begin{pmatrix} 8 \\\\ -10 \\\\ 4 \\end{pmatrix} \\right\\| = \\sqrt{8^2 + (-10)^2 + 4^2}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{64 + 100 + 16}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{180}\n\\]\n\n\\[\n= \\sqrt{36 \\times 5} = 6\\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nThe area of triangle \\( OAB \\) is half the magnitude of the cross product:\n\\[\n\\text{Area} = \\frac{1}{2} \\times 6\\sqrt{5} = 3\\sqrt{5}\n\\]\n\nThus, the area of triangle \\( OAB \\) is \\(\\boxed{3\\sqrt{5}}\\).'
"To find the image of the line \\( y = 2x + 1 \\) under the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\), we first need to determine the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) itself. We know that \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) maps the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 9 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) to \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nLet's denote the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) as:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nWe have the following system of equations from the given transformations:\n\n1. \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 9 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\n   This gives us the equations:\n   \\[\n   2a - b = 9\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   2c - d = 3\n   \\]\n\n2. \\(\\begin{pmatrix} a & b \\\\ c & d \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} 1 \\\\ -3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 7 \\\\ -1 \\end{pmatrix}\\)\n\n   This gives us the equations:\n   \\[\n   a - 3b = 7\n   \\]\n   \\[\n   c - 3d = -1\n   \\]\n\nWe solve these systems of equations to find \\(a\\), \\(b\\), \\(c\\), and \\(d\\).\n\nFrom the first pair of equations:\n- \\(2a - b = 9\\)\n- \\(a - 3b = 7\\)\n\nSolving for \\(a\\) and \\(b\\), we multiply the second equation by 2:\n\n\\[\n2a - 6b = 14\n\\]\n\nSubtract the first equation from this:\n\n\\[\n(2a - 6b) - (2a - b) = 14 - 9\n\\]\n\\[\n-5b = 5 \\implies b = -1\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(b = -1\\) into \\(a - 3b = 7\\):\n\n\\[\na + 3 = 7 \\implies a = 4\n\\]\n\nFrom the second pair of equations:\n- \\(2c - d = 3\\)\n- \\(c - 3d = -1\\)\n\nSolving for \\(c\\) and \\(d\\), we multiply the second equation by 2:\n\n\\[\n2c - 6d = -2\n\\]\n\nSubtract the first equation from this:\n\n\\[\n(2c - 6d) - (2c - d) = -2 - 3\n\\]\n\\[\n-5d = -5 \\implies d = 1\n\\]\n\nSubstitute \\(d = 1\\) into \\(c - 3d = -1\\):\n\n\\[\nc - 3 = -1 \\implies c = 2\n\\]\n\nThus, the matrix \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{M} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4 & -1 \\\\ 2 & 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, to find the image of the line \\(y = 2x + 1\\), we need to transform points on this line using \\(\\mathbf{M}\\). A point on the line can be represented as \\(\\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ 2x + 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe image of this point under \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\n\\[\n\\begin{pmatrix} 4 & -1 \\\\ 2 & 1 \\end{pmatrix} \\begin{pmatrix} x \\\\ 2x + 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 4x - (2x + 1) \\\\ 2x + (2x + 1) \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2x - 1 \\\\ 4x + 1 \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe transformed line is given by the relationship between the new coordinates. The new \\(y\\)-coordinate is \\(4x + 1\\) and the new \\(x\\)-coordinate is \\(2x - 1\\). Solving for \\(y\\) in terms of \\(x\\):\n\nLet \\(X = 2x - 1\\) and \\(Y = 4x + 1\\).\n\nFrom \\(X = 2x - 1\\), solve for \\(x\\):\n\n\\[\n2x = X + 1 \\implies x = \\frac{X + 1}{2}\n\\]\n\nSubstitute into \\(Y = 4x + 1\\):\n\n\\[\nY = 4\\left(\\frac{X + 1}{2}\\right) + 1 = 2(X + 1) + 1 = 2X + 2 + 1 = 2X + 3\n\\]\n\nThus, the image of the line \\(y = 2x + 1\\) under \\(\\mathbf{M}\\) is:\n\n\\[\ny = 2x + 3\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the image of the line is \\(y = 2x + 3\\)."
"To solve this problem, we need to track the transformations applied to the triangle and determine which transformation will return the final image back to the original triangle.\n\n1. **Initial Triangle \\(ABC\\):**\n   - \\(A(0, 2)\\), \\(B(-3, 2)\\), \\(C(-3, 0)\\).\n\n2. **Reflection about the \\(x\\)-axis:**\n   - Reflecting a point \\((x, y)\\) about the \\(x\\)-axis results in \\((x, -y)\\).\n   - \\(A'(0, -2)\\), \\(B'(-3, -2)\\), \\(C'(-3, 0)\\).\n\n3. **Rotation counterclockwise by \\(90^\\circ\\):**\n   - Rotating a point \\((x, y)\\) counterclockwise by \\(90^\\circ\\) about the origin results in \\((-y, x)\\).\n   - \\(A''(2, 0)\\), \\(B''(2, -3)\\), \\(C''(0, -3)\\).\n\nNow, we need to determine which transformation will return triangle \\(A''B''C''\\) to triangle \\(ABC\\).\n\n- **Option (A): Counterclockwise rotation about the origin by \\(90^\\circ\\):**\n  - Applying this to \\(A''(2, 0)\\), \\(B''(2, -3)\\), \\(C''(0, -3)\\):\n    - \\(A'''(0, 2)\\), \\(B'''(3, 2)\\), \\(C'''(3, 0)\\).\n  - This does not match \\(ABC\\).\n\n- **Option (B): Clockwise rotation about the origin by \\(90^\\circ\\):**\n  - Applying this to \\(A''(2, 0)\\), \\(B''(2, -3)\\), \\(C''(0, -3)\\):\n    - \\(A'''(0, -2)\\), \\(B'''(-3, -2)\\), \\(C'''(-3, 0)\\).\n  - This matches the reflection of \\(ABC\\) about the \\(x\\)-axis, not \\(ABC\\) itself.\n\n- **Option (C): Reflection about the \\(x\\)-axis:**\n  - Reflecting \\(A''(2, 0)\\), \\(B''(2, -3)\\), \\(C''(0, -3)\\) about the \\(x\\)-axis:\n    - \\(A'''(2, 0)\\), \\(B'''(2, 3)\\), \\(C'''(0, 3)\\).\n  - This does not match \\(ABC\\).\n\n- **Option (D): Reflection about the line \\(y = x\\):**\n  - Reflecting \\(A''(2, 0)\\), \\(B''(2, -3)\\), \\(C''(0, -3)\\) about the line \\(y = x\\):\n    - \\(A'''(0, 2)\\), \\(B'''(-3, 2)\\), \\(C'''(-3, 0)\\).\n  - This matches \\(ABC\\).\n\n- **Option (E): Reflection about the \\(y\\)-axis:**\n  - Reflecting \\(A''(2, 0)\\), \\(B''(2, -3)\\), \\(C''(0, -3)\\) about the \\(y\\)-axis:\n    - \\(A'''(-2, 0)\\), \\(B'''(-2, -3)\\), \\(C'''(0, -3)\\).\n  - This does not match \\(ABC\\).\n\nThe correct transformation that returns triangle \\(A''B''C''\\) to triangle \\(ABC\\) is **(D) reflection about the line \\(y = x\\)**."
'To compute \\(\\arccos \\left( -\\frac{1}{2} \\right)\\), we need to find the angle \\(\\theta\\) in the range \\(0 \\leq \\theta < \\pi\\) such that \\(\\cos \\theta = -\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n\nThe cosine of \\(-\\frac{1}{2}\\) corresponds to an angle of \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\) radians. This is because:\n\n- The cosine of \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\) is \\(-\\frac{1}{2}\\).\n- \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\) is within the range \\([0, \\pi]\\).\n\nTherefore, \\(\\arccos \\left( -\\frac{1}{2} \\right) = \\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).\n\nThe answer is \\(\\frac{2\\pi}{3}\\).'
"To find the product \\( c_1 c_2 \\) where \\( c_1 \\) and \\( c_2 \\) are the complex numbers such that \\(-5 + 3i\\), \\(8 - i\\), and \\(c\\) form the vertices of an equilateral triangle, we can use the properties of complex numbers and rotations.\n\nGiven two points \\( z_1 = -5 + 3i \\) and \\( z_2 = 8 - i \\), the third point \\( c \\) can be found by rotating one of these points around the other by \\( \\pm 60^\\circ \\).\n\nThe rotation of a complex number \\( z \\) around another complex number \\( w \\) by an angle \\( \\theta \\) is given by:\n\\[\nz' = w + (z - w) \\cdot e^{i\\theta}\n\\]\n\nFor a \\( 60^\\circ \\) rotation, \\( e^{i\\theta} = \\cos 60^\\circ + i \\sin 60^\\circ = \\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\).\n\nLet's rotate \\( z_2 \\) around \\( z_1 \\) by \\( 60^\\circ \\):\n\\[\nc_1 = z_1 + (z_2 - z_1) \\cdot \\left( \\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\( z_2 - z_1 \\):\n\\[\nz_2 - z_1 = (8 - i) - (-5 + 3i) = 13 - 4i\n\\]\n\nNow, compute the rotation:\n\\[\nc_1 = (-5 + 3i) + (13 - 4i) \\cdot \\left( \\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate the product:\n\\[\n(13 - 4i) \\cdot \\left( \\frac{1}{2} + i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right) = \\left( \\frac{13}{2} - 2i \\right) + i \\left( \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2\\sqrt{3}i \\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\\[\n= \\frac{13}{2} - 2i + i \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2\\sqrt{3}(-1)\n\\]\n\\[\n= \\frac{13}{2} + 2\\sqrt{3} + i \\left( \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2 \\right)\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\nc_1 = -5 + 3i + \\left( \\frac{13}{2} + 2\\sqrt{3} + i \\left( \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2 \\right) \\right)\n\\]\n\nNow, rotate \\( z_1 \\) around \\( z_2 \\) by \\( -60^\\circ \\) (or equivalently \\( 300^\\circ \\)):\n\\[\nc_2 = z_2 + (z_1 - z_2) \\cdot \\left( \\frac{1}{2} - i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate \\( z_1 - z_2 \\):\n\\[\nz_1 - z_2 = (-5 + 3i) - (8 - i) = -13 + 4i\n\\]\n\nNow, compute the rotation:\n\\[\nc_2 = (8 - i) + (-13 + 4i) \\cdot \\left( \\frac{1}{2} - i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nCalculate the product:\n\\[\n(-13 + 4i) \\cdot \\left( \\frac{1}{2} - i \\frac{\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right) = \\left( -\\frac{13}{2} + 2i \\right) - i \\left( \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} + 2\\sqrt{3}i \\right)\n\\]\n\nSimplify:\n\\[\n= -\\frac{13}{2} + 2i - i \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} - 2\\sqrt{3}(-1)\n\\]\n\\[\n= -\\frac{13}{2} + 2\\sqrt{3} + i \\left( 2 - \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right)\n\\]\n\nThus:\n\\[\nc_2 = 8 - i + \\left( -\\frac{13}{2} + 2\\sqrt{3} + i \\left( 2 - \\frac{13\\sqrt{3}}{2} \\right) \\right)\n\\]\n\nFinally, the product \\( c_1 c_2 \\) is calculated by multiplying these two complex numbers. However, due to symmetry and the properties of equilateral triangles, the product of the two possible third vertices is equal to the product of the original two vertices:\n\\[\nc_1 c_2 = (-5 + 3i)(8 - i)\n\\]\n\nCalculate:\n\\[\n= (-5)(8) + (-5)(-i) + (3i)(8) + (3i)(-i)\n\\]\n\\[\n= -40 + 5i + 24i - 3\n\\]\n\\[\n= -43 + 29i\n\\]\n\nThus, the product \\( c_1 c_2 \\) is \\(\\boxed{-43 + 29i}\\)."
'To find the reflection of the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over the vector \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\), we first need to find the projection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) onto \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nThe projection formula is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{a} = \\frac{\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}}{\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b}} \\mathbf{b}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\mathbf{a} = \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) and \\(\\mathbf{b} = \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nFirst, calculate the dot products:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{a} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = (-2)(2) + (3)(1) = -4 + 3 = -1\n\\]\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{b} \\cdot \\mathbf{b} = (2)(2) + (1)(1) = 4 + 1 = 5\n\\]\n\nNow, find the projection:\n\n\\[\n\\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\frac{-1}{5} \\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{2}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{1}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThe reflection \\(\\mathbf{r}\\) of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is given by:\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{r} = 2 \\mathbf{p} - \\mathbf{a}\n\\]\n\nwhere \\(\\mathbf{p} = \\operatorname{proj}_{\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}} \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{2}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{1}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\\).\n\nCalculate \\(2 \\mathbf{p}\\):\n\n\\[\n2 \\mathbf{p} = 2 \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{2}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{1}{5} \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nNow, find \\(\\mathbf{r}\\):\n\n\\[\n\\mathbf{r} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} \\end{pmatrix} - \\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} -\\frac{4}{5} + 2 \\\\ -\\frac{2}{5} - 3 \\end{pmatrix} = \\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{6}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{17}{5} \\end{pmatrix}\n\\]\n\nThus, the reflection of \\(\\begin{pmatrix} -2 \\\\ 3 \\end{pmatrix}\\) over \\(\\begin{pmatrix} 2 \\\\ 1 \\end{pmatrix}\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\begin{pmatrix} \\frac{6}{5} \\\\ -\\frac{17}{5} \\end{pmatrix}}\\).'
'In triangle \\(LMN\\), we have \\(\\angle M = 90^\\circ\\), \\(MN = 2\\), and \\(LM = \\sqrt{21}\\). We need to find \\(\\sin L\\).\n\nSince \\(\\angle M\\) is a right angle, triangle \\(LMN\\) is a right triangle with \\(LM\\) as the hypotenuse. We can use the Pythagorean theorem to find the length of the third side, \\(LN\\).\n\nThe Pythagorean theorem states:\n\\[\nLM^2 = MN^2 + LN^2\n\\]\n\nSubstituting the known values:\n\\[\n(\\sqrt{21})^2 = 2^2 + LN^2\n\\]\n\nThis simplifies to:\n\\[\n21 = 4 + LN^2\n\\]\n\nSubtract 4 from both sides:\n\\[\n17 = LN^2\n\\]\n\nTaking the square root of both sides gives:\n\\[\nLN = \\sqrt{17}\n\\]\n\nNow, we can find \\(\\sin L\\). In a right triangle, the sine of an angle is the ratio of the length of the opposite side to the hypotenuse. For \\(\\angle L\\), the opposite side is \\(MN\\) and the hypotenuse is \\(LM\\).\n\nThus, \\(\\sin L\\) is:\n\\[\n\\sin L = \\frac{MN}{LM} = \\frac{2}{\\sqrt{21}}\n\\]\n\nTo rationalize the denominator, multiply the numerator and the denominator by \\(\\sqrt{21}\\):\n\\[\n\\sin L = \\frac{2 \\cdot \\sqrt{21}}{21} = \\frac{2\\sqrt{21}}{21}\n\\]\n\nTherefore, the value of \\(\\sin L\\) is \\(\\boxed{\\frac{2\\sqrt{21}}{21}}\\).'
